Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Generality of 'Man' and 'Ma' and the Partiality vs Explanation of 'Min'
Commentary on words of generality with examples and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general words 'man' and 'ma' in Hanafi usul, and apply the rule that 'min' indicates partiality unless context shifts it to explanation.

Core Idea
Words 'man' and 'ma' are inherently general, but 'min' defaults to partiality; context can elevate it to explanation.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the scope of general words is foundational for deriving rulings from texts. This lesson focuses on two key words of generality: 'man' (for rational beings) and 'ma' (for non-rational). It also delves into the dual function of the preposition 'min', which can indicate partiality (tab'id) or explanation (bayan). The correct identification of these nuances determines the legal outcome in cases like the master's permission to free slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general words 'man' and 'ma'
Recognize that 'man' is originally for rational beings and is general unless specified otherwise, while 'ma' is for non-rational beings. These are tools for understanding commands and conditions.
The hadith 'man qatala…' uses 'man' to cover any killer who meets the condition.
2
Understand the two senses of 'min'
Know that 'min' can mean 'some of' (partiality) or 'from the category of' (explanation). The default is partiality when the genitive noun has parts.
Example: 'min 'ibadi' with 'ibad' having multiple individuals.
3
Apply the default rule to the first example
In the first example 'man shaa min 'ibadi', the master attributes wishing to the slaves. 'Man' is general and 'min' is explanatory here because the wish is a general attribute, confirming the generality of 'man'.
Thus, if all wish, all are freed.
4
Apply the rule to the second example and note the ikhtilaf
In 'man shi'ta min 'ibadi', the wish is attributed to the third party. Here Abu Hanifa says 'min' remains partiality – only one can be freed. The Sahibayn say 'min' is explanatory, so all can be freed.
The difference hinges on whether a context (qarina) shifts 'min' from tab'id to bayan.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the third party frees all at once, Abu Hanifa says they are all freed except one (master chooses), while the Sahibayn say all are freed.
The Hanafi madhhab follows Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.

بيان ألفاظ العموم
لذوات من يعقل كقوله : "من قتل قتيلاً فله سلبه"، وقد يستعمل في غير من يعقل مجازا كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَمْشِي عَلَى بَطْنِهِ والأصل في ما أن يكون في الدواب في الأرض (النور: ٤٥) ذوات ما لا يعقل يقال: "ما في الدار؟ فالجواب درهم أو دينار لا زيد أو عمرو، وقد
يستعمل في غيرها كما سيأتي.
في المتن
فإذا قال: من شاء من عبيدي العتق فهو حر فشاؤوا عتقوا، تفريع لكون كلمة "من" عامة؛ وذلك لأن معناه كل من شاء العتق من بين عبيدي فهو حر، وكلمة "من" في نفسها عامة، ووصفت بصفة عامة، وهي المشيئة، ومن يحتمل البيان، فإن شاء الكل أي الجارة لابد أن يعتقوا جميعًا عملاً بعموم كلمة "مَنْ"، بخلاف "ما" إذا قال: "من شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه" بإسناد المشيئة إلى المخاطب، فإن له حينئذ أن يعتقهم
كقوله عليه من قتل إلخ روى البخاري عن أبي قتادة الله قال : قال رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: "من قتل قتيلاً له عليه بينة، فله سلبه"، أي من أوقع القتل على المقتول باعتبار ماله كقوله: أعصر خمرا (يوسف:٣٦) كذا في إرشاد الساري في "شرح صحيح البخاري"، والسلب هو ما يأخذه أحد القرنين في الحرب من سلاح وغيرها. (القمر) أن يكون إلخ : هذا على مذهب البعض، وأما الأكثرون فقالوا: إن كلمة "ما" تعم لذوي العقول وغيرهم.(القمر) وقد يستعمل أي كلمة "ما" مجازا في غير ذوات ما لا يعقل. (القمر) معناه إلخ : أي معنى قوله: "من شاء" إلخ. (القمر) وهي المشيئة فإنها عامة لأنها أسندت إلى عام (القمر) يحتمل البيان إلخ اعلم أن استعمال كلمة "من" في التبعيض هو الشائع حيث كان مجرورها ذا أبعاض، فيحمل "من" عليه ما لم يوجد قرينة صارفة عنه ترجح كون "من" للبيان، وفي مسألة المتن هذه القرينة موجودة وهي إضافة المشيئة إلى ما هو من ألفاظ العموم، فتأكد له العموم، فحمل كلمة "من" على البيان وترك التبعيض. (القمر)
أخرجه البخاري في "صحيحه" رقم: ۲۹۷۳ ، باب من لم يخمس الأسلاب ومسلم رقم: ١٧٥١، باب استحقاق القاتل سلب القتيل والترمذي رقم: ١٥٦٢ ، باب ما جاء فيمن قتل قتيلا فله سلبه، وأبو داود رقم: ۲۷۱۷، باب في السلب يعطي القاتل، وابن حبان في صحيحه رقم: ۱۸۰۵۸ ، ۱۳۱/۱۱، وأحمد في "مسنده" رقم: ٢٢٦٦٠ ، عن أبي قتادة له حديثاً طويلاً، وفيه: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : " من قتل قتيلاً له عليه بينة، فله سلبه".
مبحث العام
بيان ألفاظ العموم
إلا واحدًا عند أبي حنيفة وله؛ لأن كلمة "من" للعموم، و"من" للتبعيض، فلا يستقيم العمل بهما، إلا إذا بقي واحد منهم غير معتق، وكذا المشيئة صفة خاصة للمخاطب، وقيل:
وهو خاص
كلمة "من" للتبعيض في كل من المثالين لكن في المثال الأول كل من العبد الشائي بعض مع
قطع النظر عن غيره، فيعتق الكل، وفي المثال الثاني الشائي واحد يتعلق مشيئته بالكل دفعة،
وهو المخاطب
فلا يستقيم إلا بتخصيص البعض، ولكن يرد عليه أنه إن شاء الكل على الترتيب، فحينئذ
معنى التبعيض
المورد صاحب "التلويح"
يصدق على كل واحد أنه شاء عتقه حال كونه بعضا من العبد، فتأمل فيه.
فإن قال لأمته: "إن كان ما في بطنك غلامًا فأنت حرة، فولدت غلامًا وجارية لم تعتق، تفريع لكون كلمة "ما" عامة؛ لأن المعنى حينئذ: إن كان جميع ما في بطنك غلامًا فأنت حرة،
و لم يكن كذلك، بل كان بعض ما في بطنها غلامًا، وبعضه جارية، فلم يوجد الشرط،
فلم تعتق
إلا واحدًا وهو الأخير إذا أعتقهم المخاطب على الترتيب وإن أعتقهم جملة عتقوا إلا واحدا، والخيار في تعيينه إلى المولى، فإنه لو أعتق المخاطب جميع العبيد لسقط معنى التبعيض بالكلية، فلا بد من معنى التبعيض بالكلية، فلا بد من أن يبقى واحد منهم. (القمر) عند أبي حنيفة له وأما عندهما فللمخاطب أن يعتقهم عملاً بكلمة العموم و "من" للبيان. (القمر) ومن للتبعيض : الشيوع استعمال "من" للتبعيض إذا كان مجرورها ذا أبعاض وليست ههنا قرينة تؤكد العموم، و توجب كون "من" للبيان . (القمر) وقيل القائل صاحب "التوضيح". (القمر)
.
من المثالين: أي من شاء من عبيدي إلخ، ومن شئت من عبيدي إلخ. (القمر) فحينئذ يصدق على كل واحد أي من العبيد أنه أي أن المخاطب شاء عتقه أي عتق كل واحد من العبيد حال كونه بعضا من العبيد، فينبغي أن يعتق الكل، والأمر ليس كذلك عند الإمام الأعظم (القمر) فتأمل إلخ فيه إيماء إلى أمرين أحدهما ما في "قمر الأقمار"، والثاني: أن يقال: إن ولاية الإعتاق للمخاطب ما حصل له إلا من هذا الكلام، وهذا الكلام محمول على البعضية، فلا يلي إعتاق الكل؛ لأنه خلاف مقصود المولى، فإذا أعتق دفعة فالخيار إلى المولى، وإن أعتق على الترتيب، فالأخير لا يعتق فتدبر. (السنبلي) فتأمل فيه : لعله إشارة إلى جواب الإيراد، وتقريره : أن تعلق المشيئة بالكل على الانفراد والترتيب أمر باطني، والظاهر
من إعتاق الكل أن يتعلق مشيئة المخاطب بالكل دفعة، فلا بد من إخراج البعض ليتحقق التبعيض فتأمل. (القمر)
Topic of the General: Statement of the words of generality. For beings with reason, like his saying: 'Whoever kills a killed person, then his spoils belong to him.' And it may be used metaphorically for other than those with reason, as in the Almighty's saying: 'And among them are those who crawl on their bellies' (An-Nur:45). The origin of 'ma' is that it is for non-rational beings. It is said: 'What is in the house?' The answer is dirham or dinar, not Zayd or Amr. And it may be used for other than them as will come. In the text: So if he says: 'Whoever among my slaves wishes emancipation, he is free,' and they wish, they become free, as a branch of the word 'man' being general. That is because its meaning is: everyone who wishes emancipation from among my slaves is free, and the word 'man' in itself is general, and it is described with a general attribute, namely wishing. And 'man' admits of explanation. So if all wish, they must all be freed, acting upon the generality of the word 'man'. Contrary to 'ma'. If he says: 'Whomever you wish from among my slaves to free, then free him,' attributing the wish to the addressee, then he may free them. Like his saying (peace be upon him) 'whoever kills...' Al-Bukhari narrated from Abu Qatada (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever kills a killed person and has proof of it, then his spoils belong to him.' That is, whoever inflicts killing on the killed person considering his property, like His saying: 'I press wine' (Yusuf:36). Likewise in Irshad al-Sari in Sharh Sahih al-Bukhari. And 'salab' is what one of the two fighters takes in war of weapons and other things. (Al-Qamar) 'That it be...' etc.: This is according to the opinion of some. As for the majority, they said: The word 'ma' is general for beings with reason and others. (Al-Qamar) And it may be used, i.e., the word 'ma', metaphorically for other than non-rational beings. (Al-Qamar) Its meaning etc.: i.e., the meaning of his saying 'whoever wishes' etc. (Al-Qamar) And it is the wish, for it is general because it is attributed to a general [word]. (Al-Qamar) 'Admits of explanation' etc.: Know that the use of the word 'min' for partiality is common when its genitive has parts. So 'min' is understood for it unless there is a context that diverts from it, preponderating that 'min' is for explanation. In the issue of the text, this context exists, which is the addition of wish to that which is among the words of generality. So generality is confirmed for it, and the word 'min' is interpreted as explanation, and partiality is left. (Al-Qamar) Al-Bukhari narrated it in his Sahih no. 2973, chapter on those who did not take khums from spoils; Muslim no. 1751, chapter on the entitlement of the killer to the spoils of the killed; Tirmidhi no. 1562, chapter on what has come about the killer taking the spoils; Abu Dawud no. 2717, chapter on spoils given to the killer; Ibn Hibban in his Sahih no. 18058, 11/131; Ahmad in his Musnad no. 22660, from Abu Qatada, a long hadith, in which the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever kills a killed person and has proof of it, then his spoils belong to him.' Topic of the General: Statement of the words of generality. Except one according to Abu Hanifa and his [companion] because the word 'min' is for generality and 'man' is for partiality, so acting with both is not sound unless one of them remains unemancipated. Similarly, the wish is a specific attribute of the addressee. And it was said: And it is specific. The word 'min' is for partiality in both examples, but in the first example, each slave who wishes is a part regardless of others, so all are freed. In the second example, the one who wishes is one; his wish relates to all at once, and he is the addressee, so it is not sound except by specifying some. But it is objected: if he wishes all in sequence, then at that time the meaning of partiality applies to each one that he wished his emancipation while he is a part of the slaves, so reflect on it. The author of al-Talwih says: It applies to each one that he wished his emancipation while he is a part of the slave, so reflect on it. Then if he says to his slave-girl: 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed, as a branch of the word 'ma' being general. Because the meaning then is: if all that is in your womb is a male, you are free. And it was not so, but rather part of what is in her womb is a male and part is a female, so the condition does not exist, so she is not freed. Except one, which is the last if the addressee frees them in sequence. If he frees them all together, they are all freed except one, and the choice of which one is up to the master. Because if the addressee frees all the slaves, the meaning of partiality is completely lost, so the meaning of partiality must remain. (Al-Qamar) According to Abu Hanifa and his [companion], but according to the two companions, the addressee may free them all, acting upon the word of generality and 'min' for explanation. (Al-Qamar) And 'min' for partiality: it is common to use 'min' for partiality when its genitive has parts, and there is no context here that confirms generality and necessitates that 'min' is for explanation. (Al-Qamar) And it was said: the speaker is the author of al-Tawdih. (Al-Qamar) . From the two examples: i.e., 'whoever among my slaves wishes' etc., and 'whomever you wish among my slaves' etc. (Al-Qamar) Then it applies to each one, i.e., of the slaves, that he (the addressee) wished his emancipation, i.e., the emancipation of each one of the slaves while he is a part of the slaves, so all should be freed, but the matter is not so according to the Great Imam. (Al-Qamar) 'So reflect etc.' contains an indication of two matters: one is what is in Qamar al-Aqmar, and the second: that it is said: The authority of emancipation for the addressee is not obtained except from this speech, and this speech is understood as partiality, so he does not have authority to emancipate all because it contradicts the master's intention. So if he frees at once, the choice is the master's; if he frees in sequence, the last is not freed, so ponder. (al-Sanbali) 'So reflect on it': perhaps it is an indication of an answer to the objection, and its explanation: that the attachment of the wish to all individually and sequentially is an internal matter, and the apparent from emancipating all is that the addressee's wish attaches to all at once, so some must be excluded to achieve partiality, so reflect. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on the chapter of general words in usul al-fiqh. The commentator explains the usage of two key words: 'man' and 'ma'. 'Man' is originally for rational beings and indicates generality. For example, in the hadith 'Whoever kills a killed person, his spoils belong to him,' 'man' includes all potential killers. Similarly, in the ruling: if a master says 'Whoever among my slaves wishes emancipation, he is free,' and all slaves wish, they are all freed because 'man' is general. However, if the master uses 'ma' as in 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to both a male and a female, she does not become free because 'ma' indicates generality over the entire content. The commentator then discusses a dispute: if the master says to a third party 'Whomever you wish among my slaves to free, free him,' Abu Hanifa holds that only one slave can be freed because 'min' in 'min 'ibadi' indicates partiality. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that all can be freed because 'man' is general and 'min' here is for explanation. The commentator analyzes the reasoning, noting that the context (the master's authority) affects the interpretation. The passage emphasizes that the literal generality of words can be modified by context and intended meaning, a key principle in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
مَنْ (man): A general word for rational beings, indicating generality unless specified otherwise.
مَا (ma): A general word for non-rational beings, but can be used metaphorically for others.
التبعيض (al-tab'id): Partiality; indicating a part of a whole, as in the preposition 'min' meaning 'some of'.
البيان (al-bayan): Explanation; the preposition 'min' used to specify or explain the general, not to indicate partiality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says: 'Whoever among my slaves wishes emancipation, he is free.' According to the Hanafi position, if all slaves wish, what is the ruling?
A) Only one slave is freed, because 'min' indicates partiality.
B) All slaves are freed, because 'man' is general and 'min' is explanatory due to the general wish.
C) None are freed until the master specifies which slave.
D) Only the first slave to wish is freed.

Key Takeaway
The default meaning of 'min' with a partitive genitive is partiality; a contextual qarina is required to shift it to explanation, as illustrated by the two examples in the master-slave case.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Words of Generality: 'Man' and 'Ma' in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of 'man' and 'ma' as general/partitive words in legal texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principles of umum and tab'eed to identify the intended meaning of 'man' and 'ma' in legal texts, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
'Man' signals partitivity when its genitive has parts; without a qareenah, it is not automatically general.

This lesson examines two key Arabic particles of generality—'man' and 'ma'—and their interpretation in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Through a disputed case on slave emancipation, we explore how Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ on whether 'man' indicates partitivity (tab'eed) or pure generality. We also analyze how the word 'ma' can be restricted by contextual indicators (qareenah) such as 'tayassara' in the Quran. Finally, the word 'kull' is defined as encompassing individuals individually, not collectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify words of generality
Locate 'man', 'ma', and 'kull' in the text and note their grammatical roles (subject, object, etc.).
Pay attention to whether they are followed by a genitive (e.g., 'min abdī' or 'ma fī batniki').
2
Determine the nature of the genitive
Check whether the noun following 'man' or 'ma' has parts (a‘dād) or is indivisible. For example, 'abdī' has multiple parts (slaves).
If the genitive has no parts, 'man' cannot be partitive.
3
Search for a qareenah
Find any contextual indicator (qareenah) that either confirms generality or forces partitivity. In the Quranic example, 'tayassara' is a qareenah for individuality.
The absence of a qareenah may lean toward partitivity for 'man' (Abu Hanifa).
4
Apply the Hanafi rule
Abu Hanifa: 'man' is partitive when the genitive has parts and no qareenah for generality; Sahibayn: 'man' is explanatory generality (bayan).
This difference leads to different rulings in the emancipation case.
5
Derive the ruling
In the emancipation case, if 'man' is partitive, one slave must remain unfreed; if general, all may be freed. In the Quranic verse, 'ma' is restricted by 'tayassara' to individual ease.
The ruling for 'kull' is that it indicates individual encompassment, not collective.

.
من المثالين: أي من شاء من عبيدي إلخ، ومن شئت من عبيدي إلخ. (القمر) فحينئذ يصدق على كل واحد أي من العبيد أنه أي أن المخاطب شاء عتقه أي عتق كل واحد من العبيد حال كونه بعضا من العبيد، فينبغي أن يعتق الكل، والأمر ليس كذلك عند الإمام الأعظم (القمر) فتأمل إلخ فيه إيماء إلى أمرين أحدهما ما في "قمر الأقمار"، والثاني: أن يقال: إن ولاية الإعتاق للمخاطب ما حصل له إلا من هذا الكلام، وهذا الكلام محمول على البعضية، فلا يلي إعتاق الكل؛ لأنه خلاف مقصود المولى، فإذا أعتق دفعة فالخيار إلى المولى، وإن أعتق على الترتيب، فالأخير لا يعتق فتدبر. (السنبلي) فتأمل فيه : لعله إشارة إلى جواب الإيراد، وتقريره : أن تعلق المشيئة بالكل على الانفراد والترتيب أمر باطني، والظاهر
من إعتاق الكل أن يتعلق مشيئة المخاطب بالكل دفعة، فلا بد من إخراج البعض ليتحقق التبعيض فتأمل. (القمر)

لا يقال: فحينئذٍ ينبغي أن يجب قراءة جميع ما تيسر من القرآن في الصلاة عملاً بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْرَأُوا مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ﴾؛ لأنا نقول: بناء الأمر على التيسر ينافي ذلك.
(المزمل: ٢٠)
(الشمس: ٥)
و"ما" تجيء بمعنى "من" مجازا، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّمَاءِ وَمَا بَنَاهَا﴾ ولم يتعرض لمثل ذلك في "من" على ما ذكرت؛ لقلته. وتدخل في صفات من يعقل أيضًا، تقول: "ما زيد؟ فجوابه الكريم، وقال الله تعالى:
فَانْكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ أي الطيبات لكم.
(النساء: (٣)
وكل للإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد أي جعل كل فرد كان ليس معه غيره، فهذا يسمى عموم الأفراد.
فحينئذٍ أي فحين إذ كان كلمة "ما" عامة (القمر) على التيسر إلخ فبهذه القرينة يحمل كلمة "ما" على الخصوص؛ لأنه يحتمله كما قال المصنف له أولاً . (السنبلي) ينافي ذلك فإنه دال على أن المراد ما تيسر بصفة الانفراد لا على سبيل الاجتماع فإنه عند الاجتماع لا يبقى اليسر بل ينقلب عسرا (القمر) والسماء إلخ : الواو للقسم، وكلمة "ما" بمعنى "من" والمراد به الله تعالى. (القمر) . في من إلخ فإن "من" تستعمل في غير ذوات العقول مجازا على ما مر . (القمر)
ما طاب لكم إلخ كلمة "ما" كناية عن النساء وهن وإن كانت ذوات العقول إلا أنه أريد ههنا الوصف لا الذات كما قال البيضاوي وإلى هذه الإرادة أشار الشارح بقوله : أي إلخ (القمر) على سبيل الإفراد : أي لا على سبيل الاجتماع كما يكون في لفظ الجميع، فلو قال: كل امرأة لي تدخل الدار فهي طالق وله نسوة أربع فدخلت واحدة منهن الدار طلقت ولا ينتظر وقوع الطلاق عليها إلى دخول الباقيات، والإفراد بكسر الهمزة مصدر من الإفعال فمعنى كلام المصنف أن كلمة "كل" لإحاطة الأفراد إذا دخلت على المنكر ، ولإحاطة الأجزاء إذا دخلت على المعرف وكل ذلك على سبيل الإفراد وفي عبارة الشارح مسامحة والأولى أن يقول أي جعل كل فرد أوكل جزء كان ليس معه غيره. (القمر) أي جعل كل فرد إلخ فقوله: "كل" عبد لي يدخل الدار فهو "حر" معناه كل عبد دخل على الانفراد حتى لو دخل واحد عتق من غير توقف على دخول الآخر بخلاف ما لو قال: "إن دخلتم". (السنبلي)
Section on general terms. Explanation of words of generality. Except one according to Abu Hanifa and for him; because the word 'man' is for generality, and 'man' is for partitivity, so acting upon both is not valid, unless one of them remains not freed, and similarly the will is a specific attribute of the addressee. And it is said: It is specific. The word 'man' is for partitivity in both examples, but in the first example each of the slave being considered as a part regardless of others, so all are freed. In the second example, the one (slave) whose will relates to all at once, and he is the addressee, so it is not valid except by specifying some. But it is objected that if he wills all sequentially, then the meaning of partitivity applies. The author of al-Talwih: It applies to each one that he willed his emancipation while being a part of the slave, so ponder on it. If he says to his female slave: 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed, as a corollary because the word 'ma' is general; because the meaning then is: If all that is in your womb is a male, you are free. And it was not so, but part of what was in her womb was a male, and part was a female, so the condition was not fulfilled, so she is not freed. Except one, which is the last, if the addressee frees them sequentially. And if he frees them all at once, they are all freed except one, and the choice in specifying him is to the owner. Because if the addressee freed all the slaves, the meaning of partitivity would be completely negated, so there must be a meaning of partitivity—so there must be that one remains among them. (al-Qamar) According to Abu Hanifa, for him. But according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the addressee may free them all, acting on the word of generality, and 'man' is for explanation. (al-Qamar) And 'man' for partitivity: the common usage of 'man' for partitivity is when its genitive has parts, and here there is no context that confirms generality, necessitating 'man' to be for explanation. (al-Qamar) And it is said: the speaker is the author of al-Tawdih. (al-Qamar) From the two examples: i.e., 'whoever wills from my slaves' etc., and 'whomever you will from my slaves' etc. (al-Qamar) Then every single one (of the slaves) is true that the addressee willed his emancipation, i.e., the emancipation of each one of the slaves while being a part of the slaves, so all should be freed. But the matter is not so according to the Greatest Imam. (al-Qamar) So 'ponder' etc. alludes to two matters, one of which is in Qamar al-Aqmar, and the second: it may be said that the authority to free for the addressee is obtained only from this statement, and this statement is interpreted as partitivity, so he is not authorized to free all; because it contradicts the owner's intent. So if he frees at once, the choice is to the owner; if he frees sequentially, the last is not freed, so reflect. (al-Sunbuli) So ponder on it: perhaps it is a pointer to the answer to the objection, and its explanation: that the will pertaining to all individually and sequentially is an internal matter, and the apparent from freeing all is that the addressee's will pertains to all at once, so some must be excluded to realize partitivity, so ponder. (al-Qamar) It cannot be said: Then it should be obligatory to recite all that is easy of the Quran in prayer, acting on His statement: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran.' For we say: Building the command on ease contradicts that. (al-Muzzammil 73:20) (al-Shams 91:5) And 'ma' may come in the meaning of 'man' metaphorically, as His statement: 'By the sky and what built it.' And he did not address similar to that in 'man' as I mentioned due to its rarity. And it is also used for attributes of rational beings: you say 'What is Zaid?' and the answer is 'the generous.' And Allah said: 'Then marry what is good for you,' i.e., the good women for you. (al-Nisa 4:3) And 'kull' for encompassment on the basis of individuality, i.e., making each individual as if not with another, so this is called generality of individuals. Then, i.e., when the word 'ma' is general (al-Qamar) 'on ease' etc., so by this context the word 'ma' is interpreted as specific; because it admits that as the author said first. (al-Sunbuli) That contradicts it, because it indicates that the intended is what is easy individually, not collectively, because in collectivity ease does not remain but becomes difficulty. (al-Qamar) 'And the sky' etc.: the waw is for oath, and 'ma' is in the meaning of 'man' and intended is Allah. (al-Qamar) 'In man' etc., for 'man' is used figuratively for non-rational beings as mentioned. (al-Qamar) 'What is good for you' etc., the word 'ma' is a metaphor for women, and they are rational beings, but here the description is intended, not the essence, as al-Baydawi said. And to this intention the commentator (al-Qamar) pointed by saying 'i.e., etc.' (al-Qamar) 'On the basis of individuality': i.e., not on the basis of collectivity as in the word 'jami'. So if one says: 'Every wife of mine who enters the house is divorced,' and he has four wives, and one of them enters the house, she is divorced, and one does not wait for the divorce to occur upon her till the entry of the others. And 'ifrad' (individuality) with kasra on the alif is a masdar from if'al, so the meaning of the author's statement is that the word 'kull' is for encompassment of individuals when it is attached to an indefinite noun, and for encompassment of parts when attached to a definite noun, and all that is on the basis of individuality. In the statement of the commentator (al-Qamar) there is leniency, and it is better to say: i.e., making each individual or each part as if not with another. (al-Qamar) i.e., making each individual etc. So his statement: 'Every slave of mine who enters the house is free' means each slave entered individually, so if one enters, he is free without waiting for the entry of the other, unlike if he said: 'If you enter.' (al-Sunbuli)
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary, specifically from Noorul Anwaar, discussing the words of generality (alfaz al-umum). The author examines the usage of the Arabic words 'man' and 'ma' in legal contexts, particularly in expressions of emancipation. 

The passage begins by addressing the dispute regarding a master's statement: 'Whoever wills from my slaves is free.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the word 'man' here indicates partitivity (tab'eed), meaning that not all slaves can be freed at once; at least one must remain unfreed. The reason is that 'man' is a word of generality ('umum) but also carries the meaning of partitivity. If the master frees them sequentially, only the last remains unfreed. If he frees them all at once, the master (owner) chooses which one remains unfreed. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the master may free all slaves because 'man' here is for explanation (bayan), not partitivity. The text then quotes the commentary Qamar al-Aqmar which explains that 'man' is used for partitivity when its object has parts, and there is no context (qareenah) indicating generality. 

The next example is a conditional statement: 'If what (ma) is in your womb is a male, you are free.' If she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed according to Abu Hanifa because 'ma' is general, meaning 'all of what is in your womb,' so the condition is not fulfilled. The text then addresses an objection: if 'ma' is general, then the Quranic verse 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' (73:20) would require reciting all that is easy collectively, which is impossible. The response is that the word 'tayassara' (easy) indicates ease individually, not collectively, so 'ma' is interpreted as specific. 

The passage also discusses the word 'kull' (every) and its usage. 'Kull' indicates encompassment on an individual basis (ifrad), not collective. For example, 'Every slave of mine who enters the house is free' means each slave individually; if one enters, he is freed without waiting for others. This differs from 'If you (pl.) enter,' which requires collective entry. 

The commentary emphasizes the importance of context (qareenah) in determining whether a word is general or specific. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the usage of 'man' and 'ma' based on whether the object has parts and whether there is a qareenah indicating generality or partitivity. These linguistic principles have direct implications for legal rulings in contracts, oaths, and emancipations.

Key Terms
من (man): A word that can indicate generality (عموم) or partitivity (تبعيض) depending on context and whether its genitive has parts.
ما (ma): A general word that can indicate 'what' or 'that which'; also used metaphorically for 'man' or for attributes.
كل (kull): A word of general encompassment, covering individuals when attached to an indefinite noun, or parts when attached to a definite noun, on an individual basis (على سبيل الإفراد).
التبعيض (al-tab'eed): Partitivity; a meaning that some specific part is intended, not the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave-girl: 'If what (ما) is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, according to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) She is free because the condition is partially fulfilled.
B) She is not free because the condition required all to be male.
C) She is free only if she gives birth to two males.
D) She is free and the master must free her.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'man' as partitive or general depends on whether the genitive has parts and the presence of a contextual indicator.

The General Words 'Ma' and 'Kull' in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing the general words 'ma' and 'kull'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general words 'ma' and 'kull' and apply the Hanafi usul rule that 'kull' with an indefinite noun gives generality of individuals (umum al-afrad) and with a definite noun gives generality of parts (umum al-ajza).

Core Idea
The general word 'kull' yields different rulings depending on whether its noun is indefinite (generality of individuals) or definite (generality of parts).

This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh text elaborates on two key words of generality: 'ma' and 'kull'. Understanding their precise usage is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts and deriving rulings. The text clarifies that 'ma' can refer to non-rational beings metaphorically for rational, while 'kull' operates on nouns and its scope changes with definiteness—a distinction that has practical consequences in oaths and rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two words of generality discussed
Note the primary focus: 'ma' (ما) and 'kull' (كل). The passage also mentions 'kullama' (كلما) as a combination. Recognize their roles in expressing generality.
2
Distinguish the functions of 'ma'
Observe how 'ma' is used in Quranic verses and Arabic language: it can substitute for 'man' metaphorically and apply to attributes of rational beings. Identify the proof texts cited.
Example: 'marry what is good for you' means 'good women'.
3
Analyze the semantics of 'kull'
Understand that 'kull' signifies encompassing each individual separately (generality of individuals). It requires annexation to a noun, not a verb, except when combined with 'ma' into 'kullama'.
The text gives examples with divorce oaths to illustrate this.
4
Apply the rule of definiteness for 'kull'
Learn the core distinction: if 'kull' enters an indefinite noun (naktah), it gives generality of individuals; if it enters a definite noun (ma'rifah), it gives generality of parts. Use the pomegranate example to test this.
The pomegranate example is a linguistic test for truth/falsity.
5
Consider 'kullama' for verbs
Note that when 'kull' is attached to 'ma', it generalizes verbs, as in 'whenever I marry a woman' meaning each time the action occurs.
This is a distinct usage from 'kull' with nouns.

لا يقال: فحينئذٍ ينبغي أن يجب قراءة جميع ما تيسر من القرآن في الصلاة عملاً بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاقْرَأُوا مَا تَيَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ﴾؛ لأنا نقول: بناء الأمر على التيسر ينافي ذلك. و(ما) تجيء بمعنى (من) مجازا، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّمَاءِ وَمَا بَنَاهَا﴾. ولم يتعرض لمثل ذلك في (من) على ما ذكرت؛ لقلته. وتدخل في صفات من يعقل أيضًا، تقول: (ما زيد؟) فجوابه الكريم، وقال الله تعالى: ﴿فَانْكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ﴾ أي الطيبات لكم. وكل للإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد أي جعل كل فرد كان ليس معه غيره، فهذا يسمى عموم الأفراد. وهي تصحب الأسماء، فتعمها، أي تدخل على الأسماء فتعمها دون الأفعال؛ لأنها لازمة الإضافة، والمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا اسما. فإن قال: (كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق) يحنث بتزوج كل امرأة، ولا يقع الطلاق على امرأة واحدة مرتين. ولما كانت كلمة (كل) لعموم مدخولها فإن دخلت على النكرة أوجبت عموم أفراده؛ لأنه مدلولها لغةً، وإن دخلت على المعرف أوجبت عموم أجزائه؛ لأنه مدلولها عرفًا، ولهذا لو قال: أنت طاق كل تطليقة يقع الثلاث، وإن قال كل التطليقة يقع واحدة. حتى فرقوا بين قولهم كل رمان مأكول، وكل الرمان مأكول بالصدق والكذب أي بصدق الأول وكذب الثاني؛ لأن معنى الأول كل فرد من الرمان مما يصلح أن يؤكل وهو صادق، ومعنى الثاني : كل أجزاء الرمان مما يؤكل وهو كذب؛ لأن القشر لا يؤكل قط. وإذا وصلت بـ(ما) أوجبت عموم الأفعال، بأن يقول : (كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق).
It should not be said: Then it would be necessary to recite all that is easy of the Quran in prayer based on His statement: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran.' For we say: The command being based on ease contradicts that. And (ma) comes in the meaning of (man) metaphorically, as in His statement: 'By the sky and what built it.' And he did not mention similar for (man) as I mentioned, due to its scarcity. And it enters the attributes of rational beings as well; you say: 'What is Zayd?' The answer is 'the generous.' And Allah said: 'Then marry what is good for you,' meaning the good women for you. And kull is for encompassing on the basis of individuality, i.e., making each individual as if nothing else is with it; this is called generality of individuals. And it accompanies nouns, making them general, i.e., it enters nouns and makes them general, not verbs, because it requires annexation, and the annexed to can only be a noun. If one says: 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' he breaks the oath by marrying any woman, and divorce does not occur on one woman twice. Since the word kull is for generality of its object, if it enters on an indefinite noun it requires generality of its individuals, because that is its linguistic meaning; and if it enters on a definite noun it requires generality of its parts, because that is its customary meaning. Therefore, if he says: 'You are divorced every divorce,' it results in three; if he says 'Every divorce,' it results in one. Thus they distinguished between their statements 'Every pomegranate is edible' and 'All the pomegranate is edible' by truth and falsehood, i.e., the first is true and the second false, because the meaning of the first is that every individual pomegranate is suitable to be eaten—that is true—and the meaning of the second is that all parts of the pomegranate are eaten—that is false, because the peel is never eaten. And when it is connected with (ma) it requires generality of verbs, so that he says: 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced.'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh text explaining two words of generality: 'ma' and 'kull'. It begins by addressing a potential objection: if the Quranic command 'recite what is easy' is taken as a general command to recite all easy parts, it would contradict the ease itself. The author clarifies that 'ma' can be used metaphorically for 'man' (who) as in the verse 'by the sky and what built it,' meaning 'He who built it.' 'Ma' can also apply to attributes of rational beings, as in 'What is Zayd?' answered by 'the generous,' and in the Quranic verse 'marry what is good for you,' meaning 'good women.' The text then explains 'kull' (every/all) as a word of generality that encompasses on an individual basis—each individual separately, not collectively. It operates on nouns, not verbs, because it requires annexation to a noun. Examples: if someone says 'every woman I marry is divorced,' he violates the oath by marrying any one woman, and the divorce does not apply twice to the same woman. If 'kull' enters an indefinite noun, it gives generality of individuals (each separate thing); if it enters a definite noun (like 'al-rumman' = the pomegranate), it gives generality of parts (each part of that one thing). This distinction is tested by the example: 'Every pomegranate is edible' is true (each individual pomegranate is edible), but 'All the pomegranate is edible' is false because the peel is inedible. Finally, when 'kull' is combined with 'ma' (kullama = whenever), it generalizes verbs, so 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced' means every time an act of marriage occurs, the woman is divorced.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A word of generality that, in this context, can be used as a generalizer for non-rational or sometimes rational beings.
كل (kull): A word of generality meaning 'every' or 'all', used to encompass individuals or parts depending on the definiteness of the noun it modifies.
عموم الأفراد (umum al-afrad): Generality of individuals: each separate entity covered by the ruling.
عموم الأجزاء (umum al-ajza): Generality of parts: each part of a singular entity covered by the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'كل رمان مأكول' means each individual pomegranate is edible.
B) 'كل الرمان مأكول' means all parts of a pomegranate are edible.
C) 'كل تطليقة' with indefinite noun gives generality of individuals, resulting in three divorces if uttered three times.
D) 'كل التطليقة' with definite noun gives generality of individuals as well.

Key Takeaway
The general word 'kull' in Hanafi usul yields generality of individuals when attached to an indefinite noun and generality of parts when attached to a definite noun, a distinction that has practical consequences in legal rulings such as oaths and divorces.

The Generalizing Particle 'Kull': Individuals vs Parts
Explanation of how 'kull' generalizes nouns and verbs, with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling of 'kull' with indefinite versus definite nouns and apply it to derive the correct legal outcome in oath and divorce examples.

Core Idea
Kull generalizes individuals when the noun is indefinite, but parts when the noun is definite.

This lesson unpacks a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the particle 'kull' (every/all) changes its scope based on the definiteness of the noun it attaches to. The passage from Noor al-Anwar illustrates this with marriage oaths and divorce statements, showing how the same word yields different legal results. Understanding this distinction is essential for interpreting general expressions (amm) in the Quran, Hadith, and juristic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun's definiteness
Look at the noun immediately following 'kull'. Is it indefinite (nakira, without al-) or definite (marifa, with al-)? This determines the type of generality.
Indefinite: 'kull imra'atin' (every woman). Definite: 'kull al-tatliqati' (the entire divorce).
2
Apply the genericity rule
If the noun is indefinite, 'kull' generalizes over individuals (afrad) – each separate entity. If definite, it generalizes over parts (ajza') – the components of a single entity.
This rule is based on language (lugha) for indefinite and custom (urf) for definite.
3
Derive the legal consequence
Use the rule to interpret statements in oaths, divorces, or contracts. For example, 'kull tatliqatin' (every divorce) means each individual divorce, so saying it three times effects three divorces. 'Kull al-tatliqati' (the entire divorce) means all parts of one divorce, so it effects only one.
The same logic applies to the pomegranate example: 'kull rumman' (every pomegranate) vs 'kull al-rumman' (the entire pomegranate).
4
Distinguish 'kullama'
When 'kull' is followed by the masdariyya 'ma' (forming kullama), it generalizes over verb actions (times), not nouns. For example, 'kullama tazawwajtu imra'atan fa-hiya taliq' means 'every time I marry a woman she is divorced', focusing on the verb's occurrence.
Here the noun's generality is implicit, needed only to the extent that the verb's generality requires it.

مبحث العام
بيان ألفاظ العموم
وهي تصحب الأسماء، فتعمها، أي تدخل على الأسماء فتعمها دون الأفعال؛ لأنها لازمة الإضافة، والمضاف إليه لا يكون إلا اسما، فإن قال: "كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق" يحنث بتزوج كل امرأة، ولا يقع الطلاق على امرأة واحدة مرتين. ولما كانت كلمة "كل" لعموم مدخولها فإن دخلت على النكرة أوجبت عموم أفراده؛ لأنه مدلولها لغةً، وإن دخلت على المعرف أوجبت عموم أجزائه؛ لأنه مدلولها عرفًا، ولهذا لو قال: أنت طالق كل "تطليقة يقع الثلاث، وإن قال كل التطليقة يقع واحدة.
العموم الأفراد
حتى فرقوا بين قولهم كل رمان مأكول، وكل الرمان مأكول بالصدق والكذب أي بصدق الأول وكذب الثاني؛ لأن معنى الأول كل فرد من الرمان مما يصلح أن يؤكل وهو أي عادة: صادق، ومعنى الثاني : كل أجزاء الرمان مما يؤكل وهو كذب؛ لأن القشر لا يؤكل قط. وإذا وصلت بـ"ما" أو جبت عموم الأفعال، بأن يقول : "كلما تزوّجت امرأة فهي طالق"، فتعمها إلخ : أي يثبت بكلمة كل العموم فيما دخلت هي عليه. (القمر) ولا يقع الطلاق إلخ أي لو تزوج امرأة مرتين لا تطلق ثانية؛ إذ العموم في لفظة "كل" يكون قصدًا في الاسم، وأما العموم في الفعل فهو ضروري ضمني يقدر بقدر الضرورة، فيجب عموم الفعل بحيث يساوي أفراد الفعل أفراد الاسم، ولا ضرورة لنا في اعتبار أفراد الفعل المتعلقة بفرد الاسم في المرتبة الثانية وما بعدها. (القمر) لأنه مدلولها لغة إلخ أي لأن عموم أفراد مدخول كل مدلول كلمة "كل" لغة. (القمر) أجزائه لا عموم أفراده، إذ لا أفراد له المحشي) لأنه مدلولها عرفاً إلخ أي لأن عموم أجزاء مدلول "كل" بحيث يشمل الحكم كل جزء من أجزائه مدلول "كل" عرفا، والعرف قاض على اللغة.(القمر) يقع واحدة فإن مجموع أجزاء تطليسقة تطليقة واحدة. (القمر) أي بصدق الأول إلخ: إيماء إلى أن قول المصنف له بالصدق والكذب نشر على ترتيب اللف. (القمر) مما يوكل أي مما يصلح أن يوكل عادة. (القمر) الأفعال : أي عموم مصادر الأفعال التي دخلت عليه "كلما"؛ لأن كلمة "كل" لازم الإضافة، والفعل لا يقع مضافًا إليه، فيدخل ما المصدرية، ليصح أن يكون مضافًا إليه، ويكون المصدر بمعنى الوقت، فمعنى قولنا: "كل ما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق كل وقت وقع مني التزوج ولو بعد زوج آخر كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر)
فمعناه كل وقت أتزوج امرأة فهي طالق، فهو قصدًا يقع على عموم التزويجات، ويثبت الأسماء فيه ضمنًا؛ لأن عموم التزوج لا يكون إلا لا يكون إلا بعموم النساء، فيحنث بكل تزوج، سواء تزوج امرأة مراراً ، أو تزوج امرأةً بعد امرأة.
عموم
كعموم الأفعال في "كل" أي كما أن عموم الأفعال يثبت في لفظ "كل" ضمنًا؛ لعموم الأسماء بعكس كلمة "كلما".
The topic of al-‘Amm. Explanation of the particles of generality. They accompany nouns, thus generalizing them, i.e., they enter upon nouns and generalize them, not verbs; because they necessarily require idāfa, and the mudāf ilayh can only be a noun. So if one says, 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' he violates by marrying every woman, and divorce does not occur upon one woman twice. Since the word 'kull' is for the generality of what it enters, if it enters upon an indefinite noun it necessitates generality of its individuals, because that is its literal meaning; and if it enters upon a definite noun it necessitates generality of its parts, because that is its customary meaning. Hence, if one says, 'You are divorced every divorce,' three divorces occur; but if he says, 'You are divorced the entire divorce,' one divorce occurs. The generality of individuals: They distinguished between saying 'every pomegranate is edible' and 'the entire pomegranate is edible' by truth and falsehood, meaning the first is true and the second false, because the meaning of the first is every individual of pomegranate that is apt to be eaten – and that is true – while the meaning of the second is all parts of the pomegranate that are eaten, which is false, because the peel is never eaten. And when it is attached to 'ma', it necessitates generality of verbs, such as saying, 'Whenever I marry a woman she is divorced.' (Commentary from al-Qamar:) So it is established by the word 'kull' generality in what it enters. (al-Qamar:) And divorce does not occur etc., meaning if he marries a woman twice, she is not divorced the second time; because generality in the word 'kull' is intended in the noun, but generality in the verb is necessary implicit estimated to the extent of necessity, so the generality of the verb must equal the individuals of the verb to the individuals of the noun, and we have no need to consider the individuals of the verb related to the individual of the noun in the second and subsequent instances. (al-Qamar:) Because it is its literal meaning etc., meaning because generality of individuals of what 'kull' enters is the literal meaning of the word 'kull'. (al-Qamar:) Its parts, not generality of its individuals, since it has no individuals (the commentator:) because it is its customary meaning etc., meaning because generality of parts of what 'kull' enters such that the ruling includes every part of its parts is the customary meaning of 'kull', and custom prevails over language. (al-Qamar:) One divorce occurs, because the total of parts of one divorce is one divorce. (al-Qamar:) 'By truth and falsehood' etc.: indicating that the author's statement 'by truth and falsehood' is a distributive arrangement of chiasmus. (al-Qamar:) 'Apt to be eaten' i.e., that which is suitable to be eaten habitually. (al-Qamar:) 'Verbs': i.e., generality of the verbal nouns that 'kullama' enters; because the word 'kull' necessarily requires idāfa, and the verb cannot be mudāf ilayh, so the masdariyya 'ma' is inserted so that it can be mudāf ilayh, and the verbal noun takes the meaning of time. So the meaning of our saying: 'Every time I marry a woman she is divorced' is every time marriage occurs from me, even if after another husband – as said by Ibn al-Malik. (al-Qamar:) So its meaning is every time I marry a woman she is divorced; it intentionally falls upon the generality of marriages, and the nouns are established therein implicitly, because the generality of marriage only occurs with generality of women. So he violates with every marriage, whether he marries one woman repeatedly or marries woman after woman. Generality: like the generality of verbs in 'kull', i.e., just as the generality of verbs is established in the word 'kull' implicitly, due to the generality of nouns, unlike the word 'kullamā'.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the rules governing the particle 'kull' (every/all) in Arabic, particularly how it generates legal generality ('umum'). The author first notes that particles of generality only apply to nouns, not verbs, because they require an idafa construct. For example, if someone says 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' he breaks the oath by marrying any woman, but divorce does not occur twice on the same woman because the generality in the noun is intentional, while the generality in the verb is implicit and only as needed. The key distinction: when 'kull' enters an indefinite noun (e.g., 'every pomegranate'), it generalizes over individuals; when it enters a definite noun (e.g., 'the entire pomegranate'), it generalizes over parts. This is illustrated with the divorce example: 'every divorce' means three divorces (individuals), while 'the entire divorce' means one divorce (parts). A further example shows the truth-value difference: 'every pomegranate is edible' is true, but 'the entire pomegranate is edible' is false because the peel is inedible. When 'kull' is attached to 'ma' forming 'kullama', it shifts to generalizing over verb actions (times of occurrence). For instance, 'Whenever I marry a woman she is divorced' means every time a marriage occurs, regardless of the woman. The commentator al-Qamar clarifies that the verb's generality is implied to match the noun's generality, but only to the extent needed. The passage emphasizes that custom ('urf') takes precedence over literal language in determining the meaning of 'kull' with definite nouns.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A particle of generality that, when attached to a noun, generalizes either its individuals (if indefinite) or its parts (if definite).
كلما (kullamā): A compound of 'kull' and the masdariyya 'ma', used to generalize over verb actions, effectively meaning 'every time'.
عموم الأفراد (umum al-afrad): Generality over individuals: each separate entity is included in the ruling.
عموم الأجزاء (umum al-ajza): Generality over parts: the ruling applies to all components of a single entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi usul explanation of the particle 'kull', what is the difference in the number of divorces between saying 'أنت طالق كل تطليقة' and 'أنت طالق كل التطليقة'?
A) Both result in three divorces.
B) Both result in one divorce.
C) 'كل تطليقة' results in three divorces; 'كل التطليقة' results in one divorce.
D) 'كل تطليقة' results in one divorce; 'كل التطليقة' results in three divorces.

Key Takeaway
The definiteness of the noun after 'kull' determines whether it generalizes over individuals or parts, a crucial distinction in interpreting legal texts and oaths under Hanafi usul.

The Collective Generality of 'Al-Jami‘' in Hanafi Usul
Usul rule: the meaning of 'al-jami‘' and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the collective generality (umum al-ijtima') of 'al-jami‘' and the distributive generality of 'kull', and apply the correct interpretation in a given scenario.

Core Idea
'Al-jami‘' requires collective sharing; 'kull' distributes individually.

The word 'al-jami‘' (all together) carries a distinct legal meaning in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: it necessitates that the ruling applies to the group collectively, not distributively. This passage from Nur al-Anwar clarifies this concept using an example from the distribution of war spoils (nafl). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting general expressions (umum) in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of generality
Recognize that 'al-jami‘' produces collective generality (umum al-ijtima'), where the ruling applies to the group as a whole, while 'kull' produces distributive generality (umum infiradi), where each individual gets the full ruling.
This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the literal meaning of 'al-jami‘'
Analyze the literal meaning of 'al-jami‘' as indicating the entire group collectively. The ruling attaches to the aggregate, not to each member separately.
The text states: 'فظ الجميع توجب عموم الاجتماع دون الانفراد'.
3
Examine the nafl example
Apply the concept to the example: if an imam promises spoils to 'all who enter the fort first' (using 'al-jami‘'), and ten enter together, they share one portion collectively. If they enter singly, the first gets the entire promised spoils, interpreted metaphorically like 'kull'.
The example illustrates the practical difference.
4
Recognize the metaphorical shift
Note that when necessity (darura) demands, 'al-jami‘' can be interpreted metaphorically as distributive, but the default is collective.
The text mentions 'بمجازه' (metaphorically) for the single entry case.

Source Text

لفظ الجميع
العموم الانفرادي
حتى إذا قيل: لجميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا"، فدخل عشرة معاً أن
الإمام وقت الجهاد
لهم نفلاً واحدا بينهم جميعًا ، والنفل هو ما يعطيه الإمام زائدا على سهم الغنيمة، فإن دخل عشرة معًا في صورة الجميع يكون الكل مشتركا بين ذلك النفل الموعود عملاً بحقيقته، وإن دخلوا فرادى يستحق النفل الأول خاصة عملاً بمجازه، وهو أن يجعل بمعنى "كل"،
ويثبت إلخ أي للضرورة؛ لأن الأفعال لا تنفك عن الأسماء. (القمر) ويثبت عموم إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن لفظ "كل" لما كان موضوعًا لعموم الأسماء، فكيف يخلو عنه ههنا؟ أو يقال : لما كان لفظ "كل" ههنا لعموم الأفعال، فبطل قول المصنف أولاً، وهي تصحب الأسماء فيعمها، وتقرير الدفع واضح (السنبلي) ضمنا لعموم الأسماء إلخ : فإن في قول القائل: كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق" كما يحنث بتزوج طالق" كما يحنث بتزوج كل امرأة يحنث بكل تزوج بامرأة، وذلك لأن عموم النساء لا يكون إلا بعموم التزوج. (السنبلي) عموم الاجتماع أي عموم أفراد المدخول على سبيل الاجتماع بأن يتعلق الحكم بالمجموع من حيث المجموع (القمر) النفل: وفي "المغرب" النفل بفتحتين ما ينفله الغازي أي يعطاه زائدا على سهمه كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر) بحقيقته أي بحقيقة لفظ الجميع، وهو عموم الاجتماع (القمر)
And the word 'al-jami‘' necessitates generality of togetherness, not individual distribution, as was in the word 'kull'. So it considers all that which the following applies to, collectively together. The word 'al-jami‘'. Individual generality: So if it is said, 'For all who enter this fort first, he gets such-and-such spoils (nafl),' and ten enter together – during the time of jihad the Imam – they have one spoils share divided among them all. The nafl is what the Imam gives in addition to the regular share of booty. If ten enter together in the case of 'al-jami‘', then the whole shares in that promised spoils, operating by its literal meaning; and if they enter singly, the first one alone deserves the spoils, operating by its metaphorical meaning, which is to make it like 'kull'. (Commentary:) 'And it is established' etc., i.e., out of necessity, because verbs are not free from nouns. (al-Qamar:) 'And it is established' etc.: a rebuttal of an expected objection that the word 'kull' was originally for generality of nouns, so how can it be devoid of that here? Or it is said, since the word 'kull' here is for generality of verbs, then the author's earlier statement 'they accompany nouns and generalize them' is invalid, and the rebuttal is clear (al-Sunbuli). 'Implicitly due to generality of nouns' etc.: for in the statement 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' just as he violates by marrying every woman, he also violates by every marriage with a woman, because generality of women only occurs with generality of marriage. (al-Sunbuli) 'Generality of togetherness' i.e., generality of the individuals of the mudāf ilayh in a collective manner, meaning the ruling attaches to the group as a group (al-Qamar). 'Al-nafl': in al-Mughrib, al-nafl (with two fathas) is what the warrior is given extra (yunafluhu) i.e., given in addition to his share, as said by Ibn al-Malik. (al-Qamar) 'By its literal meaning' i.e., by the literal meaning of the word 'al-jami‘', which is collective generality (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'al-jami‘' (all together) and its legal effect: it imposes collective generality, meaning the ruling applies to all members of the group simultaneously as a whole, not individually. This contrasts with 'kull', which distributes the ruling individually (each gets the full share under 'kull'). The example given is from military spoils: if the Imam says, 'For all who enter this fort first, such-and-such spoils,' and ten enter together, they share one portion collectively according to the literal meaning of 'al-jami‘'. If they enter one by one, the first entrant gets the spoils alone, because the word is then interpreted metaphorically like 'kull' (distributive). The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli clarifies that when 'kull' appears in other contexts, its generality of verbs is implied from the generality of nouns. The commentator also defines 'nafl' as extra spoils given by the Imam beyond the regular share. The passage emphasizes that literal meaning of 'al-jami‘' requires the ruling to be collective, not distributive.

Key Terms
الجميع (al-jami‘): A word indicating collective generality, where the ruling applies to the entire group simultaneously rather than distributively.
النفل (al-nafl): Extra spoils of war given by the Imam to soldiers beyond their regular share, used here as an example in the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi explanation of 'al-jami‘', if an imam says, 'For all who enter this fort first, such-and-such spoils,' and ten soldiers enter together, what do they receive?
A) Each soldier receives the full spoils individually.
B) The ten soldiers share one portion of spoils equally.
C) The first soldier among them receives the full spoils.
D) No one receives the spoils because they entered together.

Key Takeaway
The word 'al-jami‘' mandates collective application of the ruling; its literal meaning requires the group to share the consequence together, not each individually.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

8 min
Distinguishing 'al-Jami'' and 'Kull' in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the legal implications of general words 'جميع' and 'كل'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general words 'al-jami'' and 'kull' in Hanafi usul, apply their different legal effects in a given scenario, and explain the reasoning behind the distinction using the example of the imam's reward promise.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'al-jami'' indicates collective generality, while 'kull' indicates individual generality—they are not interchangeable.

Understanding the precise legal implications of general words is fundamental to Hanafi usul. This passage examines two key words—'al-jami'' and 'kull'—which appear similar but carry distinct rulings. By analysing an example from jihad (imam's reward for entering a fortress), you will see how the choice of word determines whether a reward is shared collectively or given individually. The passage also addresses a subtle objection about combining literal and metaphorical meanings, deepening your appreciation of Hanafi interpretive methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two general words and their definitions
Find in the passage the definitions of 'al-jami'' (الجميع) and 'kull' (كل). Note that 'al-jami'' implies 'عموم الاجتماع' (collective generality) while 'kull' implies 'عموم الأفراد' (individual generality). Understand the difference: collective means the ruling applies to the group as a whole; individual means it applies to each member separately.
Collective generality is also expressed as 'الكل من حيث المجموع'.
2
Analyze the imam's reward example for 'al-jami''
Consider the imam's statement: 'لجميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا'. According to the passage, if ten enter together, they share one reward collectively (literal meaning). If they enter individually, the first one alone gets the reward (metaphorical interpretation where 'الجميع' is taken in the sense of 'كل'). Trace how the ruling changes based on the manner of entry.
The literal meaning is applied when the group enters together; the metaphorical meaning (taking it as 'كل') is applied when they enter individually or only one enters.
3
Understand the objection and response about combining literal and metaphorical meanings
The passage mentions an objection: interpreting 'الجميع' both literally (group reward) and metaphorically (individual reward) seems to combine حقيقة and مجاز impermissibly. The response clarifies that the prohibition is about intention, not occurrence. Here, the imam intends the literal for collective entry and the metaphorical for individual entry—this is acceptable because the two scenarios are distinct. Also, a better explanation using دلالة النص (inference) is offered: if a group deserves the reward collectively, then an individual deserves it a fortiori.
This step reinforces the Hanafi principle that you cannot combine حقيقة and مجاز in one intended meaning, but you can intend different meanings for different contexts.
4
Contrast with 'kull' and derive the practical difference
Now examine the same scenario with the word 'كل': if the imam says 'كل من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا', and ten enter together, each one deserves a complete reward individually. This is because 'كل' necessitates individual generality. Compare this with the 'al-jami'' case: here, each gets a full reward, not a shared one. This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the distinction.
This difference is critical in oaths, promises, and penal rulings where the wording determines individual or collective liability.

(السنبلي) عموم الاجتماع أي عموم أفراد المدخول على سبيل الاجتماع بأن يتعلق الحكم بالمجموع من حيث المجموع (القمر) النفل: وفي "المغرب" النفل بفتحتين ما ينفله الغازي أي يعطاه زائدا على سهمه كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر) بحقيقته أي بحقيقة لفظ الجميع، وهو عموم الاجتماع (القمر)

بيان ألفاظ العموم
واعتُرِضَ عليه بأنه يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز حينئذ. والجواب: أنه لا يستعار بمعنى كل بعينه؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك كان للكل نفل تام في صورة ما دخلوا معًا، بل هو مجاز عن السابق في الدخول واحدا كان أو جماعةً، فيكون للجماعة نفل واحد كما هو للأول الواحد؛ عملاً بعموم المجاز، والأولى أن يقال: إن الغرض من هذا الكلام هو غرض الإمام إظهار الشجاعة والجلادة فإذا استحقه جماعة باعتبار ظاهر معناه الحقيقي، فاستحقاق الواحد له بالطريق الأولى بدلالة النص؛ لأنه فيه إظهار كمال الشجاعة.
وفي كلمة "كل" يجب لكل منهم النفل، يعني إذا قال: "كل من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا ، فدخل عشرة معًا، يجب لكل واحد منهم نفل تام؛ لأن كلمة "كل" يلزم الجمع إلخ لأنهم لو دخلوا معا يستحقون النفل عملاً لفظ الجمع، ولو دخلوا فرادى استحقه الأول بعموم عملاً بالمجاز كما إذا لم يدخل إلا ،واحد فقيل: إن دخلوا معًا يحمل الكلام على الحقيقة، وإن دخلوا فرادى، أو دخل واحد فقط تعين المجاز، ورد صاحب "الكشف" "والتوضيح" بأن امتناع الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، إنما هو بالنظر إلى الإرادة دون الوقوع وههنا قد جمع بينهما في الإرادة ليحمل تارة على الحقيقة وأخرى على المجاز؛ إذ لو أريد حقيقة الجمع لم يستحق الفرد، ولو أريد مجازه لم يستحق الجميع نفلاً واحدا، بل يستحق كل واحد نفلاً تاماً كما لو صرح بلفظ "كل"، فعلى هذا لابد من الرجوع إلى الجواب الذي بينه الشارح فتدبر.(السنبلي) لو كان ذلك أي استعارة الجميع لكلمة "كل". (القمر) كان للكل إلخ فإن العشرة إذا دخلوا معا يجب لكل واحد منهم نفل تام في صورة كلمة "كل" على ما سيجيء. (القمر) عملا بعموم المجاز: وهو عبارة عن إرادة معنى مجازي يكون المعنى الحقيقي فردًا منه، كأن يراد بالأسد الشجاع (القمر) والأولى إلخ ووجه هذا أن حمل اللفظ على المجاز المحض يحتاج إلى قيام القرينة، وعموم المجاز أيضا كذلك، وفيما قلنا ليس هذا ظاهرًا من كلام الشارح (المحشي) .. أن يقال: إن في وجه استحقاق الأول النفل، إن دخلوا فرادى في صورة كلمة "الجميع". (القمر) بدلالة النص: قيل: لا نسلم أن دلالة النص معتبرة في كلام العباد فيه أن هذا الكلام غير مقبول، ألا ترى أنه لو قال السيد لعبده: لا تعط ذرة، فهو منع عن إعطاء ما فوق الذرة، وهذه دلالة النص كذا قالوا. (القمر)
Section on General Terms. Explanation of the words of generality. So its meaning is 'every time I marry a woman, she is divorced,' so it intentionally applies to the generality of marriages, and nouns are established in it implicitly, because the generality of marriage only occurs through the generality of women, so he is guilty by oath breaking with every marriage, whether he marries a woman multiple times or marries one woman after another. Generality: like the generality of actions in 'kull'—that is, just as the generality of actions is established in the word 'kull' implicitly, due to the generality of the nouns, unlike the word 'kullama'. And the word 'al-jami'' necessitates the generality of collective gathering, not individuality, as was the case with the word 'kull', so everything that applies to what follows it is considered collectively together. The word 'al-jami'' [and] individual generality. So if it is said: 'For all who enter this fortress first, such-and-such extra reward [is due],' and ten entered together at the time of jihad, they have one extra reward shared among them all. The extra reward (nafl) is what the imam gives in addition to the share of the spoils. If ten entered together in the case of 'al-jami'', they all share in that promised extra reward according to its literal meaning, but if they entered individually, the first one alone deserves it according to its metaphorical meaning, which is to take it in the sense of 'kull'. 'And it is established' etc., i.e., out of necessity, because actions are not separate from nouns. (Al-Qamar) 'And it establishes the generality' etc.: a rebuttal of an implied objection: the gist is that since the word 'kull' is placed for the generality of nouns, how can it be devoid of it here? Or one says: since the word 'kull' here is for the generality of actions, the statement of the author is invalidated first, and it accompanies nouns and generalizes them, and the rebuttal is clear. (Al-Sunbulī) 'Implicitly for the generality of nouns' etc.: for in the speaker's statement 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' just as he breaks the oath by marrying every woman, he breaks the oath by every marriage with a woman, because the generality of women only occurs through the generality of marriage. (Al-Sunbulī) 'Generality of gathering' i.e., generality of the individuals of the included thing on the basis of gathering, such that the ruling attaches to the group as a group. (Al-Qamar) 'Al-nafl': In 'Al-Mughrib', al-nafl (with two fatha) is what the warrior is given, i.e., given in addition to his share, as said by Ibn al-Malik. (Al-Qamar) 'According to its literal meaning' i.e., according to the literal meaning of the word 'al-jami'', which is generality of gathering. (Al-Qamar) [Continuation from page 217] Section on General Terms. Explanation of the words of generality. And it was objected to that it necessitates combining the literal and metaphorical meanings then. The answer: it is not borrowed in the sense of 'kull' exactly, because if it were so, each would have a complete extra reward in the case where they entered together. Rather, it is a metaphor for the one who enters first, whether that is one person or a group, so the group has one extra reward just as the one individual does, acting according to the generality of the metaphor. And it is better to say that the purpose of this speech is the imam's purpose of showing bravery and courage, so if a group deserves it based on the apparent literal meaning, then the individual deserves it a fortiori by indication of the text (dalil al-nass), because in that there is the showing of complete courage. And in the word 'kull', each of them is entitled to the extra reward, meaning if he says: 'Whoever enters this fortress first, he has such-and-such extra reward,' and ten entered together, each one of them is entitled to a complete extra reward, because the word 'kull' necessitates the collective, etc. Because if they entered together they deserve the extra reward acting on the word 'al-jami'', and if they entered individually the first one deserves it individually according to the metaphor, just as if only one entered. It was said: if they entered together, the speech is taken literally, if they entered individually or only one entered, the metaphor is applied. And the author of 'Al-Kashf' and 'Al-Tawdih' refuted that the prohibition of combining literal and metaphorical meaning is only with regard to intention, not external occurrence, and here they have been combined in intention so that it is sometimes taken literally and sometimes metaphorically; because if the literal meaning of gathering were intended, the individual would not deserve it, and if its metaphorical meaning were intended, the group would not deserve one extra reward, but rather each would deserve a complete extra reward as if he had explicitly said 'kull'. So on this, one must refer to the answer that the commentator explained, so ponder. (Al-Sunbulī) 'If it were so' i.e., the borrowing of 'al-jami'' for the word 'kull'. (Al-Qamar) 'Each would have' etc.: indeed, if ten entered together, each one of them must have a complete extra reward in the case of the word 'kull', as will come. (Al-Qamar) 'Acting according to the generality of the metaphor': it is intending a metaphorical meaning of which the literal meaning is a part, like intending the brave by 'lion'. (Al-Qamar) 'And it is better' etc.: the point is that taking the word in the pure metaphor requires the presence of a contextual indicator, and the generality of the metaphor also requires that, and what we said is not apparent from the commentator's words. (Al-Muhashshī) .. that one should say: the reason the first one deserves the extra reward if they entered individually in the case of the word 'al-jami''. (Al-Qamar) 'By indication of the text': it is said: we do not accept that indication of the text is considered in human speech; this statement is unacceptable. Do you not see that if the master says to his slave: 'Do not give a grain' (zarrah), it forbids giving what is above a grain? This is the indication of the text, as they said. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of the general words 'الجميع' (al-jami') and 'كل' (kull) from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary. The author explains the key difference: 'الجميع' implies collective generality (عموم الاجتماع) where the ruling applies to the group as a whole, while 'كل' implies individual generality (عموم الأفراد) where the ruling applies to every single individual separately. The example of the imam's promise of extra reward (نفل) for the first to enter a fortress illustrates this: if the imam says 'لجميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً' (for all who enter this fortress first), and ten enter together, they share one reward collectively (literal meaning of الجميع). If they enter separately, the first one alone gets the reward (metaphorical meaning, interpreting الجميع as كل). The commentary then addresses an objection: this interpretation seems to combine literal and metaphorical usage. The response clarifies that الجميع is not taken exactly as كل, but as a metaphor for the first entrant (singular or plural). A better explanation is that the imam's goal is to encourage bravery; if a group enters together, they collectively exhibit courage, and the individual who enters first a fortiori deserves the reward by دلالة النص (inference from the text). The passage then contrasts with 'كل': if the imam says 'كل من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً' (everyone who enters first), then each of the ten who enter together individually deserves a complete reward, because كل necessitates individual generality. The discussion includes scholarly critiques from commentators like Al-Sunbulī, Al-Qamar, and Al-Muhashshī, highlighting the careful reasoning required in hanafi usul. The passage ultimately affirms the distinction and warns against conflating collective and individual generality.

Key Terms
الجميع (al-jami'): A general word that indicates collective generality (عموم الاجتماع), meaning the ruling applies to all individuals as a group.
كل (kull): A general word that indicates individual generality (عموم الأفراد), meaning the ruling applies to every single individual separately.
دلالة النص (dalil al-nass): Inference from the explicit text to a ruling that is more strongly indicated, e.g., forbidding a grain implies forbidding more by priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis in the passage, if an imam says 'لجميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا' and ten soldiers enter together, what is the ruling?
A) Each soldier gets a full reward individually.
B) The ten soldiers share one reward equally.
C) The first soldier to step in gets the full reward.
D) No reward is given because the condition is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'الجميع' and 'كل' have distinct legal scopes: collective vs. individual generality, which leads to different rulings in promises and oaths.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
Effect of 'Kull', 'Al-Jami'', and 'Man' on Conditional Rewards
Analysis of how words of generality affect conditional reward rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of 'kull', 'al-jami'', and 'man' on the interpretation of conditional reward statements and apply the Hanafi principles of `umum` and metaphor to derive rulings for simultaneous and individual entry.

Core Idea
The word of generality determines who gets the reward: 'kull' distributes to each, 'al-jami'' gives one reward to the group, 'man' voids the reward for simultaneous first entrants.

In this passage, we dive into a nuanced discussion from Hanafi usul al-fiqh about how different words of generality impact the ruling of conditional rewards. You will see how the interplay between literal and figurative meanings is handled when multiple people meet a condition simultaneously. This analysis sharpens your ability to apply `umum` and understand the limits of metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual element
Find the word of generality (`kull`, `al-jami'`, or `man`) in the conditional reward phrase. Note its position and syntactic role.
2
Classify the word of generality
Determine whether the word is `amm` (general) and its subtype: `kull` is `amm` on the basis of individuals; `al-jami'` is `amm` on the basis of the group; `man` is `amm` on the basis of genus.
Refer to Hanafi classification: `kull` is qat'i, `man` is zanni in some contexts.
3
Apply the principle of literal vs figurative
Consider the scenario of simultaneous entry. If the literal meaning would give an absurd or unintended result, a metaphorical interpretation may be used. The Hanafi rule allows combining literal and figurative in intent if not in actual occurrence.
The objection about combining haqiqa and majaz is answered by distinguishing intent from occurrence.
4
Derive the ruling for each word
For `kull`: each simultaneous entrant gets a full reward (literal for group? Actually it's metaphorical for individual). For `al-jami'`: the group shares one reward (literal for group, metaphorical for individual? Actually it's metaphorical for the preceding entrant). For `man`: no reward for simultaneous entrants because 'first' remains literal (individual prior).
The difference hinges on whether the word of generality is strong enough to force a metaphorical reading of 'first'.
5
Consider the timing order (individual vs simultaneous)
If entrants come individually, the first entrant gets the reward regardless of the word used (except `man` where only the first individual gets it). If simultaneous, the ruling diverges as above.
