واعتُرِضَ عليه بأنه يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز حينئذ. والجواب: أنه لا يستعار بمعنى كل بعينه؛ لأنه لو كان كذلك كان للكل نفل تام في صورة ما دخلوا معًا، بل هو مجاز عن السابق في الدخول واحدا كان أو جماعةً، فيكون للجماعة نفل واحد كما هو للأول الواحد؛ عملاً بعموم المجاز، والأولى أن يقال: إن الغرض من هذا الكلام هو غرض الإمام إظهار الشجاعة والجلادة فإذا استحقه جماعة باعتبار ظاهر معناه الحقيقي، فاستحقاق النفل التام للواحد له بالطريق الأولى بدلالة النص؛ لأنه فيه إظهار كمال الشجاعة. وفي كلمة "كل" يجب لكل منهم النفل، يعني إذا قال: "كل من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا" ، فدخل عشرة معًا، يجب لكل واحد منهم نفل تام؛ لأن كلمة "كل" يلزم الجمع إلخ لأنهم لو دخلوا معا يستحقون النفل عملاً بلفظ الجمع، ولو دخلوا فرادى استحقه الأول بعموم عملاً بالمجاز كما إذا لم يدخل إلا واحد فقيل: إن دخلوا معًا يحمل الكلام على الحقيقة، وإن دخلوا فرادى، أو دخل واحد فقط تعين المجاز، ورد صاحب "الكشف" و"التوضيح" بأن امتناع الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، إنما هو بالنظر إلى الإرادة دون الوقوع وههنا قد جمع بينهما في الإرادة ليحمل تارة على الحقيقة وأخرى على المجاز؛ إذ لو أريد حقيقة الجمع لم يستحق الفرد، ولو أريد مجازه لم يستحق الجميع نفلاً واحدا، بل يستحق كل واحد نفلاً تاماً كما لو صرح بلفظ "كل"، فعلى هذا لابد من الرجوع إلى الجواب الذي بينه الشارح فتدبر.(السنبلي) لو كان كذلك أي استعارة الجميع لكلمة "كل". (القمر) كان للكل إلخ فإن العشرة إذا دخلوا معا يجب لكل واحد منهم نفل تام في صورة كلمة "كل" على ما سيجيء. (القمر) عملا بعموم المجاز: وهو عبارة عن إرادة معنى مجازي يكون المعنى الحقيقي فردًا منه، كأن يراد بالأسد الشجاع (القمر) والأولى إلخ ووجه هذا أن حمل اللفظ على المجاز المحض يحتاج إلى قيام القرينة، وعموم المجاز أيضا كذلك، وفيما قلنا ليس هذا ظاهرًا من كلام الشارح (المحشي) .. أن يقال: إن في وجه استحقاق الأول النفل، إن دخلوا فرادى في صورة كلمة "الجميع". (القمر) بدلالة النص: قيل: لا نسلم أن دلالة النص معتبرة في كلام العباد فيه أن هذا الكلام غير مقبول، ألا ترى أنه لو قال السيد لعبده: لا تعط ذرة، فهو منع عن إعطاء ما فوق الذرة، وهذه دلالة النص كذا قالوا. (القمر) للإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد فاعتبر كل واحد من الداخلين كان ليس معه غيره، وهو أول بالنسبة إلى من تخلف من الناس ولم يدخل، ولو دخل عشرة فرادى كان النفل للأول خاصة؛ لأنه الأول من كل وجه، وكلمة "كل" يحتمل الخصوص. و في كلمة "من" يبطل النفل، أي إن قال: "من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النفل كذا"، فدخل عشرة معًا لا يستحق أحد منهم؛ لأن الأول اسم لفرد سابق دخل أولاً و لم يوجد بل وجد الداخلون الأولون، وكلمة "من" ليست محكمة في العموم حتى تؤثر في تغيير لفظ أولاً، بخلاف كلمة "كل" و "الجميع"، فإنه يتغير بهما قوله: "أولاً"، ولو دخل عشرة فرادى يستحق الأول النفل خاصة دون الباقيين. في صورة "من" فاعتبر إلخ فإن هذا هو موجب كلمة "كل" على ما مر . (القمر) وهو أي كل واحد من الداخلين أول إلخ، : وهذا دفع ما يتوهم من أنه لما دخل عشرة، فما تحقق الداخل الأول. (القمر) ولم يدخل هذه مسامحة، فإن الداخل أولاً يجب أن يعتبر إضافة إلى الداخل ثانيا لا إلى من ليس بداخل أصلا، فالأولى أن يقول الشارح وهو أي كل واحد من العشرة الداخلين أول بالنسبة إلى من تخلف من الناس الذي يقدر دخوله بعد فتح الحصن. (القمر) اسم لفرد سابق إلخ على ما ثبت بالنقل عن أئمة اللغة، فيقع الأول عند الإطلاق على الفرد السابق، وأما الفريق الأول أو الجماعة الأولى فصرفُ عن الظاهر. (القمر) وكلمة "من" إلخ: دفع دخل هو : أنه لم لا يحمل لفظ أولاً ههنا على المجاز كما حمل عليه في كل.(القمر) كلمة "من" إلخ: لأن عموم "من" ليس على سبيل الإفراد، بل عموم الجنس. (السنبلي) في تغيير لفظ إلخ بأن يكون أول مجازا عن السابق في الدخول واحدا كان أو جماعة.(القمر) :أولاً لأن الأول اسم لفرد سابق، فلما قرن بـ "من" سقط عموم "من"؛ لأن الأول فرد محكم للفرد السابق وكلمة "من" ليست محكمة في العموم، فيحمل المطلق المحتمل على المحكم. (السنبلي) فإنه يتغير إلخ لأن كلمة "كل" و "جميع" تقتضيان التعدد في مدخولهما، فلا بد من أن يراد بالأول السابق في الدخول واحدًا كان أو جماعة ليحصل التعدد. (القمر) يتغير بهما ولا يسقطان للتعارض؛ لأن السقوط مشروط بعدم إمكان العمل بالمتعارضين وههنا العمل ممكن. (القمر)
An objection was raised against it that combining the literal and figurative is necessitated then. The answer: that it is not borrowed in the sense of 'each' specifically; because if it were so, it would be for the all a complete reward in the case of if they entered together, rather it is a metaphor for the preceding in entry, whether one or a group, so for the group there is one reward as for the first individual, acting upon the generality of the metaphor. And better it is to say: the purpose of this speech is the Imam's purpose of showing bravery and toughness. So if a group deserves it by virtue of the apparent literal meaning, then the entitlement of the complete reward for the individual is by the primary path by indication of the text; because in it there is showing of complete bravery. And regarding the word 'kull', it is obligatory for each of them the reward, meaning if he said: 'Each who enters this fortress first has such reward,' and ten entered together, it is obligatory for each one of them a complete reward; because the word 'kull' necessitates the collective etc. because if they entered together they deserve the reward acting upon the word of collective, and if they entered individually, the first deserves it by generality acting upon the metaphor, as if only one entered. So it is said: if they enter together, the speech is carried on the literal; if they enter individually, or only one enters, then the metaphor is determined. The author of 'al-Kashf' and 'al-Tawdih' responded that the prohibition of combining literal and figurative is only with respect to intent, not occurrence, and here it is combined in intent so that it is carried sometimes on literal and other times on figurative; for if the literal of collective were intended, the individual would not deserve, and if its metaphor were intended, the group would not deserve one reward, rather each would deserve a complete reward as if he explicitly used the word 'kull'. So based on this, it is necessary to return to the answer that the commentator explained, so ponder. (al-Sanbali) If it were so, i.e., borrowing of 'al-jami'' for the word 'kull'. (al-Qamar) It would be for the all etc. For the ten if they enter together, each one of them must have a complete reward in the case of the word 'kull' as will come. (al-Qamar) Acting upon the generality of metaphor: which is meaning a metaphorical meaning such that the literal meaning is an individual of it, like meaning by 'lion' the brave. (al-Qamar) And better etc. And the aspect of this is that carrying the word on pure metaphor requires existence of a context, and generality of metaphor is also like that, and in what we said, this is not apparent from the speech of the commentator (al-Muhashi) .. It could be said that in the aspect of the first deserving the reward, if they enter individually in the case of the word 'al-jami''. (al-Qamar) By indication of the text: it was said: we do not accept that indication of the text is considered in the speech of the subjects, and therein this speech is not acceptable, do you not see that if the master said to his slave: 'Do not give a particle,' it is a prohibition of giving what is above the particle, and this is indication of the text, thus they said. (al-Qamar) For encompassment on the basis of individuality, so consider each one of the entrants as if there was no one with him, and he is first relative to those who remained behind from people and did not enter. And if ten entered individually, the reward is for the first specifically; because he is first from every aspect, and the word 'kull' admits specificity. And regarding the word 'man', the reward is void. That is, if he said: 'Whoever enters this fortress first has such reward,' and ten enter together, none of them deserves; because 'first' is a name for a prior individual who entered first, and he did not exist, rather the first entrants existed. And the word 'man' is not definitive in generality such that it affects changing the word 'first', unlike the words 'kull' and 'al-jami'', for they change with it his saying 'first'. And if ten entered individually, the first deserves the reward specifically without the rest. In the case of 'man': So consider etc., for this is the requirement of the word 'kull' as passed. (al-Qamar) And he, i.e., each one of the entrants, is first etc.: This refutes what is imagined that when ten entered, the first entrant is not realized. (al-Qamar) And 'did not enter' is a leniency, for the first entrant must be considered in relation to the second entrant, not to one who is not an entrant at all. So better the commentator would say: He, i.e., each one of the ten entrants, is first relative to those who remained behind from people who are expected to enter after the fortress is opened. (al-Qamar) Name for a prior individual etc.: as established by transmission from language scholars, so 'first' upon absolute usage applies to the prior individual. As for the first group or the first multitude, it is a departure from the apparent. (al-Qamar) And the word 'man' etc.: repelling an objection: why is the word 'first' not carried here on metaphor as it was carried on it in 'kull'? (al-Qamar) The word 'man' etc.: because the generality of 'man' is not on the basis of individuality, but generality of genus. (al-Sanbali) In changing the word etc., by making 'first' a metaphor for the preceding in entry, whether one or a group. (al-Qamar) First, because 'first' is a name for a prior individual, so when it is conjoined with 'man', the generality of 'man' falls; because 'first' is an individual definitive for the prior individual, and the word 'man' is not definitive in generality, so the absolute probable is carried upon the definitive. (al-Sanbali) For it changes etc., because the words 'kull' and 'jami'' necessitate multiplicity in their modified, so it is necessary that by 'first' be meant the preceding in entry, whether one or a group, so that multiplicity occurs. (al-Qamar) It changes by them and does not fall due to conflict; because falling is conditioned by impossibility of acting upon the two conflicting items, and here acting is possible. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of how different words of generality ('kull', 'al-jami'', 'man') affect the ruling in a conditional reward statement such as 'Whoever enters the fortress first gets a reward.' The discussion is part of a commentary on a matn about 'umum. The author first addresses an objection: using the word 'al-jami'' (the all) in such a phrase would require combining its literal meaning (the group) with its figurative meaning (each individual) when multiple people enter simultaneously. The response explains that 'al-jami'' is not borrowed to mean 'each' specifically; rather, it is a metaphor for the preceding entrant, whether one or many, so the group shares one reward. Alternatively, a better approach is that the purpose is to show bravery, and if a group enters together, by the literal meaning they deserve the reward, but by textual indication the individual deserves it more. Then the discussion turns to the word 'kull' (each): if one says 'Each who enters first...', then if ten enter together, each gets a full reward because 'kull' necessitates individual entitlement. If they enter individually, only the first gets it by a metaphorical interpretation that 'first' means the one before others, which applies individually. The author of 'al-Kashf wa al-Tawdih' responds that combining literal and figurative is not problematic in terms of intent, even if not in actual occurrence, and here the intent allows both interpretations: for simultaneous entry the literal meaning applies (the group gets one reward? Actually the text is complex), but for separate entry the figurative applies. On page 218, the analysis continues with the word 'man' (who): if one says 'Whoever enters first...' and ten enter together, none deserves the reward because 'first' is a name for a prior individual, and with 'man' the generality is not definitive enough to change the meaning of 'first' to include a group, unlike 'kull' and 'al-jami''. If they enter individually, only the first gets it. The marginal notes from al-Sanbali, al-Qamar, and al-Muhashi provide additional clarifications and defenses. Al-Qamar explains that each of the ten can be considered 'first' relative to those who haven't entered yet, but this is a leniency. Al-Sanbali distinguishes the generality of 'man' as genus generality, not individual generality. The underlying principle is that words of generality differ in their strength and scope, affecting legal rulings in conditional statements.

Key Terms
الجميع (al-jami'): The word 'all' or 'the entire group', used to indicate totality but in this context analyzed for its metaphorical and literal uses in conditional rewards.
كل (kull): A word of generality meaning 'each' or 'every', implying individual distribution and entitlement.
من (man): A relative pronoun meaning 'whoever', used for generality of genus (umum al-jins) rather than individuals.
الحقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning of a word, as opposed to figurative (majaz).
المجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning, a word used for a meaning other than its original due to a connection.
دلالة النص (dalalah al-nass): Indication of the text, a type of implied meaning where the wording indicates a ruling beyond its literal scope, e.g., 'Do not give a particle' implies not giving anything above a particle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'Whoever enters this fortress first gets a reward,' if ten people enter simultaneously, what is the ruling when the word 'kull' (each) is used instead of 'man'?
A) Only one person gets the reward.
B) Each of the ten gets a complete reward.
C) No one gets the reward.
D) The reward is split equally among them.

Key Takeaway
Words of generality differ in strength: 'kull' distributes, 'al-jami'' gives one reward to a group, and 'man' fails to override the literal meaning of 'first', voiding rewards for simultaneous entry.

The Word 'First' and Particles of Generality
Analyzes the word 'first' with different generality particles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a definitive individual term overrides a probable general particle to determine the ruling of a condition like 'whoever enters first'.

Core Idea
The word 'first' is definitive for one individual; only a strong generality particle like 'كل' can extend it to a group.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar explores a subtle interplay between a definitive word and particles of generality. The phrase 'Whoever enters this fort first' seems straightforward, but Hanafi usul teaches that not all general terms are equal. The outcome depends on whether the particle is individually definitive or only general in genus. Understanding this hierarchy prevents misinterpretation of conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default meaning of 'أولاً'
First, note that 'أولاً' (awwalan) linguistically denotes a single preceding individual. This is its definitive (محكم) meaning unless changed by context.
Linguistic authorities confirm this.
2
Analyze the particle 'من'
The particle 'من' (man) means 'whoever' and gives generality of the genus (عموم الجنس), not of individuals. It is not definitive (غير محكمة) in generality.
Hanafi principle: 'من' is weak in generality.
3
Apply the rule of conflict
When 'أولاً' (definitive individual) and 'من' (probable general) conflict, the absolute probable (المطلق المحتمل) is interpreted in light of the definitive (المحكم). Thus, the individual meaning of 'أولاً' prevails, and 'من' does not change it.
This is based on the principle of الإحكام.
4
Contrast with 'كل' and 'الجميع'
Both 'كل' (kull) and 'الجميع' (al-jami') demand multiplicity inherently. They are definitive in demanding generality of individuals, so they force a metaphorical reinterpretation of 'أولاً' to mean 'the first ones'.
The metaphor becomes necessary to satisfy both terms.
5
Derive the practical ruling
In the 'من' case, if ten enter together, none deserves the reward because no single individual entered first. In the 'كل' case, the first group collectively deserves the reward.
This shows the practical outcome of the usul analysis.

Source Text

Chapter on the General. Explanation of expressions of generality. For comprehensive coverage on the basis of individuality, so each one of the entrants was considered such that none other was with him, and he is first relative to those who lagged behind from the people and did not enter. And if ten entered individually, the reward (nafl) is specifically for the first because he is first from every perspective. But the word 'each' (kull) admits particularity. (Al-Qamar) 'First, because 'first' is a name for a preceding individual, and when associated with 'man' the generality of 'man' is dropped because 'first' is a definitive individual for the preceding individual, and the word 'man' is not definitive in generality, so the absolute probable is carried upon the definitive. Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of how the word 'first' (أولاً) behaves when combined with different particles of generality. The author explains that in the phrase 'Whoever enters this fort first' (من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً), if ten people enter together, none deserves the reward because 'first' linguistically denotes a single preceding individual. The particle 'man' (من) is not strong enough in generality to override this individual meaning; it only indicates generality of the genus (عموم الجنس) not of individuals. However, if the phrase uses 'every/كل' or 'all/الجميع', the meaning of 'first' is extended to include a group—thus 'the first ones' are considered, and the reward goes to the first group. The marginal notes (القمر and السنبلي) provide further reasoning: 'كل' and 'جميع' inherently demand multiplicity, so 'first' must be interpreted metaphorically as the earliest entrant(s). In contrast, 'من' is not definitive (محكمة), so the absolute probable (المطلق المحتمل) is interpreted in light of the definitive (المحكم), preserving the individual meaning of 'أولاً'. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that the strength of a general term can affect the interpretation of other words in the sentence.

Key Terms
أولاً (awwalan): An adverb meaning 'first', linguistically denoting a single preceding individual.
كل (kull): A particle of generality meaning 'every', which demands multiplicity and can extend the meaning of 'first' to a group.
من (man): A relative pronoun meaning 'whoever', but its generality is not on an individual basis; it denotes generality of the genus (عموم الجنس).
الجميع (al-jami'): A term meaning 'all', which like 'كل' demands multiplicity and changes the meaning of 'first' to include a group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The first among them gets the reward.
B) All ten share the reward equally.
C) None of them gets the reward.
D) The reward is given to the last entrant.

Key Takeaway
The strength of a generality particle determines whether it can override the definitive individual meaning of a word like 'first'; only strong particles like 'كل' force a metaphorical extension.

Al-Mutlaq: The Absolute Term in Positive Contexts
Defining mutlaq and distinguishing from 'aam and nakirah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'al-mutlaq' as a Hanafi usul term, distinguish it from 'al-‘aam' and 'al-nakirah', and apply the rule that a nakirah in a positive statement indicates one unspecified individual unrestricted in attributes.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, an indefinite noun in a positive statement indicates one individual, not all — the absolute is specific in number but open in description.

This lesson delves into a subtle but foundational distinction: what does a simple indefinite noun like 'raqabah' (a slave) mean when it appears in a positive command? The Hanafi school holds it signifies one unspecified individual of that kind, unrestricted in its attributes. This differs sharply from the Shafi'i view, which treats such expressions as general ('aam). Understanding this difference is key to deriving rulings from texts like 'fa-tahriru raqabah' (then free a slave). We will explore the definition, the cross-madhhab dispute, and the practical implication for istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'al-mutlaq' in the context of positive statements. Recognize that it is not the philosophically absolute essence but rather an indefinite noun in an affirmative sentence.
The term 'mutlaq' here has a specific technical meaning in usul al-fiqh.
2
Distinguish mutlaq from 'aam
'Aam covers all individuals of a kind without exception. Mutlaq covers exactly one individual but allows any attributes (e.g., color). This distinction is central to Hanafi istinbat.
Example: 'A'tiq raqabah' requires freeing one slave (mutlaq), not all slaves ('aam).
3
Understand the effect of negation
When a nakirah occurs under negation (e.g., 'ma ja'ani rajul'), it becomes 'aam (general). In positive statements, it remains mutlaq.
This is because partial negation does not contradict partial affirmation.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i disagreement
Al-Shafi'i viewed the indefinite noun in positive as indicating the pure essence (al-mahiyyah min haythu hiya hiya), leading to a general interpretation. The Hanafi response is that this is a misconception; the term is restricted to one individual.
The passage states: 'This is what deceived al-Shafi'i.'
5
Apply to an example
Given 'fa-tahriru raqabah' in Quran 5:89, the Hanafi ruling is that one slave must be freed. Any slave suffices, regardless of description.
If the intended meaning were general, it would impose an impossible requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث العام
العام العارضي
ردًّا له على سبيل الإيجاب الجزئي؛ لأن السلب الجزئي لا يناقض الإيجاب الجزئي. وفي الإثبات تخص لكنّها مطلقة أي إذا لم تكن تحت النفي بل كانت في الإثبات، فتكون خاصة لفرد واحد غير معين، لكنّها مطلقة بحسب الأوصاف كما إذا قلت: "أعتق رقبة" يدل على عتق رقبةٍ واحدةٍ محتملة لأوصاف كثيرة بأن يكون سوداء أو بيضاء أو غير ذلك، وإذا قلت: "جاءني رجل" يفهم منه مجيء واحد مبهم مجهول الوصف، وليس المراد بالمطلق ههنا هو الدال على الماهية من غير دلالة على الوحدة والكثرة
على سبيل الإيجاب الجزئي: وهذا بناء على أن تعلق الحكم بفرد معين من الشيء كموسى من البشر تعلق ببعض أفراده، فلا يرد أنه ليس ههنا إيجاب جزئي بل الحكم على فرد خاص، وهو يستلزم الشخصية تدبر . (السنبلي) وفي الإثبات إلخ أي لفظًا ومعنى ليخرج وقوعها في سياق الشرط المثبت، فإنه إثبات لفظًا نفي معنى كما قدمناه . [فتح الغفار: ۱۲۳] (المحشي) غير معين لأن النكرة تدل على فرد و لم يقترن بها ما يوجب العموم. (المحشي) بحسب الأوصاف إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن قول المصنف : "لكنها مطلقة" يدل على أنها ماهية محضة من حيث هي هي فيلزم كونها عامة والعموم ينافي الخصوص، فأجاب بأن المراد بالمطلقة ههنا مطلق بحسب الأوصاف لا نفس الماهية من غير دلالة على الوحدة والكثرة، كما فهمه الشافعي له إلخ، وليعلم أن النكرة الواقعة تحت الإثبات إنما تكون مطلقة إذا وقعت في الإنشاء، وأما إذا وقعت في الإخبار مثل: رأيت رجلاً، فهي لإثبات واحد مبهم من ذلك الجنس غير معلوم التعيين عند السامع، هذا ما في "التلويح" قلت: على هذا المراد بالمطلق ما يدل على نفس الحقيقة من غير تعرض لأمر زائد (السنبلي وليس المراد إلخ للقطع بأن أن تذبحوا بقرة واحدة، وكذا معنى فتحرير رقبة إعتاق رقبة واحدة. (القمر)

ههنا إنما قال: ههنا؛ لأن المطلق كثيرًا ما يطلق في الأصول على ما يدل على الحقيقة من حيث هي هي، قال

بل هي الدالة على الوحدة من غير دلالة على تعيين الأوصاف، وهذا هو الذي غرّ الشافعي
Chapter of the General. The Incidental General. As a response to him by way of partial affirmation, because partial negation does not contradict partial affirmation. In affirmation it specifies, but it is absolute: i.e., if it is not under negation but in affirmation, it becomes specific to a single unspecified individual, but it is absolute with respect to attributes. As when you say: 'Free a slave' it indicates freeing one slave likely possessing many attributes such as being black or white or otherwise. And when you say: 'A man came to me' it implies the coming of one vague individual of unknown description. And the intended meaning of 'absolute' here is not that which denotes the essence without indication of unity and multiplicity. [commentary continues] ... And this is what deceived al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an elucidation of the concept of mutlaq (absolute) in usul al-fiqh. The author explains that an indefinite noun (nakirah) in a positive statement indicates a single individual (fard wahid) but is unrestricted (mutlaq) in terms of attributes. This is distinguished from the philosophically absolute term that denotes the pure essence without any quantification. The author also addresses a subtle point: that the absolute here is not the same as the general ('aam); rather, it is a specific individual open to various descriptions. The discussion includes a response to al-Shafi'i who understood such absolute terms as general. The passage concludes with the statement that this mistaken understanding is what led al-Shafi'i to his position.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): An expression that indicates a single unspecified individual of a kind without restriction to specific attributes.
النكرة (al-nakirah): An indefinite noun that indicates a single unspecified member of a class.
العام (al-'aam): An expression that encompasses all individuals of a kind without limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) All individuals of the kind
B) One unspecified individual of that kind
C) The pure essence without any quantification
D) A specific known individual

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a nakirah in a positive statement signifies one individual of the kind, unlimited in attributes — the absolute is specific in number but open in description.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Indefinite in Affirmative: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Analyzing the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute over the generality of the indefinite in affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi principles (dabitatan) for interpreting the indefinite in affirmative, contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the indefinite, and evaluate the claim that the dispute is verbal (lafzi).

Core Idea
The indefinite in affirmative is not general but absolute; the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute is verbal, not substantive.

This passage engages one of the classic usul debates between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does the indefinite noun in an affirmative command indicate generality (umum) in attributes? The discussion centers on the word 'raqabah' in the Quranic verse on expiation for zihar. By the end, you will understand the Hanafi position based on two key principles and why the disagreement is considered merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
State the debated ruling: whether the indefinite noun 'raqabah' in 'fa-tahriru raqabah' implies generality in attributes (e.g., including all types of slaves) or remains absolute (mutlaq).
The verse is from Surah al-Nisa 4:92 or Surah al-Mujadilah 58:3; OCR indicates (النساء: ٩٢) but context is zihar.
2
State the Shafi'i position and proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that the indefinite in affirmative is general (amm) in a substitutional (badali) sense, covering all individuals that possess the essence, but excluding some by consensus or analogy.
Shafi'is call it 'general' but not comprehensive (istighraqi).
3
State the Hanafi position and proof
Hanafis hold that the indefinite is absolute (mutlaq), not general. They rely on two principles: (1) the absolute proceeds upon its absoluteness; (2) it is directed to the perfect individual.
The first principle applies to attributes (e.g., faith/disbelief), the second to essence (e.g., defects).
4
Analyze the exclusion of zaminah and mudabbara
Hanafis exclude the chronically ill (zaminah) and mudabbara because they lack the genus of benefit or complete ownership, so they fall outside the absolute slave. Shafi'is exclude them by consensus but consider them included in the general term.
This difference leads to the same practical result.
5
Evaluate the verbal dispute
Both schools require only one slave; the dispute is terminological (lafzi) – whether to call it 'general' (Shafi'i) or 'absolute' (Hanafi). The practical ruling is identical.
The text from al-Sanbali confirms the dispute is verbal, as comprehensive inclusion would require freeing all slaves, which neither school holds.
6
Apply the rule for indefinite with a general attribute
If an indefinite is described with a general attribute (e.g., 'a believing slave'), it becomes general according to custom (urf) because the ruling attaches to the cause (illah) derived from the attribute.
Examples: Quranic verses about a believing slave and a kind word.

العام العارضي إن هذا النزاع لفظي؛ إذ لا يقول الشافعي له بتحرير رقبات في الظهار، وإنما يقول: بتحرير رقبة واحدة فقط ونحن ما قلنا: إلا بعموم الأوصاف، فسواء إن سمي هذا إطلاقًا أو عموماً .

عموم الأوصاف

وإن وصفت بصفة عامة تعم، هذا بمنزلة الاستثناء مما سبق كأنه قال: وفي الإثبات تخص، إلا إذا كانت موصوفة بصفة عامة فإنها تعم لكل ما وجدت فيه هذه الصفة، وإن كانت خاصة في إخراج ما عداها، وهذا بحسب العرف والاستعمال، وإلا فمفهوم الصفة هو الخصوص والتقييد بحسب الظاهر ، ولهذا لم تكن عامة إذا كانت تلك الصفة

هذا النزاع إلخ النزاع بين الحنفية والشافعية في أن إطلاق النكرة بحسب الأوصاف في الإثبات عموم أو ليس بعموم، فالحنفية لا تسمونه عمومًا، والشافعية تسمونه عموماً ، نزاع لفظي ما فهم كل فريق ما فهم الآخر، وإلا لا يتصور نزاع، فإن المآل متحد؛ إذ لا يقول إلخ. (القمر) النزاع لفظي إلخ لأن المراد بالرقبة في الآية واحد مبهم، وهو يصدق على كل رقبة على سبيل البدلية، ولعل هذا المعنى هو المراد بالإطلاق عند الشافعي، وهو العموم في الرقبة عنده. وأما العموم بمعنى الشمول الاستغراقي، فلا يوجد في كتب الشافعية لهذه النكرة،

وإلا لزم إعتاق جميع الرقبات في الكفارة، ولا يقول الشافعية بهذا، فثبت أن النزاع لفظي لا معنوي. (السنبلي) هذا بمنزلة إلخ إنما أقحم لفظ "بمنزلة"؛ لأن هذا القول ليس باستثناء ظاهرا، نعم هو بمنزله الاستثناء في الخروج عن الحكم السابق. (القمر) هذا بمنزلة إلخ: دفع دخل تقريره أن هذا الكلام يعارض ما قبله، فإن المفهوم مما قبله أن النكرة تخص تحت الإثبات، وإن وصفت بصفة عامة، وهذا الكلام يخالفه، فقال: هذا بمنزلة الاستثناء. (السنبلي) بمنزلة : لا هو بعينه لعدم حرف الاستثناء (المحشي) عامة أي شاملة للمتعدد غير مختصة بفرد من أفراد الموصوف. (القمر) وإن كانت خاصة إلخ هذا دفع دخل تقريره أن مقتضى الوصف التخصيص والتقييد، سواء كان في النفي أو الإثبات كما هو ظاهر فكيف عمت بالصفة؟ والجواب: أن المراد العموم في الجملة، وذلك لا ينافي الخصوص بوجه ما، فالنكرة الموصوفة خاصة بالنسبة إلى المطلق الذي لا يكون فيه ذلك القيد، وعام بالنسبة إلى شمول أفراده. (السنبلي) وهذا إلخ أي عموم النكرة الموصوفة بحسب العرف، ألا ترى إلى قوله تعالى: وَلَعَبْدٌ مُؤْمِنٌ خَيْرٌ مِنْ مُشْرِك (البقرة: ۲۲۱) وقوله تعالى: ﴿قَوْلٌ مَعْرُوفٌ وَمَغْفِرَةٌ خَيْرٌ مِنْ صَدَقَةٍ يَتْبَعُهَا أَذَى ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٦٣) أي المن والإحسان، فإن هذا الحكم عام لكل عبد مؤمن وكل قول معروف وقس على هذا والسرّ أن الحكم إذا علق على الوصف المشتق ذكر موصوفه أو لا يكون علته مأخذ اشتقاق ذلك الوصف، فحينئذ يعم الحكم بعموم تلك العلة. (القمر)

وإلا : أي وإن لم يكن البناء على العرف (القمر) ولهذا : أي لكون مفهوم الصفة هو الخصوص. (القمر) .
Section on the general (al-amm). The incidental general (al-amm al-aridi). But it is the one indicating singleness without indicating specification of attributes, and this is what? [unclear] The indefinite in affirmation (al-nakirah fi al-ithbat) i.e., the absolute (al-itlaq) in attributes. Al-Shafi'i [concerning] the slave mentioned in zihar; for he says: The word 'slave' in the statement of Allah Most High: 'then the freeing of a slave' is general in his opinion general, and it is the meaning of his statement: And according to al-Shafi'i it is made to include generally until he said with generality (al-Nisa: 92) inclusive of the believing woman, the disbelieving woman, the black, the white, the chronically ill, the insane, the blind, the mudabbara, and others. And from these the chronically ill and mudabbara and similar have been specified by consensus, so I specify the disbelieving woman from them by analogy upon them. And we say: The specification of the chronically ill is not specification; rather it is not included under the absolute slave because it lacks the genus of benefit, and the absolute slave is one that is free from defect, i.e., the perfect slave. And the mudabbara is not owned from one aspect, so the name 'slave' does not apply to it, and the disbelieving woman should not be analogized to the chronically ill in specification. For us in this place are two principles: one is that the absolute proceeds upon its absoluteness, and the second is that the absolute is directed to the perfect individual. The first is in respect of attributes like faith and disbelief, and the second is in respect of essence (dhat) like chronic illness and blindness. The author of al-Talwih said: So the chronically ill and the blind are not sufficient. And with exposure to an unspecified multitude is the general, and to a specified unity is the definite, and to an unspecified unity is the indefinite, and with exposure to a specified multitude are the expressions of number, ponder. (al-Qamar). In his opinion it is general, i.e., in the opinion of al-Shafi'i: the indefinite in affirmation is general (al-Qamar). Zihar i.e., in the expiation of zihar, and it is a Muslim's likening his wife or what is expressed by her like the head and neck, or a common part like half, or a member whose looking is unlawful from the body parts of his mahrams like the thigh and private parts (al-Qamar). And similar like the one with amputated hands and umm walad (al-Qamar). 'Lacks the genus' etc. indicating the defect that does not cause loss of genus, something that loses some benefit that does not prevent freeing in expiation, so freeing a one-eyed person is valid, as in Tanwir al-Absar (al-Qamar). 'Not owned' etc. due to its entitlement to freedom completely (al-Qamar). 'In respect of essence' i.e., the perfect one in respect of essence i.e., the limbs, so the chronically ill, blind, and their likes are excluded (al-Qamar). The incidental general: This dispute is verbal (lafzi); for al-Shafi'i does not say that multiple slaves are to be freed in zihar; rather he says freeing only one slave, and we said nothing except generality of attributes. So it is the same whether this is called 'absolute' or 'general'. Generality of attributes: And if it is described with a general attribute, it applies generally. This is like an exception from what preceded, as if he said: In affirmation it specifies, except if it is described with a general attribute, then it applies generally to everything in which this attribute is found, even if it is specific in excluding others. And this is according to custom and usage; otherwise the implication of the attribute is specification and restriction apparently, and for this it is not general if that attribute. This dispute etc. The dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding whether the absolute of the indefinite in respect of attributes in affirmation is general or not general. The Hanafis do not call it general, and the Shafi'is call it general. A verbal dispute: each group did not understand what the other understood; otherwise no dispute could be conceived, for the outcome is unified; since they do not say... (al-Qamar). The dispute is verbal etc. because what is meant by 'slave' in the verse is one ambiguous, and it applies to every slave on a substitutional basis, and perhaps this meaning is what is meant by 'absolute' according to al-Shafi'i, and it is generality in the slave according to him. As for generality in the sense of comprehensive inclusion (shumul istighraqi), it is not found in the books of the Shafi'is for this indefinite; otherwise it would necessitate freeing all slaves in the expiation, and the Shafi'is do not say this. So it is established that the dispute is verbal not substantive. (al-Sanbali). 'This is like' etc. The word 'like' is inserted because this statement is not literally an exception; rather it is like an exception in departing from the previous ruling. (al-Qamar). 'This is like' etc.: Repelling an objection that this statement contradicts what preceded, for the understood from what preceded is that the indefinite specifies in affirmation, but if described with a general attribute, this statement contradicts it, so he said: This is like an exception. (al-Sanbali). 'Like' is not the same due to the absence of the exception particle. (al-Muhashshi). 'General' i.e., inclusive of multiple not restricted to one individual of the described. (al-Qamar). 'Even if it is specific' etc. This repels an objection that the requirement of the attribute is specification and restriction, whether in negation or affirmation as apparent, so how does it become general with the attribute? The answer: The intended is general in the total sense, and that does not conflict with specification in any way. So the described indefinite is specific relative to the absolute that does not have that restriction, and general relative to the inclusion of its individuals. (al-Sanbali). 'And this' etc. i.e., the generality of the described indefinite is according to custom. Do you not see the statement of Allah Most High: 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist' (al-Baqarah: 221) and His statement: 'A kind word and forgiveness is better than charity followed by injury' (al-Baqarah: 263) i.e., reproach and kindness. For this ruling is general for every believing slave and every kind word. And analogize to this. And the secret is that when a ruling is attached to a derived attribute, whether its described is mentioned or not, its cause is the derivational source of that attribute, then the ruling becomes general due to the generality of that cause. (al-Qamar). 'And otherwise' i.e., if not built upon custom (al-Qamar). 'And for this' i.e., because the implication of the attribute is specificity (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the interpretation of the indefinite noun (nakirah) in affirmative contexts. The discussion centers on the phrase 'raqabah' (slave) in the Quranic verse on slave emancipation. Al-Shafi'i holds that the indefinite in an affirmative statement indicates generality (umum) in terms of attributes, meaning it includes all types of slaves (believer, disbeliever, white, black, etc.) except those excluded by consensus like the chronically ill (zaminah) and the mudabbara (slave whose emancipation is deferred). The Hanafis counter that the indefinite is not general in the sense of comprehensive inclusion; rather, it is absolute (mutlaq), which according to their principles runs on its absoluteness but is directed to the perfect individual (afrad kamil). Therefore, the slave required must be free from defects that negate the genus of benefit (jins al-manfa'ah). The chronically ill and mudabbara are not valid because they lack complete ownership or utility, but the disbelieving slave is valid because she is a full slave. The text introduces two principles (dabitatan): (1) the absolute proceeds upon its absoluteness in attributes, so the attribute of faith/disbelief does not restrict the absolute; (2) the absolute is directed to the perfect individual in essence (dhat), so defects like blindness or chronic illness exclude the slave. The commentator then clarifies that the entire dispute is verbal (lafzi) because both sides agree that only one slave is to be freed in zihar expiation. The Shafi'is call it 'general' in a substitutional sense (badali), not comprehensive inclusion (istighraqi). The Hanafis call it 'absolute' (mutlaq) but the practical outcome is identical. The passage further discusses the case where an indefinite is qualified by a general attribute (sifah 'ammah), such as 'a believing slave' or 'a kind word'. In such cases, the attribute imparts generality according to custom (urf), because the ruling attaches to the cause derived from the attribute. This does not conflict with the general principle that an indefinite in affirmation specifies; it is like an exception. The commentary cites Quranic examples: 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist' (al-Baqarah: 221) and 'A kind word and forgiveness is better than charity followed by injury' (al-Baqarah: 263) to illustrate that the rulings apply generally to all individuals with that attribute.

Key Terms
العام العارضي (al-amm al-aridi): The incidental general; refers to the generality that arises due to the context (e.g., indefinite in affirmation being considered general by some).
النكرة في الإثبات (al-nakirah fi al-ithbat): An indefinite noun that occurs in an affirmative statement; it is the focus of the dispute regarding whether it indicates generality of attributes.
المطلق (al-mutlaq): The absolute; an unrestricted term that does not indicate any attribute beyond its essence; in Hanafi usul, it runs on its absoluteness and is directed to the perfect individual.
الضابطتان (al-dabitatan): The two principles: (1) the absolute proceeds upon its absoluteness; (2) the absolute is directed to the perfect individual.
النزاع لفظي (al-niza' lafzi): A verbal dispute where both parties agree on the practical ruling but disagree on terminology.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is a chronically ill slave (zaminah) not valid for zihar expiation?
A) Because it is excluded by consensus (ijma).
B) Because it lacks the genus of benefit (jins al-manfa'ah), so it is not included under the absolute slave (al-raqabah al-mutlaqah).
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly excludes it.
D) Because it is not a slave (raqabah) at all.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite in affirmative is absolute (mutlaq) for Hanafis, governed by two principles, and the dispute with Shafi'is is verbal because both require only one slave.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Generality of Descriptions ('Umum al-Awsaf) and Its Limits
Explaining the principle of 'umum al-awsaf with its akthari limitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun in an affirmative context is presumptively specific, and identify when it becomes general due to a general description, accounting for the akthari nature of the rule.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun is presumed specific, but a general description makes it general — a majority rule, not absolute.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the default ruling for an indefinite noun (nakira) in an affirmative context is specificity (takhsis) — it refers to a single individual. However, when that indefinite noun is qualified by a general description (sifa 'amma), it becomes general, applying to every entity that possesses that description. This principle, known as 'umum al-awsaf, is essential for deriving rulings from texts. But it is not a universal law; it is an akthari (majority) rule with notable exceptions, as the Quranic verse 'every soul will know' and the saying 'a date is better than a locust' demonstrate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite noun and its context
Determine whether the noun is in an affirmative (ithbat) or negative (nafy) context. The rule primarily applies to affirmative contexts.
2
Check for a general description
If the indefinite noun is qualified by a description (sifa), assess whether that description is general (i.e., applies to many individuals) or specific (e.g., 'who gave birth to me').
A general description triggers 'umum al-awsaf and makes the noun general.
3
Consider the akthari nature of the rule
Remember that the principle 'indefinite + general description = generality' is a majority rule, not universal. There are counterexamples: some indefinite nouns are general without any description (Quranic 'nafs'), and some remain specific despite a description (oath: 'I will marry a Kufan woman').
4
Apply custom and usage
The application of 'umum al-awsaf is subject to 'urf (custom) and the intended meaning of the speaker. In oaths, the intention of the swearer is given weight.
5
Derive the ruling
Based on the above steps, determine whether the indefinite noun yields generality or specificity for the purpose of legal interpretation (e.g., in oaths, contractual language, or Quranic exegesis).

Source Text

عموم الأوصاف
وإن وصفت بصفة عامة تعم، هذا بمنزلة الاستثناء مما سبق كأنه قال: وفي الإثبات تخص، إلا إذا كانت موصوفة بصفة عامة فإنها تعم لكل ما وجدت فيه هذه الصفة، وإن كانت خاصة في إخراج ما عداها، وهذا بحسب العرف والاستعمال، وإلا فمفهوم الصفة هو الخصوص والتقييد بحسب الظاهر ، ولهذا لم تكن عامة إذا كانت تلك الصفة في نفسها خاصة كقولك: "والله لا أضرب إلا رجلاً ولدني"، فإن الوالد لا يكون إلا واحدًا، ولكن هذا الأصل أكثري لا كلي وإلا فقد تعم بدون الصفة كما في قوله: النكرة في الإثبات "تمرة خير من جرادة"، وقوله: ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ﴾ (التكوير: 14) ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ﴾ (الانفطار: 5) وقد تخص بالصفة كما إذا قال: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية بتزوج امرأة واحدة"، ومثل النكرة في الإثبات قولك: "لقيت رجلاً عاما" كقوله : والله لا أكلم أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا، مثال لعموم النكرة الموصوفة، فإن رجلاً كان نكرةً في الإثبات خاصة برجل واحد لو لم يتكلم بقوله: "كوفيا" فيحنث إن تكلم رجلين ولما قال: "كوفيًا" عم جميع رجال الكوفة،
هذا الأصل: أي قولنا كل نكرة في الإثبات تخص إلا إذا كانت موصوفة بصفة عامة. (القمر) هذا الأصل أكثري إلخ: دفع دخل تقرير الدخل: أن هذه القاعدة منقوضة بمثل قول القائل: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية" فإنها خاصة، ولذلك لو تزوج امرأة واحدة يكون باراً، فقال: هذه القاعدة أكثرية لا كلية، وإلا فكان ينبغي أن يكون خاصة متى لم يوصف بصفة، وقد جاء بدون الصفة أيضًا عامًا كما في قوله تمرة خير من جرادة.(السنبلي)
تمرة خير من جرادة: قاله عمر : في صدقة قتل المحرم جرادة كذا في "ذخيرة العقبى".(القمر)
عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما أحضرت من خير و شر.(القمر) عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ: أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما قدمت في الدنيا من خير وشر، والتعبير بالماضي لتيقن الوقوع . (القمر) بتزوج إلخ : أي يكون بارًا بتزوج امرأة واحدة كوفية كذا في "كشف البزدوي"، فلو كانت النكرة مفيدة للعموم لا يكون بارا إلا بتزوج جميع نساء الكوفة (القمر)
ومثل قولك إلخ وكذا إذا قال: والله ما كلمت أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا فالنكرة وإن وصفت بصفة عامة لكنه يكون بارا لو كان كلم رجلاً واحدًا من الكوفة؛ لتعذر العمل بالعموم بالمعنى الخارجي، وهو لزوم الكذب للعلم الحاصل يقينًا أنه كلم جميع رجال الكوفة. (القمر) لا أكلم أحدًا أي لا رجلاً كوفيا ولا رجلاً بصريا، ولا مدنيًا، ولا مكياً، ولا غيره إلا رجلاً كوفيا. (القمر) عم جميع إلخ لأن ما هو المستثنى هو بعينه كان واقعا في سياق النفي وعامًا، فيبقى عمومه بعد الاستثناء أيضًا للعينية وإن انتقض النفي، بخلاف "والله لا أكلم أحدا إلا رجلاً" بلا ذكر الوصف، فإنه لا عموم ههنا لعدم دخول ما هو المستثنى ههنا بعينه تحت الصدر حتى لو قدر المستثنى منه هكذا "لا أكلم رجلاً ولا امرأة ولا صبيًا إلا رجلاً، فحينئذ تعم النكرة ،البتة، والمرجع إلى بيان الحالف كذا قيل. (القمر)
Generality of descriptions: If [the indefinite noun] is described with a general description, it becomes general. This is like an exception to what preceded, as if it said: In affirmative [contexts], it is specific, except when it is described with a general description, then it becomes general for everything in which this description is found, even though it is specific in excluding what is other than it. And this is based on custom and usage; otherwise, the implication of the description is particularization and restriction in apparent meaning. Thus it is not general when that description is in itself specific, like your saying: 'By Allah, I will not strike except a man who gave birth to me' – for the parent is only one. But this principle is majority, not universal; otherwise it would be general without description as in his saying: 'a date is better than a locust', and His saying: 'Every soul will know what it has brought' (81:14) 'Every soul will know what it has sent forth' (82:5). And it may be particularized by the description, as when he said: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman' – he becomes virtuous by marrying one woman. And like your saying: 'I met a man generally' [example omitted]. His saying: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man' – example of generality of the described indefinite noun. For 'man' was an indefinite noun in affirmative context specific to one man had he not said 'Kufan'; he would break the oath if he spoke to two men. But when he said 'Kufan,' it became general to all men of Kufa.
This principle: i.e. our saying: every indefinite noun in affirmative context is specific unless it is described with a general description. (al-Qamar): This principle is majority etc. – a rebuttal of an objection. The objection is that this rule is contradicted by the example of one who says: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman' – it is specific, therefore if he marries one woman he is virtuous. So he said: this rule is majority, not universal; otherwise it would be that it is specific whenever it is not described with a description, but it has come without description also general as in 'a date is better than a locust.' (al-Sanbali)
'A date is better than a locust': 'Umar said concerning charity for a pilgrim killing a locust – this is found in Dhakhirat al-‘Uqba. (al-Qamar)
'Every soul will know what it has brought' means every soul will know on the Day of Judgment what good and evil it has brought. (al-Qamar) 'Every soul will know what it has sent forth' – means every soul will know on the Day of Judgment what good and evil it sent forth in the world, and the expression in past tense is for certainty of occurrence. (al-Qamar) 'He marries' etc. – i.e. he is virtuous by marrying one Kufan woman – this is in Kashf al-Bazdawi. If the indefinite noun conveyed generality, he would not be virtuous except by marrying all Kufan women. (al-Qamar)
And like your saying etc. – similarly if he said: 'By Allah, I have not spoken to anyone except a Kufan man,' then the indefinite noun [in the exception] even if described with a general description, he would be virtuous if he spoke to one man from Kufa, because acting on the external generality is impossible – it would necessitate lying, since it is known with certainty that he did not speak to all Kufan men. (al-Qamar) 'I will not speak to anyone' means neither a Kufan man nor a Basran, nor a Medinan, nor a Meccan, nor any other except a Kufan man. (al-Qamar) 'It became general to all' etc. – because what is excepted is exactly what fell in the context of negation and was general; its generality remains after exclusion due to identity [of the excepted item], even though the negation is revoked. Unlike 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a man' without mentioning the description, for there is no generality here because the excepted item does not fall under the first part [of the statement]; even if one were to estimate the subject of exclusion as 'I will not speak to a man, nor a woman, nor a child except a man,' then the indefinite noun becomes general absolutely, and the reference is to the intention of the swearer – thus it is said. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage elaborates the principle of 'umum al-awsaf (generality of descriptions) and its limits. The matn explains that an indefinite noun in an affirmative context is normally specific (takhsis), but when qualified by a general description, it becomes general, applying to every individual possessing that description. However, this is subject to custom and usage. The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sanbali provides important nuances: (1) The rule that 'every indefinite noun in affirmative is specific unless described by a general description' is akthari (majority) not kulli (universal). There are exceptions: some indefinite nouns are general without any description (e.g., 'a date is better than a locust', which implies all dates and all locusts) and some are specific despite being described (e.g., 'I will marry a Kufan woman' where the oath is fulfilled by one woman). (2) In the context of oaths, when an exception is made from a negative general statement, the excepted indefinite noun may retain its generality if the exception is identical to what was negated; otherwise it remains specific. The examples illustrate these rules. The passage emphasizes that the principle is based on 'urf (custom) and that one must consider the intended meaning of the speaker.

Key Terms
عموم الأوصاف (‘umūm al-awṣāf): The generality that results from describing an indefinite noun with a general description, making it apply to all individuals with that description.
أصلي أكثري (aṣlī aktharī): A rule that is majority (applies in most cases) but not universal (there are exceptions).
النكرة في الإثبات (al-nakira fī al-ithbāt): An indefinite noun used in a positive statement, normally specific to a single indefinite referent unless there is a reason for generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example from the passage that shows an indefinite noun in an affirmative context being general without a description?
A) 'Wallahi la adribu illa rajulan waladani' (والله لا أضرب إلا رجلاً ولدني)
B) 'Tamratun khayrun min jaradatin' (تمرة خير من جرادة)
C) 'Wallahi la atazawwajanna imra'atan kufiyya' (والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية)
D) 'Wallahi la aklimu ahadan illa rajulan kufiyyan' (والله لا أكلم أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of 'umum al-awsaf is a majority rule: an indefinite noun in an affirmative context is typically specific unless described by a general description, but exceptions exist and custom/intention must be considered.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
7 min
Nakirah in Affirmative: The Core Rule and Its Akthari Nature
Explaining the rule of nakirah in affirmative context and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts, identify its exceptions, and apply it to simple examples.

Core Idea
A nakirah in an affirmative sentence is specific unless described by a general attribute.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding how an indefinite noun (nakirah) functions in an affirmative statement is crucial for interpreting legal texts and oaths. The default rule states that it indicates specificity, but this rule is only dominant (akthari), not absolute. This lesson unpacks the rule, its exceptions, and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle as given in the text: every indefinite in an affirmative sentence is specific (takhassus) unless qualified by a general attribute.
This is the foundational claim from the Hanafi school.
2
Recognize the exceptions
Notice that there are cases where a nakirah without any attribute is general (e.g., 'A date is better than a locust' and Quranic verses). This shows the rule is akthari (majority) not kulli (universal).
The text explicitly calls these exceptions as evidence for the akthari nature.
3
Understand the qualification
When a nakirah is qualified by a general attribute (e.g., 'Kufan'), it becomes general (amm). This is the main part of the rule.
Contrast with qualification by a specific attribute (e.g., 'this date') which keeps it specific.
4
Apply to oaths
See how this rule affects the interpretation of conditional oaths. For instance, 'I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man' makes the excepted term general, so speaking to one Kufan man does not break the oath, but speaking to two does.
The text provides detailed analysis of this oath.
5
Reflect on akthari
Grasp why the rule is described as majority: because of the existence of general nakirah without attribute, the rule cannot be universal, yet it holds in most cases.
This epistemological nuance is important for legal reasoning.

مبحث العام
العام العارضي
في نفسها خاصة كقولك: "والله لا أضرب إلا رجلاً ولدني"، فإن الوالد لا يكون إلا واحدًا، ولكن هذا الأصل أكثري لا كلي وإلا فقد تعم بدون الصفة كما في قوله: النكرة في الإثبات "تمرة خير من جرادة"، وقوله: ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ﴾ ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ﴾ وقد تخص بالصفة كما إذا قال: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية" بتزوج امرأة واحدة، ومثل النكرة في الإثبات قولك: "لقيت رجلاً عاما" كقوله : والله لا أكلم أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا، مثال لعموم النكرة الموصوفة، فإن رجلاً كان نكرةً في الإثبات خاصة برجل واحد لو لم يتكلم بقوله: "كوفيا" فيحنث إن تكلم رجلين ولما قال: "كوفيًا" عم جميع رجال الكوفة، هذا الأصل: أي قولنا كل نكرة في الإثبات تخص إلا إذا كانت موصوفة بصفة عامة. (القمر) هذا الأصل أكثري إلخ: دفع دخل تقرير الدخل: أن هذه القاعدة منقوضة بمثل قول القائل: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية" فإنها خاصة، ولذلك لو تزوج امرأة واحدة يكون باراً، فقال: هذه القاعدة أكثرية لا كلية، وإلا فكان ينبغي أن يكون خاصة متى لم يوصف بصفة، وقد جاء بدون الصفة أيضًا عامًا كما في قوله تمرة خير من جرادة.(السنبلي) تمرة خير من جرادة: قاله عمر : في صدقة قتل المحرم جرادة كذا في "ذخيرة العقبى".(القمر) عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما أحضرت من خير و شر.(القمر) عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ: أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما قدمت في الدنيا من خير وشر، والتعبير بالماضي لتيقن الوقوع . (القمر) بتزوج إلخ : أي يكون بارًا بتزوج امرأة واحدة كوفية كذا في "كشف البزدوي"، فلو كانت النكرة مفيدة للعموم لا يكون بارا إلا بتزوج جميع نساء الكوفة (القمر) ومثل قولك إلخ كذا إذا قال: والله ما كلمت أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا فالنكرة وإن وصفت بصفة عامة لكنه يكون بارا لو كان كلم رجلاً واحدًا من الكوفة؛ لتعذر العمل بالعموم بالمعنى الخارجي، وهو لزوم الكذب للعلم الحاصل يقينًا أنه كلم جميع رجال الكوفة. (القمر) لا أكلم أحدًا أي لا رجلاً كوفيا ولا رجلاً بصريا، ولا مدنيًا، ولا مكياً، ولا غيره إلا رجلاً كوفيا. (القمر) عم جميع إلخ لأن ما هو المستثنى هو بعينه كان واقعا في سياق النفي وعامًا، فيبقى عمومه بعد الاستثناء أيضًا للعينية وإن انتقض النفي، بخلاف "والله لا أكلم أحدا إلا رجلاً" بلا ذكر الوصف، فإنه لا عموم ههنا لعدم دخول ما هو المستثنى ههنا بعينه تحت الصدر حتى لو قدر المستثنى منه هكذا "لا أكلم رجلاً ولا امرأة ولا صبيًا إلا رجلاً، فحينئذ تعم النكرة ،البتة، والمرجع إلى بيان الحالف كذا قيل. (القمر)

مبحث العام
العام العارضي
فلا يحنث بتكلم كل من كان من رجال الكوفة، وقوله: "والله لا أقربكما إلا يوما أقربكما فيه "، مثال ثانٍ لعموم النكرة الموصوفة، وهو خطاب لامرأتيه، فإن قوله: "يوما" نكرة موضوعة ليوم واحد، فلو لم يصفه بقوله : أقربكما فيه لكان موليا بعد قربان يوم واحد؛ لأن هذا إيلاء مؤبد وليس مؤقتًا بأربعة أشهر حتى تنقص الأشهر الأربعة بيوم ولما وصفه بقوله: "أقربكما فيه" لم يكن موليًا أبدًا؛ لأن كل يوم يقربهما فيه يكون مستثنى من اليمين لهذه الصفة العامة فلا يحنث به. أي أقربكما فيه أي بقربان كل يوم وكذا إذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر ، فضربوه فإنهم يعتقون، مثال ثالث لكون النكرة عامة بعموم الوصف على سبيل التشبيه للقاعدة، فإن قوله: "أي عبيدي" ليس بنكرة نحوية؛ لكونه مضافًا إلى المعرفة، ولكن يشبه النكرة في الإبهام وصف بصفة عامة، وهو قوله: "ضربك" فيعم بعموم الصفة، فيعتق كل منهم إن ضربوا المخاطب جملة مجتمعين أو متفرقين، بخلاف ما إذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر" بإضافة الضرب إلى المخاطب، وجعل العبيد مضروبين، فإنهم لا يعتقون كلهم إذا ضرب المخاطب جميعهم، بل إن ضربهم فلا يحنث إلخ سواء تكلم معا أو متفرقًا. (القمر) لكان موليا : الإيلاء لغة اليمين، وشرعا: الحلف على ترك قربان الزوجة بالله أو بالطلاق أو العتاق أو غيرهما مطلقًا أو مؤقتاً بوقت وأقله للحرة أربعة أشهر، وللأمة شهران، ولا حد للأكثر ، ولا إيلاء لو حلف على ترك القربان أقل من ذلك، وحكمه: وقوع طلقة بائنة إن بر فلم يطأ، والكفارة أي في الحلف بالله والجزاء أي في الحلف بغير الله وهو المعلق إن حنث بالقربان.(القمر) هذا دفع وهم يوهم أن تنقيص يوم من أيامه المقررة يبطله، وهي أربعة أشهر. (المحشي) على سبيل التشبيه إلخ : أي ليس مثالاً حقيقيًا بل هو بمنزلة المثال للقاعدة الكلية، وهي: أن كل نكرة موصوفة بصفة عامة تعم في الإثبات فإن إلخ. (القمر) ليس بنكرة نحوية إلخ قيل: إن كلمة "أي" تبقى نكرة وإن أضيفت إلى المعرفة؛ لأنه أريد بها بعض غير معين تدبر . (القمر)
Chapter on the General: The Incidental General. In itself it is specific, like your saying: 'By Allah, I will not strike except a man who gave birth to me,' for the father can only be one. But this principle is majority, not universal, otherwise it would be general without the attribute, as in the statement: 'A date is better than a locust,' and His statement: 'Every soul knows what it has brought forth,' 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead.' And it may be specified by an attribute, as if he said: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman,' he is obedient by marrying one woman. And similar to the indefinite in affirmative is your saying: 'I met a man generally' like his saying: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man,' an example of the generality of the qualified indefinite. For 'a man' was an indefinite in affirmation specific to one man if he had not said 'Kufan,' then he would break his oath if he spoke to two men, but when he said 'Kufan,' it became general to all men of Kufa. This is the principle: our saying every indefinite in affirmation is specific unless it is qualified by a general attribute. (Al-Qamar) 'This principle is majority' etc.: Refuting an objection. Explanation of the objection: That this rule is refuted by such as the sayer's saying: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman,' for it is specific, therefore if he marries one woman he is obedient. So he said: This rule is majority, not universal, otherwise it would be that it is specific whenever it is not qualified by an attribute, and it has come without an attribute also general, as in his saying: 'A date is better than a locust.' (Al-Sanbali) 'A date is better than a locust': 'Umar said it concerning the charity of the pilgrim killing a locust, as in 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba.' (Al-Qamar) 'Every soul knows what it has brought forth': i.e., every soul on the Day of Judgment knows what good and evil it has brought. (Al-Qamar) 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead': i.e., every soul on the Day of Judgment knows what good and evil it has sent ahead in the world, and the use of the past tense is for certainty of occurrence. (Al-Qamar) 'By marrying' etc.: i.e., he is obedient by marrying one Kufan woman, as in 'Kashf al-Bazdawi'; so if the indefinite were beneficial for generality, he would not be obedient except by marrying all the women of Kufa. (Al-Qamar) And similar to your saying etc. Likewise if he says: 'By Allah, I have not spoken to anyone except a Kufan man,' then the indefinite, although qualified by a general attribute, he is obedient if he spoke to one man from Kufa, because acting on generality is impossible in the external sense, which is the necessity of lying due to certain knowledge that he has spoken to all men of Kufa. (Al-Qamar) 'I will not speak to anyone' i.e., neither a Kufan man nor a Basran man, nor a Madinan, nor a Meccan, nor other than a Kufan man. (Al-Qamar) 'Became general to all' etc. because that which is excepted is itself that which was in a context of negation and general, so its generality remains after the exception also due to the identity, even though the negation is broken, unlike 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a man' without mentioning the attribute, for there is no generality here due to the lack of the excepted thing itself being under the first part; even if one estimates the excepted-from as such: 'I will not speak to a man, nor a woman, nor a boy, except a man,' then the indefinite becomes general absolutely, and the reference is to the intention of the swearer, as it is said. (Al-Qamar) So he does not break his oath by speaking to everyone who is from the men of Kufa. And His saying: 'By Allah, I will not approach you two except on a day on which I approach you in it,' a second example of the generality of the qualified indefinite, and it is an address to his two wives. For his saying 'a day' is an indefinite placed for one day; so if he had not qualified it by his saying 'I approach you in it,' he would be making an oath of abstinence after approaching one day, because this is a perpetual oath of abstinence, not limited to four months such that the four months are decreased by one day. And when he qualified it by his saying 'I approach you in it,' he is not a Mu'li ever, because every day in which he approaches them is excepted from the oath due to this general attribute, so he does not break his oath by it. 'I approach you in it' i.e., by approaching every day. And likewise if he says: 'Any of my slaves who hits you, he is free,' and they hit him, then they are all freed, a third example of the indefinite being general by the generality of the attribute by way of analogy for the rule. For his saying 'any of my slaves' is not a grammatical indefinite because it is governor to a definite, but it resembles the indefinite in ambiguity, qualified by a general attribute, which is his saying 'hits you,' so it becomes general by the generality of the attribute, so each of them is freed if they hit the addressee collectively, whether together or separately, unlike if he said: 'Any of my slaves whom I hit, he is free,' making the hitting directed to the addressee and the slaves hit, then they are not all freed if the addressee hits all of them; rather if he hits them... etc. (Al-Qamar) 'He would be a Mu'li': Ila' linguistically is an oath, and legally: swearing to abandon approaching the wife by Allah or by divorce or manumission or other, absolutely or temporarily with a minimum for a free woman of four months and for a slave woman two months, and no maximum, and there is no Ila' if he swears to abandon approaching for less than that. Its legal effect: a final divorce occurs if he persists and does not have intercourse, and expiation if by Allah, and penalty if by other than Allah, i.e., suspended consequences, if he breaks the oath by approaching. (Al-Qamar) This refutes a misunderstanding that a decrease of one day from its established days invalidates it, which are four months. (Al-Muhashshi) 'By way of analogy' etc.: i.e., it is not a real example but is like an example for the universal rule: that every indefinite qualified by a general attribute becomes general in affirmative. (Al-Qamar) 'It is not a grammatical indefinite' etc.: It is said that the word 'ayy' remains indefinite even if added to a definite because it intends some unspecified thing, consider. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar discusses the rule of the indefinite noun (nakirah) in affirmative contexts. The author states that according to the established principle (asl), every indefinite in an affirmative sentence is specific (takhassus) unless it is qualified by a general attribute. However, he immediately notes that this rule is a majority rule (akthari) and not universal (kulli), because there are exceptions where an indefinite without any attribute is general, as in the examples 'a date is better than a locust' and the Quranic verses 'Every soul knows what it has brought forth' (Surah al-Infitar, 5?) and 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead' (Surah al-Takwir, 14?). In these, the indefinite 'a date' and 'every soul' are general despite lacking a qualifying attribute. Conversely, an indefinite can be specified by an attribute, as in 'I will marry a Kufan woman,' which applies to one Kufan woman.

The passage then elaborates on the case where an indefinite is qualified by a general attribute, using the example of an oath: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man.' Here, 'a man' is initially specific, but when qualified by 'Kufan' (a general attribute covering all Kufan men), it becomes general. The commentator (Al-Qamar) explains that if the attribute is general, the indefinite becomes general, so the swearer would be oath-breaker if he speaks to two Kufan men, but if he speaks to one, he is obedient because acting on the external generality would lead to certain disbelief. He also contrasts this with the plain exception 'except a man' without attribute, which remains specific.

Two more examples illustrate the rule. In the second example, a man swears to his two wives: 'By Allah, I will not approach you except on a day on which I approach you.' The indefinite 'a day' qualified by the relative clause 'I approach you in it' becomes general, so every day he approaches them is excepted from the oath, and he never becomes a Mu'li (one who swears to abstain). The third example uses the word 'ayy' which, though grammatically definite, functions like an indefinite due to ambiguity. If a master says: 'Any of my slaves who hits you, he is free,' and all slaves hit the addressee, all become free. This is because the attribute 'hits you' is general. But if he says: 'Any of my slaves whom I hit, he is free,' the attribute is specific to the master's action, so only the one he hits is freed.

The passage concludes with clarifications on Ila' (oath of abstinence) and the grammatical status of 'ayy'.

Key Terms
النكرة (an-nakirah): اسم غير معين يدل على شائع في جنسه
الصفة العامة (as-sifah al-ammah): وصف يشمل أفراداً كثيرة (مثلاً: كوفي)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, what is the default ruling for an indefinite noun (nakirah) in an affirmative sentence?
A) It is always general.
B) It is specific unless qualified by a general attribute.
C) It is always specific.
D) It is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The rule of nakirah in affirmative is a dominant (akthari) principle: specific by default, general when qualified by a general attribute.

Applications of Nakirah in Affirmative: Oaths and Legal Examples
Explaining the rule of nakirah in affirmative context and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of nakirah in affirmative to complex oath scenarios and distinguish between general and specific attributes.

Core Idea
Qualifying a nakirah with a general attribute transforms it into a general term, affecting oath obligations and legal consequences.

Building on the foundational rule, this lesson explores its application in three detailed examples from the text: oaths involving 'except a Kufan man', oaths of abstinence (Ila'), and the ambiguous word 'ayy' in manumission. These cases illustrate how the qualification of a nakirah determines legal outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Example 1: Oath with 'except a Kufan man'
Analyze how the attribute 'Kufan' makes the excepted nakirah general. The swearer is only obedient if he speaks to one Kufan man, but breaks the oath if he speaks to two, because the general attribute covers all Kufan men.
Contrast with the case without attribute: 'except a man' remains specific.
2
Example 2: 'except on a day on which I approach you'
See how the relative clause 'I approach you in it' acts as a general attribute for 'a day', preventing Ila'. Every day he approaches is excepted.
Understand the concept of Ila' and how the rule affects it.
3
Example 3: 'Any of my slaves who hits you'
Treat 'ayy' as functionally similar to a nakirah despite being grammatically definite. The attribute 'hits you' is general, so all slaves who hit are freed.
This is an analogy (tashbih) for the rule.
4
Contrast with specific attribute
Examine the counterexample 'any of my slaves whom I hit' where the attribute is specific to the master's action, keeping it specific.
Only the slave the master actually hits is freed.
5
Generalize across examples
Conclude that the rule consistently determines whether an indefinite becomes general: general attribute → general; specific attribute → specific.
The same principle applies in all three examples.

العام العارضي
في نفسها خاصة كقولك: "والله لا أضرب إلا رجلاً ولدني"، فإن الوالد لا يكون إلا واحدًا، ولكن هذا الأصل أكثري لا كلي وإلا فقد تعم بدون الصفة كما في قوله: النكرة في الإثبات "تمرة خير من جرادة"، وقوله: ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ﴾ ﴿عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ﴾ وقد تخص بالصفة كما إذا قال: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية" بتزوج امرأة واحدة، ومثل النكرة في الإثبات قولك: "لقيت رجلاً عاما" كقوله : والله لا أكلم أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا، مثال لعموم النكرة الموصوفة، فإن رجلاً كان نكرةً في الإثبات خاصة برجل واحد لو لم يتكلم بقوله: "كوفيا" فيحنث إن تكلم رجلين ولما قال: "كوفيًا" عم جميع رجال الكوفة، هذا الأصل: أي قولنا كل نكرة في الإثبات تخص إلا إذا كانت موصوفة بصفة عامة. (القمر) هذا الأصل أكثري إلخ: دفع دخل تقرير الدخل: أن هذه القاعدة منقوضة بمثل قول القائل: "والله لأتزوجن امرأة كوفية" فإنها خاصة، ولذلك لو تزوج امرأة واحدة يكون باراً، فقال: هذه القاعدة أكثرية لا كلية، وإلا فكان ينبغي أن يكون خاصة متى لم يوصف بصفة، وقد جاء بدون الصفة أيضًا عامًا كما في قوله تمرة خير من جرادة.(السنبلي) تمرة خير من جرادة: قاله عمر : في صدقة قتل المحرم جرادة كذا في "ذخيرة العقبى".(القمر) عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا أَحْضَرَتْ أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما أحضرت من خير و شر.(القمر) عَلِمَتْ نَفْسٌ مَا قَدَّمَتْ: أي تعلم كل نفس يوم القيمة ما قدمت في الدنيا من خير وشر، والتعبير بالماضي لتيقن الوقوع . (القمر) بتزوج إلخ : أي يكون بارًا بتزوج امرأة واحدة كوفية كذا في "كشف البزدوي"، فلو كانت النكرة مفيدة للعموم لا يكون بارا إلا بتزوج جميع نساء الكوفة (القمر) ومثل قولك إلخ كذا إذا قال: والله ما كلمت أحدًا إلا رجلاً كوفيا فالنكرة وإن وصفت بصفة عامة لكنه يكون بارا لو كان كلم رجلاً واحدًا من الكوفة؛ لتعذر العمل بالعموم بالمعنى الخارجي، وهو لزوم الكذب للعلم الحاصل يقينًا أنه كلم جميع رجال الكوفة. (القمر) لا أكلم أحدًا أي لا رجلاً كوفيا ولا رجلاً بصريا، ولا مدنيًا، ولا مكياً، ولا غيره إلا رجلاً كوفيا. (القمر) عم جميع إلخ لأن ما هو المستثنى هو بعينه كان واقعا في سياق النفي وعامًا، فيبقى عمومه بعد الاستثناء أيضًا للعينية وإن انتقض النفي، بخلاف "والله لا أكلم أحدا إلا رجلاً" بلا ذكر الوصف، فإنه لا عموم ههنا لعدم دخول ما هو المستثنى ههنا بعينه تحت الصدر حتى لو قدر المستثنى منه هكذا "لا أكلم رجلاً ولا امرأة ولا صبيًا إلا رجلاً، فحينئذ تعم النكرة ،البتة، والمرجع إلى بيان الحالف كذا قيل. (القمر)

فلا يحنث بتكلم كل من كان من رجال الكوفة، وقوله: "والله لا أقربكما إلا يوما أقربكما فيه "، مثال ثانٍ لعموم النكرة الموصوفة، وهو خطاب لامرأتيه، فإن قوله: "يوما" نكرة موضوعة ليوم واحد، فلو لم يصفه بقوله : أقربكما فيه لكان موليا بعد قربان يوم واحد؛ لأن هذا إيلاء مؤبد وليس مؤقتًا بأربعة أشهر حتى تنقص الأشهر الأربعة بيوم ولما وصفه بقوله: "أقربكما فيه" لم يكن موليًا أبدًا؛ لأن كل يوم يقربهما فيه يكون مستثنى من اليمين لهذه الصفة العامة فلا يحنث به. أي أقربكما فيه أي بقربان كل يوم وكذا إذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر ، فضربوه فإنهم يعتقون، مثال ثالث لكون النكرة عامة بعموم الوصف على سبيل التشبيه للقاعدة، فإن قوله: "أي عبيدي" ليس بنكرة نحوية؛ لكونه مضافًا إلى المعرفة، ولكن يشبه النكرة في الإبهام وصف بصفة عامة، وهو قوله: "ضربك" فيعم بعموم الصفة، فيعتق كل منهم إن ضربوا المخاطب جملة مجتمعين أو متفرقين، بخلاف ما إذا قال: "أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر" بإضافة الضرب إلى المخاطب، وجعل العبيد مضروبين، فإنهم لا يعتقون كلهم إذا ضرب المخاطب جميعهم، بل إن ضربهم فلا يحنث إلخ سواء تكلم معا أو متفرقًا. (القمر) لكان موليا : الإيلاء لغة اليمين، وشرعا: الحلف على ترك قربان الزوجة بالله أو بالطلاق أو العتاق أو غيرهما مطلقًا أو مؤقتاً بوقت وأقله للحرة أربعة أشهر، وللأمة شهران، ولا حد للأكثر ، ولا إيلاء لو حلف على ترك القربان أقل من ذلك، وحكمه: وقوع طلقة بائنة إن بر فلم يطأ، والكفارة أي في الحلف بالله والجزاء أي في الحلف بغير الله وهو المعلق إن حنث بالقربان.(القمر) هذا دفع وهم يوهم أن تنقيص يوم من أيامه المقررة يبطله، وهي أربعة أشهر. (المحشي) على سبيل التشبيه إلخ : أي ليس مثالاً حقيقيًا بل هو بمنزلة المثال للقاعدة الكلية، وهي: أن كل نكرة موصوفة بصفة عامة تعم في الإثبات فإن إلخ. (القمر) ليس بنكرة نحوية إلخ قيل: إن كلمة "أي" تبقى نكرة وإن أضيفت إلى المعرفة؛ لأنه أريد بها بعض غير معين تدبر . (القمر)
Chapter on the General: The Incidental General. In itself it is specific, like your saying: 'By Allah, I will not strike except a man who gave birth to me,' for the father can only be one. But this principle is majority, not universal, otherwise it would be general without the attribute, as in the statement: 'A date is better than a locust,' and His statement: 'Every soul knows what it has brought forth,' 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead.' And it may be specified by an attribute, as if he said: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman,' he is obedient by marrying one woman. And similar to the indefinite in affirmative is your saying: 'I met a man generally' like his saying: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man,' an example of the generality of the qualified indefinite. For 'a man' was an indefinite in affirmation specific to one man if he had not said 'Kufan,' then he would break his oath if he spoke to two men, but when he said 'Kufan,' it became general to all men of Kufa. This is the principle: our saying every indefinite in affirmation is specific unless it is qualified by a general attribute. (Al-Qamar) 'This principle is majority' etc.: Refuting an objection. Explanation of the objection: That this rule is refuted by such as the sayer's saying: 'By Allah, I will marry a Kufan woman,' for it is specific, therefore if he marries one woman he is obedient. So he said: This rule is majority, not universal, otherwise it would be that it is specific whenever it is not qualified by an attribute, and it has come without an attribute also general, as in his saying: 'A date is better than a locust.' (Al-Sanbali) 'A date is better than a locust': 'Umar said it concerning the charity of the pilgrim killing a locust, as in 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba.' (Al-Qamar) 'Every soul knows what it has brought forth': i.e., every soul on the Day of Judgment knows what good and evil it has brought. (Al-Qamar) 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead': i.e., every soul on the Day of Judgment knows what good and evil it has sent ahead in the world, and the use of the past tense is for certainty of occurrence. (Al-Qamar) 'By marrying' etc.: i.e., he is obedient by marrying one Kufan woman, as in 'Kashf al-Bazdawi'; so if the indefinite were beneficial for generality, he would not be obedient except by marrying all the women of Kufa. Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar discusses the rule of the indefinite noun (nakirah) in affirmative contexts. The author states that according to the established principle (asl), every indefinite in an affirmative sentence is specific (takhassus) unless it is qualified by a general attribute. In these, the indefinite 'a date' and 'every soul' are general despite lacking a qualifying attribute. Conversely, an indefinite can be specified by an attribute, as in 'I will marry a Kufan woman,' which applies to one Kufan woman.

The passage then elaborates on the case where an indefinite is qualified by a general attribute, using the example of an oath: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man.' Here, 'a man' is initially specific, but when qualified by 'Kufan' (a general attribute covering all Kufan men), it becomes general. The commentator (Al-Qamar) explains that if the attribute is general, the indefinite becomes general, so the swearer would be oath-breaker if he speaks to two Kufan men, but if he speaks to one, he is obedient because acting on the external generality would lead to certain disbelief. He also contrasts this with the plain exception 'except a man' without attribute, which remains specific.

Two more examples illustrate the rule. In the second example, a man swears to his two wives: 'By Allah, I will not approach you except on a day on which I approach you.' The indefinite 'a day' qualified by the relative clause 'I approach you in it' becomes general, so every day he approaches them is excepted from the oath, and he never becomes a Mu'li (one who swears to abstain). But if he says: 'Any of my slaves whom I hit, he is free,' the attribute is specific to the master's action, so only the one he hits is freed.

The passage concludes with clarifications on Ila' (oath of abstinence) and the grammatical status of 'ayy'.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
