Generality of Majaz: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Defines majaz and disputes its ability to convey general meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis hold that majaz can be general and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Generality of majaz depends on linguistic indicators, not on being haqiqah.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: can a word used metaphorically (majaz) indicate a general meaning? The Hanafi school says yes, provided the same indicators of generality (like alif lam, nafi context, plural forms) are present. The Shafi'i school argues that majaz is a necessity used only when haqiqah is impossible, so it must be restricted to the specific context. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to interpret legal texts and appreciate the role of linguistic principles in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Articulate the Hanafi view that majaz can be general like haqiqah if indicators of generality are present.
The text says: 'majaz is like haqiqah in being specific or general.'
2
Identify the opposing view
Restate the Shafi'i argument that majaz cannot be general because it is a necessity limited to specific need.
Al-Shafi'i is quoted: 'No generality for majaz because it is a necessity.'
3
Analyse the Shafi'i proof
Understand the reasoning: necessity is measured by its extent, so it cannot extend to all individuals of a category.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: generality comes from additional indicators (alif lam, nafi, plural), not from being haqiqah; these can appear in majaz.
Also, the frequent use of majaz in the Quran refutes the necessity argument.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, when a metaphorical word like 'asab' (tribe) is used, Hanafis take it as general, Shafi'is as specific.
This difference can affect rulings in many chapters of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

267 Definition of Majaz and Its Ruling. Explanation of the divisions of the third division so that the two definitions are not refuted by inclusion and exclusion. For the word salat in language is for supplication, and in law it is for the known pillars. So from the linguistic perspective it is haqiqah in supplication, because it is true that it is what it is placed for, and majaz in the pillars, because it is other than what it is placed for in general. From the legal perspective it is haqiqah in the pillars, because they are what it is placed for, and majaz in supplication, because it is other than what it is placed for in general. Its ruling: the existence of what is borrowed (musta'ar lahu), whether specific or general, that majaz is like haqiqah in being specific or general. That is, affirmation, meaning. And it is not intended by majaz being general that it includes all types of its relations altogether in one word, but that it includes all individuals of one type, just as by al-sa' all that is measured in it is intended. So that is permissible in our view. (al-Qamar: explanation of refutation etc.) (al-Sunbuli: etc.) 268 Explanation of the divisions of the third division. Definition of Majaz and Its Ruling. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no generality for majaz, because it is a necessity resorted to in speech when haqiqah is impossible, and necessity is measured by its measure, and it is removed by establishing specificity, so generality is not established. And we say: The generality of haqiqah is not because it is haqiqah, but due to an additional indication such as the alif and lam in a definite noun not previously known, the occurrence of an indefinite in the context of negation, its description by a general attribute, the form being a plural form, or the meaning being plural. So when these indications are found in majaz, it also becomes general, because haqiqah is not a condition for generality, nor is majaz an impediment. How can it be said that it is a necessity when it occurs frequently? (al-Qamar: explanation etc.) (al-Sunbuli: etc.)
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on whether majaz (metaphorical usage) can be general (amm). The text begins by defining majaz and explaining the need for the third division to avoid circular definitions. It uses the example of 'salat' which is haqiqah in linguistic sense (supplication) and majaz in legal sense (specified acts), and vice versa. It then states the ruling that majaz can be specific or general like haqiqah. The key point is that generality in majaz is not about encompassing all types of rhetorical relations, but about covering all individuals of a single type (e.g., 'sa' for all measured things). Al-Shafi'i's objection is presented: majaz is a necessity used only when haqiqah is unavailable, so it should be restricted to the specific case and cannot be general. The Hanafi response argues that generality depends on additional linguistic indicators (definite article, negation context, plural forms, etc.), which can also appear in majaz. Since these indicators are not exclusive to haqiqah, majaz can be general. The text also addresses the claim of necessity by noting that majaz is frequent in the Quran, so calling it a necessity would imply deficiency in divine speech, which is untenable. The marginal commentaries by al-Qamar and al-Sunbuli refine these arguments, discussing the conditions for generality and refuting the opponent's reasoning.

Key Terms
المجاز (al-majaz): A word used for a meaning other than the one it was originally placed for, often due to a relation (al-munasabah) and a context preventing the primary meaning.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): A word used for the meaning it was originally placed for, whether in language or law.
العموم (al-‘umum): Inclusion of all individuals of the intended category without exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, why can majaz (metaphorical usage) be general?
A) Because all haqiqah is general, so majaz must also be general.
B) Because the indicators of generality (e.g., alif lam, nafi, plural) can be present in majaz, and generality does not depend on being haqiqah.
C) Because majaz is a necessity and necessity requires generality.
D) Because the Shafi'i view is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Majaz can be general if it carries the same linguistic indicators of generality.

Generality of Figurative Language (Majaz)
Dispute on generality of majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi analysis of majaz to Quranic verses and identify when figurative language retains generality.

Core Idea
Majaz can be general because indicators of generality do not depend on literalness.

This lesson explores a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: can figurative language (majaz) be general (amm)? The Shafi'i school argues that majaz is necessitated only when literal meaning is impossible, so it cannot be general. The Hanafi school disagrees, asserting that generality depends on linguistic indicators, not literality. Understanding this dispute is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses that use metaphor, like 'lower the wing of humility.' We will trace the arguments, examine Quranic examples, and see how the Hanafi position prevails through careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the opening statement: Shafi'i claims 'no generality for metaphor' because it is necessitated. Hanafi counters that generality arises from extra indicators, not literalness.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i argument
Understand the logic: necessity is measured by its extent, so metaphor is restricted to specific contexts and cannot be general.
The Shafi'i view sees metaphor as a fallback, thus limited.
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes between indicators of generality (alif-lam, indefinite in negation, plural forms) and literalness. These indicators can appear in figurative expressions too.
Also note the rejection of necessity: abundant majaz in Quran shows it is not due to incapacity.
4
Review Quranic examples
Study the verses cited: 'the water overflowed' (69:11) and 'a wall intending to collapse' (18:77). Both are metaphorical yet carry general implications.
The Hanafi use these as proof that majaz can be general.
5
Synthesize the tarjih
The passage concludes with a balanced view (insaf): even if majaz is necessitated, it is for the hearer's benefit, not the speaker's deficiency. Hence generality is valid.
This solidifies the Hanafi position as mufta bihi.

Source Text

(القمر) منزه إلخ لأن الضرورة عجز ونقصان. (القمر) واقع في القرآن كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ (النساء: ٩٢) أي رقبة مملوكة (القمر) :إنه أي أن المقتضى من أقسام الاستدلال كما ذكر من أقسام الوقوف على المراد الذي هو حظ السامع المستدل. (القمر) من أقسام الاستدلال إلخ لأن الاقتضاء هو جعل غير المنطوق كالمنطوق لتصحيح المنطوق، وهو صفة المعنى دون اللفظ فيكون ضرورته راجعة إلى الكلام؛ لأنه إنما اعتبر لتصحيح معناه أو إلى السامع؛ لأنه ما اعتبر إلا لتفهمه، ولا يكون راجعا إلى المتكلم، لأنه لم يعتبر ليتحقق تكلمه، بخلاف المجاز؛ فإنه صفة اللفظ، وإنما ثبت ليحصل التوسع للمتكلم في التكلم، فتحقق الضرورة فيه راجع إلى المتكلم أو نقول: إن العموم من اللفظ والمقتضى غير ملفوظ، بخلاف المجاز ؛ فإنه ملفوظ فيعم. (السنبلي) ترجع إلخ لأن المقتضى يثبت ضرورة تصحيح الكلام شرعًا؛ كيلا يؤدي إلى الإخلال بفهم السامع المستدل. (القمر) فلو كان إلخ إيراد كلمة "لو" إيماء إلى أن ضرورة المجاز مجرد فرض (القمر) والإنصاف إلخ هذا جواب بتسليم كون المجاز ضروريًا، والأول كان بمنعه يقول: سلمنا أن المجاز ضروري، لكن ضرورته ليس باعتبار المتكلم بل بالنسبة إلى السامع فلا مانع من كونه عامًا، ويمكن أن يكون جوابا من جانب الشافعية بأن كون المجاز ضروريًا لا ينافي وقوعه في القرآن؛ لأنه ضروري بالنسبة إلى السامع لا المتكلم (السنبلي) لرعاية بلاغات إلخ : ألا ترى إلى ما عد من عجيب بلاغة القرآن، وغريب مناسباته قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاخْفِضْ لَهُمَا جَنَاحَ الذُّلِّ مِنَ الرَّحْمَةِ (الإسراء:٢٤) من أنه ليس للذل جناح. (القمر) صفات
And al-Shafi'i said: There is no generality for metaphor because it is necessitated, resorted to in speech when the literal meaning is impossible, and necessity is measured by its extent, and it is removed by establishing specificity, so generality is not established. But we say: The generality of the literal was not because it is literal, but due to an extra indication like the alif and lam in the indefinite general noun, the occurrence of an indefinite in the context of negation, its description with a general description, the form being a plural form, or the meaning being plural meaning. When these indications are found in metaphor, it also becomes general; for literalness is not a condition for generality, nor is metaphor an obstacle to it. And how can it be said that it is necessitated when it is abundant in the Book of Allah, and Allah is exalted above necessity? It cannot be said that the implied (muqtada) occurs frequently in the Qur'an even though it is necessitated by agreement between us and you; for we say: It is from the categories of inference, so the necessity there reverts to the inferrer, not to the speaker. And metaphor is from the categories of wording, so if it were necessitated, the necessity would revert to the speaker, and the speaker is Allah, exalted above it. This is what they said. But the fair assessment is that the speaker utters metaphor despite ability to use literal wording for the sake of rhetorical effects and purposes not present in the literal. The implied from the Book of Allah: Allah said in the story of Nuh, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, when the water overflowed, We carried you in the ship' (69:11) — and there is no real transgression in water, but rather metaphorically. And in the story of Musa and Khidr, peace be upon them: 'And they found therein a wall intending to collapse' (18:77) — so intending in the wall is metaphorical, not literal; intention was borrowed for nearness. And measure likewise. (Al-Qamar) 'Exalted' etc. because necessity is incapacity and deficiency. (Al-Qamar) 'Occurs in the Qur'an' as in His saying: 'freeing a slave' (4:92) i.e., a slave owned. (Al-Qamar) 'It' i.e., the implied is from the categories of inference as mentioned, from the categories of determining the intended meaning which is the lot of the hearer/inferrer. (Al-Qamar) 'From the categories of inference' etc. because implication is making the unspoken like the spoken for correcting the spoken, and it is a quality of meaning not wording, so its necessity reverts to the speech because it is considered only to correct its meaning, or to the hearer because it is considered only for his understanding, and it does not revert to the speaker because it is not considered to realize his speech, unlike metaphor; for it is a quality of wording and is established to give the speaker expansion in speech, so the realization of necessity in it reverts to the speaker. Or we say: Generality is from wording, and the implied is not uttered, unlike metaphor, for it is uttered, so it is general. (Al-Sanbali) 'Reverts' etc. because the implied is established by necessity to correct speech legally, so as not to lead to deficiency in the understanding of the hearer/inferrer. (Al-Qamar) 'For if it were' etc. — the use of the word 'if' indicates that necessity of metaphor is merely hypothetical. (Al-Qamar) 'But the fair assessment' etc. — this is a response by conceding that metaphor is necessitated, while the first response was by denying it. He says: We concede that metaphor is necessitated, but its necessity is not with respect to the speaker but with respect to the hearer, so there is no impediment to it being general. And it is possible that this is a response from the Shafi'i side that the necessity of metaphor does not contradict its occurrence in the Qur'an because it is necessary for the hearer, not the speaker. (Al-Sanbali) 'For the sake of rhetorical effects' etc.: Do you not see what is counted as the wondrous rhetoric of the Qur'an and its strange purposes, His saying: 'And lower to them the wing of humility from mercy' (17:24) — that humility has no wing? (Al-Qamar) 'Attributes'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerning the generality (umum) of figurative language (majaz). The Shafi'i argument, as quoted, holds that majaz cannot be general because it is used only when literal meaning is impossible (necessity), and necessity is measured by its extent—thus it only establishes specific meanings, not general ones. The Hanafi response is multi-pronged. First, they argue that generality in literal words is not due to literality per se, but due to specific linguistic indicators (qara'in) such as the definite article alif lam in a non-referential indefinite, an indefinite in a negative context, plural forms, etc. These same indicators can exist in figurative expressions, thereby conferring generality. Thus, literality is not a condition for generality, nor is figurativeness a preventer. Second, they address the necessity argument: if majaz were truly necessitated, it would imply deficiency in the speaker (Allah) who uses it abundantly in the Qur'an. To counter the possible objection that iqtida (implicature) is also necessitated and occurs in the Qur'an, the Hanafis distinguish: iqtida is a type of inference (istidlal) where necessity refers to the inferrer (hearer), not the speaker. Majaz, however, is a quality of the wording itself, so its necessity would refer to the speaker—which is inappropriate for Allah. The passage then offers a more balanced view: the speaker (Allah) uses majaz despite being able to use literal expressions for rhetorical purposes (balagha) and suitability. This concedes that majaz is not necessitated by incapacity, but by choice for eloquence. Examples from the Quran are given: 'the water overflowed' (tughyan is metaphorical for rising), 'a wall intending to collapse' (intention is metaphorical for near-collapse), and 'freeing a slave' (raqaba implies a owned slave). The commentary by al-Qamar and al-Sanbali further refines the discussion, noting that the implied (muqtada) is unspoken and its necessity relates to the hearer's understanding, whereas majaz is spoken and its generality is valid. The fair assessment (insaf) is that even if majaz is necessitated, it is necessitated for the hearer's benefit, not the speaker's deficiency. This passage is crucial for understanding Hanafi insistence on the possibility of general majaz, which affects interpretation of many Quranic verses and hadiths in fiqh.

Key Terms
مجاز (majāz): Figurative or metaphorical usage where a word is used in a non-literal sense due to a relationship (علاقة) between the literal and figurative meanings.
عموم (ʿumūm): The quality of a linguistic expression that encompasses all individuals of its referent without restriction.
مقتضى (muqtaḍā): An implied or presupposed meaning necessary to make a statement coherent, though not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, what is the primary reason that figurative language (majaz) can be general?
A) Because metaphor is always literal in a hidden sense.
B) Because the indicators of generality are not dependent on the word being literal.
C) Because necessity requires generality in metaphor.
D) Because the Shafi'i position was based on a misunderstanding.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that majaz can be general because the linguistic indicators of generality are independent of literalness, a principle crucial for interpreting Quranic metaphors as carrying universal legal import.

Definition of Majaz and Its Generality in the Quran
Defining majaz, addressing the necessity objection, and proving its generality in Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define majaz, explain why it does not imply necessity for Allah, and apply the principle that majaz can carry generality (umum) using the hadith of Ibn 'Umar on riba.

Core Idea
Majaz is not a sign of deficiency but a choice for eloquence; it can convey general rulings just like haqiqa.

This lesson explores the linguistic category of majaz (metaphor) in the Quran and its legal implications. It addresses the objection that majaz implies darurah (necessity), which would be unbecoming of Allah, and establishes that majaz is used for eloquence. It further demonstrates that majaz can carry generality (umum) through the example of the word 'sa'' in a hadith on riba, yielding a broader prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Define majaz and distinguish it from haqiqa
From the text, identify majaz as a word used in a non-literal sense due to a relationship (alaqah) and a contextual indicator (qarinah). Understand that the speaker (Allah) is capable of using haqiqa but chooses majaz for eloquence.
The text emphasizes that necessity (darurah) in majaz is from the listener's perspective, not the speaker's.
2
Address the necessity objection
The objection: majaz implies necessity, which is impossible for Allah. The Hanafi response: necessity in muqtada (implicit meaning) falls on the inferrer, not the speaker; in majaz, necessity is only from the listener's side, not the speaker's. The speaker uses majaz voluntarily for eloquence.
The text says: 'Al-insaaf anna al-mutakallim yatalaffadu bi al-majaz ma'a qudratihi 'ala al-haqiqa' – The fair view is that the speaker utters majaz while able to use haqiqa.
3
Identify Quranic evidence for majaz
The text cites three verses: al-Haqqah 11 ('overflowing water'), al-Kahf 77 ('wall wanting to collapse'), and al-Isra 24 ('wing of humility'). Recognize that literal interpretation is impossible, proving majaz exists in the Quran.
These examples show that Allah uses metaphor, and it is not a defect.
4
Analyze the hadith of Ibn 'Umar on riba
The word 'sa'' cannot refer to the physical container (selling a wooden sa' for two is permissible), so it must be metaphorical for the commodity it contains. The definite article 'al-' indicates generality (istighraq), so the prohibition covers all commodities measured by sa', including non-food items like plaster and lime.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi position extends riba to non-food; Shafi'i restricts it to food.
5
Understand the difference between majaz and muqtada
Muqtada (implicit meaning) is a rational necessity not uttered, limited to correcting speech; majaz is uttered and can carry generality. Generality is a property of wording, so majaz can be general like haqiqa.
The text: 'al-umum min al-lafth wa al-muqtada ghayru malfuz, bikhilaf al-majaz fa innahu malfuz faya'um.'

(السنبلي) ترجع إلخ لأن المقتضى يثبت ضرورة تصحيح الكلام شرعًا؛ كيلا يؤدي إلى الإخلال بفهم السامع المستدل. (القمر) فلو كان إلخ إيراد كلمة "لو" إيماء إلى أن ضرورة المجاز مجرد فرض (القمر) والإنصاف إلخ هذا جواب بتسليم كون المجاز ضروريًا، والأول كان بمنعه يقول: سلمنا أن المجاز ضروري، لكن ضرورته ليس باعتبار المتكلم بل بالنسبة إلى السامع فلا مانع من كونه عامًا، ويمكن أن يكون جوابا من جانب الشافعية بأن كون المجاز ضروريًا لا ينافي وقوعه في القرآن؛ لأنه ضروري بالنسبة إلى السامع لا المتكلم (السنبلي) لرعاية بلاغات إلخ : ألا ترى إلى ما عد من عجيب بلاغة القرآن، وغريب مناسباته قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاخْفِضْ لَهُمَا جَنَاحَ الذُّلِّ مِنَ الرَّحْمَةِ (الإسراء:٢٤) من أنه ليس للذل جناح. (القمر) صفات ولكنه ضروري بحسب السامع بمعنى أن السامع لابد له أن يصرف أولاً إلى الحقيقة، فإذا لم يستقم حمله عليها، فحينئذ يصرفه إلى المجاز. ولهذا جعلنا لفظة الصاع في حديث ابن عمر وما عامًا فيما يحله أي لأجل أن المجاز يكون عامًا جعلنا لفظ الصاع في حديث رواه ابن عمر ما عن الرسول عليه وهو قوله : "لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين عامًا في كل ما يحل الصاع ويجاوره؛ لأن الحقيقة ليست بمرادة اتفاقاً؛ إذ نفس الصاع الذي يكون من الخشب يجوز بيعه بالصاعين في الشريعة، فلابد أن يكون مجازا عما يحله، فالشافعي له يقدر لفظ الطعام ولكن ضروري إلخ وإنما ثبتت ضرورته؛ لئلا يلزم إلغاء الكلام وإخلاء اللفظ عن المرام، فكانت الضرورة راجعة إلى الكلام والسامع فلا يستحيل وجود المجاز في كتاب الله تعالى، وهذه الضرورة لا تنافي العموم؛ إذ هو متعلق بدلالة اللفظ وإرادة المتكلم، فإذا وجب حمل اللفظ على المعنى المجازي ضرورة عدم إمكان العمل بالحقيقة، يجب أن يحمل على ما قصده المتكلم، واحتمل اللفظ بحسب القرينة إن عامًا ،فعام وإن خاصا فخاص، بخلاف المقتضى فإنه لازم عقلي غير ملفوظ فيقتصر فيه على ما يحصل به صحة الكلام من غير آيات العموم الذي هو من صفات اللفظ خاصة كذا في "التلويح" وبعض شروح "الحسامي". (السنبلي) يصرفه إلخ لئلا يلزم إلغاء الكلام. (القمر) مجازا : إطلاقا لاسم المحل على الحال . (القمر) عما يحله إلخ بطريق إطلاق اسم المحل على الحال لكن المراد عندنا جميع ما يحل في الصاع مطعوماً أو غير مطعوم؛ لأنه محلى بلام التعريف وأنه للاستغراق، والعموم عند عدم المعهود دليل فيما نحن فيه وليس فيما نحن فيه معهود فيفيد العموم في المجاز كما يفيده في الحقيقة، فيدل الحديث بعبارته على أن الربا يجري في غير المطعوم كالجص والنورة؛ كما يجري في المطعوم (السنبلي) أخرج مسلم في صحيحه رقم ۱٥٩٥، باب بيع الطعام مثلاً بمثل، والنسائي رقم: ٤٥٥٥، باب بيع التمر بالتمر متفاضلاً، عن أبي سعيد الخدري الله قال: كنا نرزق ثمر الجمع على عهد رسول الله الله الخلط التمر، فكنا نبيع صاعين بصاع، فبلغ ذلك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "لا صاعي تمر بصاع، ولا صاعي حنطة بصاع، ولا درهم بدرهمين"، وأخرج ابن ماجه في "سننه" رقم ٢٢٥٦، باب الصرف وما لا يجوز متفاضلا يدا بيد، عن أبي سعيد الخدري قال: كان النبي الله يرزقنا تمراً من تمر الجمع، فنتبدل به تمراً هو أطيب منه، ونزيد في السعر، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: لا يصلح صاع تمر بصاعين، ولا درهم بدرهمين إلخ. وهو من
Explanation of the sections of the third division. Definition of majaz and its ruling in the Book of Allah the Exalted. And Allah the Exalted is free from necessity. It cannot be said that [implicit meaning] occurs in the Quran often while it is necessary by agreement between us and you; because we say: it is from the types of inference, so the necessity there refers back to the one who infers, not to the speaker. And majaz is from the types of words, so if it were necessary, the necessity would refer back to the speaker, and the speaker is Allah the Exalted, who is free from it. Thus they said. And the fair view is that the speaker utters majaz while being able to use haqiqa, for the sake of attending to eloquent expressions and occasions that were not present in the haqiqa. That [implicit meaning] is from the Book of Allah the Exalted: Allah the Exalted said in the story of Nuh, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, when the water overflowed, We carried you in the ark' (Surah al-Haqqah: 11), and there is no real overflowing in water, but rather metaphorically. And in the story of Musa and Khidr, peace be upon them: 'So they found therein a wall that wanted to collapse' (Surah al-Kahf: 77), so the wanting in the wall is metaphorical, not real; wanting is borrowed for approaching. And analogize to this. (al-Qamar) 'free from' etc. because necessity is incapacity and deficiency. (al-Qamar) 'occurs in the Quran' as in His saying: 'then free a neck' (Surah al-Nisa: 92), meaning a possessed neck. (al-Qamar) 'that is, that implicit meaning is from the types of inference' as mentioned, from the types of attaining the intended meaning which is the lot of the listener/inferrer. (al-Qamar) 'from the types of inference' etc. because istidlaal is making what is not spoken like the spoken to correct the spoken, and it is a quality of meaning not the wording, so its necessity refers back to the speech because it was only considered to correct its meaning, or to the listener because it was only considered for his understanding, and it does not refer back to the speaker because it was not considered to realize his speech. Contrary to majaz, for it is a quality of the wording, and it is established only to give the speaker expansion in speech, so the realization of necessity in it refers back to the speaker. Or we say: Generality is from the wording, and the implicit meaning is not uttered, unlike majaz, for it is uttered, so it is general. (al-Sanbli) 'refers back' etc. because the implicit meaning is established by necessity to correct the speech legally, so as not to lead to deficiency in the understanding of the listener/inferrer. (al-Qamar) 'for if it were' etc. The use of the word 'if' indicates that the necessity of majaz is merely hypothetical. (al-Qamar) 'and the fair view' etc. This is a response by conceding that majaz is necessary, while the first one was by denying it. He says: We concede that majaz is necessary, but its necessity is not with respect to the speaker, but rather relative to the listener, so there is no impediment to its being general. And it is possible that it is a response from the side of the Shafi'is: that majaz being necessary does not contradict its occurrence in the Quran, because it is necessary relative to the listener, not the speaker. (al-Sanbli) 'for attending to eloquent expressions' etc. Do you not see what is counted of the wonderful eloquence of the Quran and the strange occasions of His saying: 'And lower to them the wing of humility out of mercy' (Surah al-Isra: 24) — that humility has no wing. (al-Qamar) 'attributes' But it is necessary with respect to the listener, meaning that the listener must first turn to the haqiqa, and if it is not consistent to apply it to the haqiqa, then he turns it to the majaz. And for this reason we made the word 'sa'' in the hadith of Ibn 'Umar general in what it contains — that is, because majaz can be general, we made the word 'sa'' in the hadith narrated by Ibn 'Umar from the Messenger of Allah, which is his saying: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa's' general in everything that the sa' contains and what is adjacent to it; because the haqiqa is not intended by agreement, since the actual sa' that is made of wood is allowed to be sold for two sa's in the Sharia, so it must be majaz for what it contains. So al-Shafi'i for him specifies the word 'food'. But it is necessary, etc. And its necessity is established only so as not to lead to nullification of speech and emptying the word from the intended meaning. So the necessity refers back to the speech and the listener, so the existence of majaz in the Book of Allah the Exalted is not impossible. And this necessity does not contradict generality, because it is related to the indication of the word and the intent of the speaker. So when the word must be taken in the metaphorical sense due to the impossibility of acting upon the haqiqa, it must be taken according to what the speaker intended, and the word can carry, according to the context, either general or specific. If general, then general; if specific, then specific. Unlike the implicit meaning, for it is a rational necessity not uttered, so it is limited to that which renders the speech correct without indication of generality, which is a property of the wording specifically. Thus in 'al-Talwih' and some commentaries of 'al-Hisami'. (al-Sanbli) 'he turns it' etc. lest nullification of speech occur. (al-Qamar) 'majaz' by applying the name of the container to the content. (al-Qamar) 'for what it contains' etc. by way of applying the name of the container to the content, but the intended meaning with us is everything that is contained in the sa', edible or non-edible, because it is prefixed with the definite article 'alif lam', and that indicates generality, and generality in the absence of a specified referent is evidence in our case. And there is no specified referent in our case, so it conveys generality in the majaz just as it does in the haqiqa. So the hadith by its wording indicates that riba applies to non-food items like plaster and lime, just as it applies to food. (al-Sanbli) Muslim narrated in his Sahih, no. 1595, chapter of selling food like for like, and al-Nasa'i no. 4555, chapter of selling dates for dates with disparity, from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: We used to be given mixed dates from the collection at the time of the Messenger of Allah, so we used to sell two sa's for one sa'. That reached the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he said: 'No two sa's of dates for one sa', nor two sa's of wheat for one sa', nor a dirham for two dirhams.' And Ibn Majah narrated in his Sunan, no. 2256, chapter of sarf and what is not allowed with disparity hand to hand, from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri who said: The Prophet gave us dates from the collection, so we exchanged them for better dates and increased the price. So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'It is not permissible: a sa' of dates for two sa's, nor a dirham for two dirhams' etc. And it is from.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of majaz (metaphorical usage) and its permissibility in the Quran, as part of a larger usul al-fiqh commentary. The author begins by stating the third division of linguistic categories, defining majaz and its ruling. The key objection addressed is that majaz implies darurah (necessity), which would be unbecoming of Allah. The Hanafi response distinguishes between darurah in the speaker (mutakallim) and the listener (sam'i). The implicit meaning (muqtada) is considered a type of inference (istidlal) where necessity falls on the inferrer, not the speaker. Majaz, as a property of the word itself, if necessary, would reflect on the speaker, which is impossible for Allah. However, the author argues that majaz is not necessary for the speaker; rather, it is used for eloquence (balaghah). Some concede that majaz is necessary from the listener's perspective because the listener must first attempt the haqiqa and only resort to majaz if the haqiqa is impossible. This does not contradict generality. The author then applies this to the hadith of Ibn 'Umar prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams or a sa' for two sa's. The word 'sa'' is majaz for what it contains (the commodity). Hanafis hold that the definite article makes it general, so riba applies to all things measured by sa', including non-food items like plaster and lime. Al-Shafi'i restricts it to food. The passage concludes with references to hadith sources and supports the Hanafi position that generality in majaz is valid.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a sense other than its original meaning due to a relationship (alaqah) and a contextual indicator (qarinah).
حقيقة (haqiqa): A word used in its original, literal meaning.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity or compulsion; in this context, the necessity to interpret a word metaphorically because the literal meaning is impossible or leads to absurdity.
مقتضى (muqtada): An implicit meaning (muqtada) that is not literally stated but necessary to understand the speech correctly; a category of inference (istidlal).
عموم (umum): Generality; a word that includes all members of a category. Here applied to majaz via the definite article.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the word 'sa'' in the hadith of Ibn 'Umar carry generality?
A) Because the literal meaning (the container) is intended, and the prohibition applies to the container itself.
B) Because the word is metaphorical for the content, and the definite article indicates generality, so the prohibition covers all commodities measured by sa'.
C) Because the hadith explicitly lists all types of commodities.
D) Because al-Shafi'i agreed that it is general.

Key Takeaway
Majaz is a valid linguistic category in the Quran and hadith, used for eloquence, and it can carry general rulings when accompanied by indicators like the definite article.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Three Types of Oaths and Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Ghamus
Classification and proof of oaths and Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on ghamus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i usul arguments on ghamus by identifying the role of literal meaning (haqiqah) vs metaphor (majaz) and the principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad.

Core Idea
Ghamus incurs sin but no expiation: the literal meaning of 'contracted oaths' excludes past oaths.

Oaths are classified into three categories: laghw (unintentional false), ghamus (intentional false about past), and mun'aqida (about future). While laghw and mun'aqida are agreed upon, ghamus is disputed between Hanafis and Shafi'is. This lesson unpacks the textual proof and usul principles that lead to the Hanafi position that ghamus requires sin but not expiation.

Guided Path
1
Classify the three oaths
State the definitions and agreed rulings for laghw, ghamus, and mun'aqida from the passage.
Laghw: no sin, no expiation; Mun'aqida: sin and expiation if broken.
2
Identify the dispute
Pinpoint ghamus as the contested category and the two positions: Hanafi (sin only) vs Shafi'i (sin and expiation).
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Examine how each school interprets Quran 2:225 and 5:89. The Shafi'i harmonizes them; the Hanafi separates them based on literal meaning.
Focus on the phrase 'contracted oaths' (aqadtum al-ayman).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why the literal meaning is prioritized over metaphor, leading to the rule: ghamus has sin but no kaffarah.
The marginal notes emphasize: 'if literal is possible, metaphor is dropped.'

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث وتحقيقه أن اليمين ثلاث لغو، وغموس ومنعقدة. فاللغو أن يحلف على فعل ماض كاذبًا ظاناً أنه حق ولا إثم فيه ولا كفارة والغموس أن يحلف على فعل ماض كاذبًا عمدًا، وفيه الإثم دون الكفارة عندنا، وعند الشافعي له فيه الكفارة أيضًا. والمنعقدة: أن يحلف على فعل ،آتٍ فإن حنث فيه يجب الإثم والكفارة جميعًا بالاتفاق؛ وذلك لأن الله تعالى ذكر هذه المسألة في الموضعين، فقال في سورة البقرة: لَا يُؤَاخِذُكُمُ اللَّهُ بِاللَّغْوِ فِي أَيْمَانِكُمْ وَلَكِنْ يُوَاحِدُكُمْ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ، وقال في سورة المائدة عوضه: ﴿وَلَكِنْ يُوَاحِدُكُمْ (البقرة: ٢٢٥) بِمَا عَقَدْتُمُ الْأَيْمَانَ فَكَفَّارَتُهُ (الآية)، فالشافعي له: يقول: بأن قوله: ﴿بمَا عَقْدتُمُ ﴾ (المائدة: ۸۹) الْأَيْمَانَ معناه ومعنى بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ ،واحد، فيشمل كلا الآيتين الغموس والمنعقدة جميعًا، والمؤاخذة في المائدة مقيدة بالكفارة، فتحمل عليها المؤاخذة المطلقة المذكورة في البقرة، فيكون الإثم والكفارة في كليهما فيطبق بين الآيتين بهذا النمط، ونحن الشافعي نقول: إن معنى العزم والكسب مجاز في قوله تعالى: بِمَا عَقَدْتُمُ الْأَيْمَانَ ، والحقيقة هو المنعقدة فقط، فآية المائدة تدل على أن الكفارة في المنعقدة فقط، بخلاف بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ في البقرة، فإنه عام للغموس والمنعقدة جميعًا، والمؤاخذة فيها مطلقة فتصرف إلى البقرة الفرد الكامل، وهو المؤاخذة الأخروية، فيكون الإثم في الغموس والمنعقدة جميعًا، هذا هو غاية التحرير في هذا المقام، وسيجيء هذا في بحث المعارضة أيضًا إن شاء الله تعالى. والغموس: مبالغة في الغمس سميت به؛ لأنها تغمس صاحبها في الإثم ثم في النار. (القمر) بما كسبت إلخ: أي بما عزمت وقصدت قلوبكم وهو الغموس والمنعقدة (القمر) عوضه : أي عوض قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنْ يُوَاحِدُكُمْ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ قُلُوبُكُمْ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٢٥) (القمر) الآيتين: أي آية البقرة والمائدة (المحشي) عليها : أي على المؤاخذة المذكورة في المائدة (القمر) المطلقة لأن الشوافع يحملون المطلق على المقيد . (المحشي) كليهما: أي الغموس والمنعقدة. :فقط لأن المجاز بعد إمكان العمل بالحقيقة ساقط. (المحشي فيها : أي غير مقيدة بالكفارة.
Explanation of the divisions of the third division and its verification: that the oath is three: laghw, ghamus, and mun'aqida. Al-laghw: that one swears about a past action falsely while thinking it is true; there is no sin nor expiation. Al-ghamus: that one swears about a past action falsely intentionally; there is sin without expiation according to us, while according to al-Shafi'i there is expiation as well. Al-mun'aqida: that one swears about a future action; if he breaks it, sin and expiation are obligatory by consensus. This is because Allah mentioned this issue in two places: He said in Surah al-Baqarah: 'Allah does not take you to account for the laghw in your oaths, but He takes you to account for what your hearts have earned'; and in Surah al-Ma'idah in its place: 'But He takes you to account for the oaths you have contracted; its expiation...' Al-Shafi'i says that His saying 'the oaths you have contracted' means the same as 'what your hearts have earned', so both verses cover ghamus and mun'aqida together. The taking to account in al-Ma'idah is restricted to expiation, so the unrestricted taking to account mentioned in al-Baqarah is carried to it; thus sin and expiation apply to both, and the two verses are harmonized in this manner. We (the Hanafis) say: the meaning of 'determination and earning' is metaphorical in His saying 'the oaths you have contracted'; the reality is only the mun'aqida. So the verse of al-Ma'idah indicates that expiation is only for mun'aqida, unlike 'what your hearts have earned' in al-Baqarah, which is general for both ghamus and mun'aqida, and the taking to account there is unrestricted, so it is diverted to the perfect individual, which is the other-worldly taking to account; thus sin applies to both ghamus and mun'aqida. This is the ultimate explanation in this context, and this will come again in the discussion of opposing proofs, God willing. Al-ghamus: an intensive form of 'immersion', so called because it immerses its doer in sin and then in the Fire. (Al-Qamar) 'what your hearts have earned' etc.: i.e., what your hearts determined and intended, and it is ghamus and mun'aqida. (Al-Qamar) 'in its place': i.e., in place of His saying 'but He takes you to account for what your hearts have earned' (al-Baqarah 2:225). (Al-Qamar) 'the two verses': i.e., the verse of al-Baqarah and al-Ma'idah. (Al-Muhashi) 'upon it': i.e., upon the taking to account mentioned in al-Ma'idah. (Al-Qamar) 'the unrestricted': because the Shafi'is carry the unrestricted to the restricted. (Al-Muhashi) 'both of them': i.e., ghamus and mun'aqida. 'Only because after it is possible to act by the reality, the metaphor is dropped.' (Al-Muhashi) 'therein': i.e., not restricted by expiation.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the ruling on ghamus (perjury by swearing intentionally about a past event). The author first classifies oaths into three types: laghw (unintentional false swearing), ghamus (intentional false swearing), and mun'aqida (swearing about a future action). By consensus, laghw has no sin or expiation, and mun'aqida has both sin and expiation if broken. The dispute centers on ghamus: Hanafis hold that it incurs sin but no expiation, while Shafi'is hold that it incurs both sin and expiation. The proof is based on two Quranic verses: al-Baqarah 2:225 (unrestricted taking to account) and al-Ma'idah 5:89 (taking to account restricted to expiation for 'contracted oaths'). The Shafi'i argues that 'contracted oaths' means all oaths with intent, including ghamus, and since the Ma'idah verse restricts the taking to account to expiation, the unrestricted taking to account in al-Baqarah is carried to it, making expiation obligatory for ghamus. The Hanafi response uses the tool of majaz (metaphor): the term 'contracted oaths' literally only applies to oaths about the future (mun'aqida), not past oaths. Ghamus is included only metaphorically in 'what your hearts earned' in al-Baqarah, which is a general verse that is taken as referring to other-worldly punishment (sin) only, not expiation. Thus, Hanafis conclude that ghamus carries sin but no expiation. The marginal notes clarify that this interpretation relies on prioritizing the literal meaning over metaphor, and that the Shafi'i principle of carrying the unrestricted to the restricted (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad) is rejected here because the two verses are not identical in subject matter.

Key Terms
اليمين اللغو (al-yamin al-laghw): An oath about a past action that is false but the swearer believes it to be true; no sin or expiation.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghamus): An oath about a past action that is intentionally false; sin without expiation according to Hanafis.
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqida): An oath about a future action; if broken, sin and expiation by consensus.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath, which is feeding or clothing ten poor people, or freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on ghamus (intentionally false oath about the past)?
A) Sin and expiation are both required.
B) Sin is required but no expiation.
C) Neither sin nor expiation is required.
D) Expiation is required but no sin.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on ghamus (sin without expiation) is derived from prioritizing the literal meaning of 'contracted oaths' as future oaths.

Linguistic vs. Legal Meaning of Nikah and Its Effect on Mahramiyat
Usul dispute on the interpretation of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the interpretation of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22, and evaluate the linguistic-legal argument that determines whether zina creates mahramiyat.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that 'nikah' linguistically means intercourse, so zina creates mahramiyat.

The word 'nikah' appears in Quran 4:22 prohibiting marriage to women whom one's father 'married'. At the heart of a key Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute is whether this refers to the act of intercourse or the marriage contract. The resolution depends on whether the linguistic literal meaning (intercourse) or the later legal meaning (contract) is taken as the intended sense. This lesson unpacks the usul al-fiqh reasoning behind each position and its practical consequence for in-law prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Textual Ambiguity
Examine the word 'nikah' in Quran 4:22. It can mean either the act of intercourse or the marriage contract. This ambiguity (mujmal) requires clarification (bayan).
The ambiguity is resolved by determining which meaning is the literal (haqiqi) and which is metaphorical (majazi).
2
State the Hanafi and Shafi'i Positions
The Hanafi school takes 'nikah' as intercourse (linguistic literal meaning), so the verse prohibits marrying any woman with whom the father had intercourse, including zina. The Shafi'i school takes it as the contract (legal reality), so only the contract triggers prohibition.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether zina establishes mahramiyat.
3
Trace the Hanafi Dalil: Linguistic Origin
Hanafis argue that 'nikah' linguistically means 'joining' (damm), physically realized by intercourse. The contract is only a cause of joining, so it is metaphorical. At the time of revelation, no legal reality had been established, so the linguistic meaning must be used.
Key proof: 'the contract is called nikah because it is a cause of joining'.
4
Analyze the Objections and Hanafi Responses
Objections include: (1) exegetes say 'nikah' always means contract in the Quran, (2) the linguistic meaning is legally restricted. Hanafis respond: exegetes' view is not binding on jurists, and the legal reality was deduced later, so it does not retroactively change the revelation's meaning.
The passage also notes a counterargument that the contract itself implies joining, but the Hanafis maintain the contract is not a true individual of 'joining'.
5
Determine the Practical Outcome
Hanafi ruling: A woman with whom a man had intercourse (even through zina) becomes mahram to his sons, so marriage to her is forbidden. Shafi'i ruling: Only a valid marriage contract creates mahramiyat; zina does not.
This difference affects inheritance eligibility, seclusion (khalwa), and other rulings related to mahrams.

Source Text

والنكاح للوطء دون العقد أي يكون النكاح المذكور في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَنْكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُمْ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ» محمولاً على الوطء دون العقد، فيشمل الوطء الحلال والحرام، (النساء: ٢٢) والوطء بملك اليمين أيضا؛ لأن النكاح في الأصل الضم وهو إنما يكون بالوطء والعقد إنما نكاحا؛ لأنه سبب الضم، فمن حيث اللغة حقيقة النكاح الوطء، والعقد مجاز، ومن حيث الشرع بالعكس فالشافعي يحلله حمل النكاح ههنا على معناه المتعارف، فلا يُثبِتُ حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا ونحن نحمله على حقيقته اللغوية، فنثبت حرمة المصاهرة بالزنا. للوطء إلخ فيه أن هذا مخالف لما ذكر في "المدارك" في تفسير سورة الأحزاب: أنه لم يرد لفظ النكاح في كتاب الله تعالى إلا في معنى العقد؛ لأنه في معنى الوطء، إلا أن يقال: إن المذكور في "المدارك" قول المفسرين، والمذكور ههنا قول الفقهاء فلا تخالف. (القمر) بالوطء إلخ قلت: فالوطء فرد للمعنى الحقيقي أي الضم فهو كالحقيقة لا عين الحقيقة؛ بخلاف العقد فهو ليس بفرد له أيضًا، فهو خارج عن المعنى الموضوع له قطعا قلت فيه نظر؛ لأن العقد أيضًا ينبئ عن معنى الضم، فإنه يقال له في الفارسية بندش ،وبستن وهو لا يمكن بدون الضم بل هو الضم نفسه. (القمر) بالعكس : أي حقيقة النكاح العقد والوطء بجاز. (القمر)
And 'nikah' is for intercourse, not the contract, meaning that the 'nikah' mentioned in His saying 'Do not marry what your fathers married from women' is carried to intercourse, not the contract, so it includes lawful and unlawful intercourse and also intercourse by right of ownership, because nikah originally means 'joining' and that is only achieved by intercourse, while the contract is called nikah because it is a cause of joining. So from a linguistic perspective, the literal meaning of nikah is intercourse, and the contract is metaphorical; from a legal perspective, the opposite. Al-Shafi'i analyzes it by carrying nikah here to the commonly understood meaning (contract), so he does not establish in-law prohibition through zina. We (Hanafis) carry it to its linguistic literal meaning, so we establish in-law prohibition through zina. (Al-Qamar) 'For intercourse etc.': There is in this that it contradicts what is mentioned in 'Al-Madarik' in the commentary of Surah al-Ahzab: that the word nikah does not occur in the Book of Allah except in the sense of contract, because it is in the sense of intercourse, except that it can be said that what is mentioned in 'Al-Madarik' is the statement of the exegetes, and what is mentioned here is the statement of the jurists, so there is no contradiction. (Al-Qamar) 'It means etc.': An indication that the author's statement 'and nikah' is coordinated to his statement 'the contract'. (Al-Qamar) 'Carried to intercourse etc.': So the meaning is 'do not marry women whom your fathers had intercourse with, whether lawful or unlawful intercourse'. As for the prohibition of a woman contracted to the father without intercourse, it is by consensus, as al-Tahtawi said. (Al-Qamar) 'And it is etc.': i.e., joining is only by intercourse, whether lawful or unlawful. (Al-Qamar) 'By intercourse etc.': I say: intercourse is an individual of the literal meaning, i.e., joining, so it is like the reality but not the very reality; unlike the contract, which is not even an individual of it, so it is definitely outside the assigned meaning. I say: there is consideration in this, because the contract also indicates the meaning of joining, as it is called in Persian 'bandish' and 'bastan', and it cannot be without joining, rather it is the joining itself. (Al-Sunbuli) 'And the contract is metaphorical, and when acting by the reality is possible, acting by the metaphor is invalid.' (Al-Muhashi) 'And the contract is metaphorical etc.': There is in it that there is no certainty that the contract is a metaphorical meaning of nikah, because in the books of language both meanings are mentioned. (Al-Qamar) 'The opposite': i.e., the literal meaning of nikah is the contract and intercourse is metaphorical. (Al-Qamar) 'We carry it to its literal meaning etc.': It is impaired by the fact that the linguistic meaning of the word nikah is legally restricted, and the legally restricted is like the customarily restricted; so it is not valid to intend the linguistic meaning from nikah, because the customary-legal reality takes precedence over the linguistic reality as will come. Unless it is said that the contract being the legal reality of the word nikah was only deduced by the jurists from the usage of the Law, and it was not established at the time of the revelation of the noble verse 'Do not marry what your fathers married' (al-Nisa 4:22) — so reflect. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the interpretation of the word 'nikah' in the Quranic verse 4:22 ('Do not marry what your fathers married among women'). The Hanafi position is that 'nikah' here means the act of sexual intercourse (wat'), not the marriage contract ('aqd). This is based on the linguistic origin of 'nikah' meaning 'joining' (damm), which is physically realized by intercourse, while the contract is only called nikah metaphorically as a cause of such joining. Consequently, the verse prohibits marrying any woman with whom one's father had intercourse, whether lawful (through marriage or concubinage) or unlawful (through zina). Thus, Hanafis establish the prohibition of in-law relationships (mahramiyat) through zina. The Shafi'i position, according to the passage, interprets 'nikah' in the verse as the commonly understood legal meaning (the contract), so the prohibition only applies to women with whom the father had a valid marriage contract, not through zina. The marginal notes (القمر, المحشي, السنبلي) discuss objections and counterpoints. One objection is that the well-known principle from the tafsir of al-Madarik states that 'nikah' in the Quran always means contract, but the text responds that this is the exegetes' view, while the jurists' technical usage may differ. Another objection argues that the linguistic meaning is legally restricted by later usage, so one cannot intend it in the Quran; the response is that the legal reality of 'nikah' as contract was deduced by later jurists and may not have been intended at the time of revelation. Overall, the passage defends the Hanafi reliance on the linguistic literal meaning.

Key Terms
النكاح (al-nikah): In this context, Hanafis define it linguistically as sexual intercourse (wat'), while Shafi'is define it legally as the marriage contract.
المصاهرة (al-musaharah): In-law relationships that create marriage prohibitions (e.g., wife's mother, daughter). This passage discusses whether zina creates such prohibitions.
الضم (al-damm): Joining; the original meaning of 'nikah' linguistically, which is physically realized through intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi madhhab, does zina (illicit intercourse) with a woman create mahramiyat (prohibit her marriage to one's son)?
A) Yes, because nikah in the verse means intercourse.
B) No, because nikah means the contract only.
C) It depends on whether the zina was public or private.
D) Only if the father also had a marriage contract.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'nikah' in Quran 4:22 as intercourse or contract determines whether zina establishes mahramiyat; the Hanafi school prefers the linguistic literal meaning because the legal reality was not established at the time of revelation.

Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz with Three Examples
Explaining the principle of haqiqah/majaz incompatibility through bequest, khamr, and son examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot coexist in a single usage, and apply it to derive correct rulings in bequests, khamr definition, and terms of familial relationship.

Core Idea
One word, one meaning: haqiqah and majaz are mutually exclusive in a single utterance.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, a word cannot simultaneously convey its literal and figurative meanings. This foundational rule prevents interpretive confusion and ensures consistency in deriving rulings from sacred texts. The passage below illustrates this principle through three concrete legal examples: bequests to multiple recipients, the definition of khamr (wine), and bequests to 'sons'. Each example shows how the inability to combine haqiqah and majaz leads to specific Hanafi positions and reveals differences with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle of Mutual Exclusivity
Grasp that haqiqah (the original, literal meaning) and majaz (figurative meaning) cannot both be intended for the same word in one context. If context (qarina) indicates one, the other is excluded.
This principle is a core tool for interpreting legal texts; it prevents assuming both meanings simultaneously.
2
Analyze the Bequest Example (Wasaya)
Examine the bequest to 'mawali' (plural). The plural form is haqiqah for two or more; using it for a single person would be majaz. Since both cannot be combined, the literal plural meaning takes precedence, and with only one recipient half the bequest is returned to heirs.
This illustrates how the principle determines the minimum number of legatees.
3
Examine the Khamr Definition
Study the Hanafi definition of khamr as raw grape wine that ferments. The word is haqiqah for that specific substance. Other intoxicants are not khamr (haqiqah), and calling them khamr would be majaz. The two cannot be combined, so other intoxicants do not carry the hadd penalty for drinking a drop.
Al-Shafi'i considers all intoxicants as khamr (majaz covering all). The Hanafi response relies on the primacy of haqiqah.
4
Apply to the 'Bequest to Sons' Example
The word 'ibn' (son) is haqiqah for a direct son and majaz for a grandson. A testator cannot intend both. Abu Hanifa holds that only direct sons are included, even if there is only one direct son. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow including grandsons when the plural is used but only one direct son exists, because the oddity (qarina) forces a figurative reading.
Note the role of qarina: in the two-son case, no qarina exists, so all agree only direct sons are meant.

Statement of the divisions of the third division. Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and figurative meaning. Because a bequest is only executed in one-third, and the minimum plural in a bequest is two, so the remaining half of the third is returned to the heirs of the testator. And the freed-slave of the freed-slave gets nothing unless the freed-slave is without an intermediary, then he gets what was bequeathed. And other than khamr (wine) is not annexed to khamr, a second derivative, and it is connected to his saying 'the bequest' meaning that other than tila' (cooked wine), date infusion, raisin infusion, and similar other intoxicants are not annexed to khamr from its sisters, and they [are evaluated] with khamr in terms of prohibition and imposition of hadd. For in khamr, the hadd is obligatory by drinking a drop of it, and a drop of it is prohibited even without reaching the state of intoxication. But other [drinks] are not prohibited and do not necessitate hadd unless one becomes intoxicated. If he has only one mawla, he deserves half of it, and the remaining half is returned to the heirs of the testator. (al-Qamar) 'And the minimum plural in the bequest etc.' I said: because two and above is a group in a bequest as in inheritance, because both are successions after death in ownership. He said in 'Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya': 'The face of considering the minimum plural as two in bequests is not apparent, and qiyas on inheritance is invalid; for it does not necessitate that using a word in a meaning figuratively in one case can be used in a similar case in that meaning, nor ever. Yes, if it is supported by usage, then it has a face.' This is what Bahr al-Uloom said in his commentary on al-Muslim. (al-Sanbali) 'He deserves etc.' because the literal meaning is impossible then, so the speech is carried on the figurative. (al-Qamar) al-Tila' is cooked grape juice, so less than two-thirds of it goes away and it becomes intoxicating, and it is called tila' because of Umar's saying: 'This resembles the tila' of a camel,' which is the tar that is applied to a camel with mange. (al-Qamar) And date infusion: this is sukr, which is the raw of the wet date when it becomes strong and throws foam. (al-Qamar) And raisin infusion: the raw of the raisin water with the condition that it throws foam after boiling. (al-Qamar) 'with khamr' is connected to the negation in his saying 'not annexed,' and likewise his saying 'in terms of' (al-Qamar) 'in terms of prohibition and imposition of hadd etc.' : This is a refutation of an objection that arises against the author's statement: 'And other than khamr is not annexed to khamr,' for it is incorrect in terms of its annexation to it in prohibition. The explanation of the refutation: that other than khamr is annexed to khamr in prohibition only, but not annexed to it in the total of the two rulings, i.e., prohibition and imposition of hadd. (al-Sanbali) 'By drinking a drop of it': because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'If someone drinks khamr, flog him' as recorded by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i. (al-Qamar) And khamr is the raw of grape juice when it boils, becomes strong, and throws foam. If it is not raw but cooked, or if it is from other than grapes, like dates, wheat, honey, and raisin soaked in water, it is not called khamr and does not take its ruling. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) calls all of them khamr, based on the fact that it is derived from covering the intellect, and this applies to all. And the sons of his son are not intended in the bequest to his sons: a third derivative, i.e., if someone bequeaths to the sons of Zayd, and he has sons and grandsons (sons of sons), the sons enter into the bequest, but the grandsons do not enter, because the word 'son' is literal for a son and figurative for a grandson, so it cannot be combined with the literal. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the grandsons also enter, because the word is applied to them, so it includes them due to the apparent [meaning]. General figurative. al-Niy: with kasra of the first letter and shadda of the ya, i.e., raw, uncooked. (al-Qamar) 'When it boils' i.e., its bottom becomes its top. (al-Qamar) 'and becomes strong' i.e., such that it becomes capable of causing intoxication. (al-Qamar) 'and throws foam' i.e., it throws froth and removes it, so it becomes clear and settles. The condition of throwing foam is because it is the completion of intensification and boiling. This is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). As for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong it becomes khamr and throwing foam is not required. This is as al-Birjandi said. (al-Qamar) 'and al-Shafi'i etc.' and Imam Muhammad agrees with him (may Allah have mercy on them) said: All intoxicating beverages are forbidden, a little and a lot of them. So khamr either refers to what covers the intellect, thus includes all, or the intended meaning of khamr in the verse is, by way of general figurative, what covers the intellect, as indicated by the hadiths recorded in the Sahih collections which rule that whatever intoxicates, a jar of it, then a sip of it is forbidden. Thus there is no combination of literal and figurative. Therefore the scholars gave fatwa according to the saying of Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). (al-Qamar) 'Based on the fact that it is etc.' because in Sahih al-Bukhari our master Umar said in a sermon on the pulpit of the Messenger: 'Khamr is what covers the intellect.' He said in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'It is said khamara means it mixed with it.' And Sulayman al-Jamal said in his supercommentary on Tafsir al-Jalalayn: 'It is called khamr because it covers the intellect, i.e., it mixes with it, and it is said because it conceals and covers it.' (al-Qamar) 'Based on what preceded' i.e., on his saying 'the bequest etc.' (al-Qamar) 'And the two said' i.e., Imam Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) and Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). (al-Qamar) 'So it includes them etc.' i.e., the word 'sons' also includes grandsons, but only in the case where there is only one son. For when he uses the plural form, i.e., 'sons', while knowing that there is no son except one, it is known that he intended a more general meaning that includes the descendants, i.e., grandsons also. In the case where there are two sons, they are not included by agreement, because there is no evidence indicating the intended figurative meaning. (al-Sanbali)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary explains the principle that haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (figurative meaning) cannot be combined in a single usage. The author demonstrates this through three fiqh examples. First, in bequests (wasaya), if someone bequeaths to his mawali (clients freed slaves), the word mawali is plural and the minimum plural in bequests is two, as per the Hanafi position. If there is only one mawla, he gets half of the one-third (the bequest portion), and the remainder goes back to the heirs. This is because the word mawali is literally plural and cannot be used figuratively for a singular while also retaining its literal plural meaning. Second, the ruling on khamr (wine) and other intoxicants. Khamr strictly is raw grape juice that ferments. Other intoxicants like date wine or raisin wine are not legally khamr and do not carry the same hadd penalty unless intoxication occurs. However, they share the prohibition. Al-Shafi'i considers all intoxicants as khamr based on the linguistic derivation from 'covering the intellect'. The Hanafi response maintains that the word khamr is specific to grape wine based on the primary usage (istilah), and other drinks are not included unless they cause intoxication. The third example is a bequest 'to the sons of Zayd'. According to Abu Hanifa, this only includes direct sons, not grandsons, because 'ibn' is literal for son and figurative for grandson, and both cannot be intended. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if there is only one direct son, the plural 'sons' shows the testator intended a broader meaning including grandsons, but if there are two or more direct sons, only they are included. The passage emphasizes that in usul al-fiqh, one cannot intend both the literal and figurative sense from a single word, and context (qarina) determines if figurative meaning is intended.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqīqah): Literal or real meaning of a word as originally assigned.
مجاز (majāz): Figurative or metaphorical meaning that is not the original one.
خمر (khamr): Specifically raw grape juice that boils and intoxicates; does not include other intoxicants.
ابن (ibn): Literally 'son'; can be used figuratively for grandson when context warrants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a testator bequeaths to 'my sons' and he has one son and several grandsons, which of the following is correct?
A) Only the direct son inherits, because 'ibn' is haqiqah for son.
B) Both the son and grandsons inherit, because the plural indicates general offspring.
C) Only the grandsons inherit, because the son is already assumed.
D) The bequest is invalid due to uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined ensures legal terms retain their primary meanings unless compelling context (qarina) dictates otherwise, directly influencing rulings on bequests, criminal penalties, and family relationships.

8 min
Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz
Establishes the usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be simultaneously intended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that a word cannot intend both literal and metaphorical meaning in a single usage, and apply it to evaluate the Shafi'i argument regarding Quran 4:43 and derive the resulting ruling on wudu nullification.

Core Idea
A word cannot simultaneously intend its literal and metaphorical meanings; to claim otherwise is rationally impossible.

This lesson explores a foundational disagreement in usul al-fiqh: can a single divine utterance carry both its literal and figurative meanings? The dispute centers on Quran 4:43 'or you have touched women'. Imam al-Shafi'i argued that both meanings are operative, yielding two legal rulings. The Hanafi school counters with a logical principle: combining haqiqah and majaz in one intention is impossible. Understanding this principle is key to mastering Hanafi textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed text and linguistic ambiguity
Recognize that the word 'lamastum' in Quran 4:43 has a literal meaning (touching with the hand) and a metaphorical meaning (intercourse). This ambiguity is the root of the ikhtilaf.
The Arabic word can bear both meanings depending on context.
2
State the Shafi'i position on the verse
Imam al-Shafi'i claims that both the literal and metaphorical meanings are intended in the verse. The literal touch would nullify wudu (so tayammum for hadath), and the metaphorical intercourse would require tayammum for janabah.
Al-Shafi'i sees two distinct legal effects from one expression.
3
Present the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) is intended by consensus (ijma) between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i himself. Therefore, it is impossible to also intend the literal meaning, because combining haqiqah and majaz in one utterance is rationally impossible (istihalah).
The consensus is not the technical ijma of all scholars, but agreement between the two schools on the metaphorical meaning.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The disagreement yields different fiqh rulings: Hanafi – touching women without intercourse does not nullify wudu; Shafi'i – it does nullify wudu. Trace how each school derives its ruling from the same verse.
This shows how usul principles directly affect fiqh outcomes.
5
Generalize the usul principle
Whenever a word has both a literal and a metaphorical meaning, only one can be operative in a given context. If evidence (such as consensus or clear context) establishes the metaphorical, the literal is abandoned. The same applies in reverse when the literal is fixed.
This principle is applied consistently across Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز ولا يراد اللمس باليد في قوله تعالى: أَوْ لامَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ عطف على ما قبله، وتفريع رابع؛ وذلك لأن "لامستم" حقيقة في اللمس باليد ومجاز في الجماع، فالشافعي حلبه يقول: إن كليهما مراد ههنا؛ لأن الله تعالى قال: أَوْ لا مَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَاءً فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيداً طَيِّيان، فإن كان اللمس باليد فالتيمم فيه لأجل الحدث، فيكون لمس النساء ناقضا للوضوء، وإن كان اللمس بالجماع، فالتيمم فيه لأجل الجنابة، فيحل تيمم الجنب بهذه الآية، ونحن نقول: إن المجاز ههنا مراد بالإجماع بيننا وبينكم، فلا يجوز أن تراد الحقيقة أيضًا؛ لاستحالة الجمع بينهما، فلا يكون اللمس باليد ناقضا للوضوء حتى يكون التيمم خلفا عنه، بل إنما هو خلف عن الجنابة فقط، فالأمثلة الثلاثة الأول الحقيقة فيها معينة، فلا يصار إلى المجاز، والمثال الأخير أي الجماع أي المعنى الحقيقي التيمم المجاز فيه متعين فلا يصار إلى الحقيقة وهذا معنى قوله: لأن الحقيقة فيما سوى الأخير، والمجاز فيه مراد، فلم يبق الآخر مرادًا أي المعنى الحقيقي في الأمثلة الثلاثة الأول والمعنى المجازي في المثال الأخير مراد فلم يبق المعنى الآخر أعني المجاز في الأول، والحقيقة في الأخير مرادًا على ما حررناه. أي الجماع أي في المثال الأخير كذا على ما قبله أي على قوله: إن الوصية إلخ. (القمر) يقول إلخ كما نقله الغزالي له عن الشافعي له كذا قيل. (القمر) فيحل تيمم إلخ وابن مسعود مما لم يجز التيمم للجنابة، فاحتج عليه أبو موسى الأشعري ما بهذه الآية لجواز التيمم للجنب وقبلها ابن مسعود، فاتفقا على أنه يحل التيمم للجنب بهذه الآية، فالمراد بالملامسة. الجماع قال بحر العلوم أي مولانا عبد العلي محله. (القمر) بيننا وبينكم لما قال صاحب "التنقيح": إن المجاز ههنا مراد بالإجماع فورد عليه إنا لا نسلم الإجماع، فإن الصحابة كابن العاص يريدون بالملامسة اللمس باليد ولا يجوزون التيمم للجنابة، فأين الإجماع، فزاد الشارح، لفظ بيننا وبينكم إيماء إلى أن المراد ليس الإجماع الاصطلاحي بل الاتفاق بيننا وبين الشافعي له، فإنه حمل الملامسة على المس باليد والجماع كليهما. (القمر) فلا يكون إلخ لأن الشافعي له يحتج على كون لمس النساء باليد ناقضا للوضوء بهذه الآية، وقد عرفت أن المعنى الحقيقي ليس مراد فيها. (القمر) والمثال الأخير: أي قوله تعالى: أَوْ لامَستُمُ النِّسَاءَ) (النساء: ٤٣). (القمر) الثلاثة الأول: وهي الوصية للموالي، وإلحاق غير الخمر بها، والوصية لأبناء فلان [ فتح الغفار: ۱۰۳] في الأول : أي في الأمثلة الثلاثة الأول. (القمر) بعض
Explanation of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning: And [the meaning of] touching with the hand is not intended in His statement 'or you have touched women' as an apposition to what preceded it, and a fourth branch. That is because 'lamastum' is literal for touching with the hand and metaphorical for intercourse. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) says that both are intended here, because Allah said 'or you have touched women and do not find water, then perform tayammum with clean earth'. If it is touching with the hand, then tayammum is for hadath, so touching women would nullify wudu. If it is intercourse, then tayammum is for janabah, so tayammum for the junub is permitted by this verse. We say: The metaphorical meaning is intended here by consensus between us and you, so it is not permissible to also intend the literal meaning, because combining them is impossible. Thus touching with the hand does not nullify wudu such that tayammum would be a substitute for it; rather it is only a substitute for janabah. So in the first three examples the literal meaning is specified, so the metaphorical is not resorted to, and in the last example [intercourse] the metaphorical is determined so the literal is not resorted to. This is the meaning of his saying: because the literal is in the other than the last, and the metaphorical is intended in it, so the other is not intended, i.e., the literal meaning in the first three examples and the metaphorical meaning in the last example are intended, so the other meaning, i.e., the metaphorical in the first and the literal in the last, is not intended, as we have verified.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a classic Hanafi usul dispute over the impossibility of combining literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in a single utterance. The context is the interpretation of Quran 4:43: 'or you have touched women.' The Shafi'i school, represented here by Imam al-Shafi'i, argues that both the literal meaning (touching with the hand) and the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) are intended in the verse. Their reasoning is that if it meant literal touch, tayammum would be for minor ritual impurity (hadath), implying that touching women nullifies wudu. If it meant intercourse, tayammum would be for major impurity (janabah). They see the verse as supporting both rulings. The Hanafi response is decisive: the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) is intended by consensus—but this consensus is not the technical ijma of scholars; rather it is the agreement between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i himself, as the commentator clarifies. Since the metaphorical meaning is intended, it is impossible to also intend the literal meaning. Therefore, the verse does not establish that touching women with the hand nullifies wudu; tayammum from this verse is only a substitute for janabah. The author then compares this to three other examples (the bequest to mawali, analogy of non-wine to wine, and bequest to someone's sons) where the literal meaning is clear. In those, the metaphorical is not adopted. But in the last example (the verse), the metaphorical is fixed, so the literal is abandoned. This illustrates the principle that one cannot simultaneously intend the literal and metaphorical senses of a word.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word; the meaning for which it was coined.
المجاز (al-majaz): The metaphorical, secondary meaning of a word; used when the literal is not intended.
الاستحالة (al-istihalah): Impossibility, here referring to the rational impossibility of combining two contradictory meanings in one utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why cannot the literal meaning of 'lamastum' (touch) be intended alongside the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) in Quran 4:43?
A) Because the literal meaning is not valid at all in this context.
B) Because it is impossible to combine literal and metaphorical meanings in one utterance.
C) Because the consensus (ijma) of all scholars specifies only the literal meaning.
D) Because the verse was abrogated by another verse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined is applied to Quran 4:43, yielding the ruling that touching women does not nullify wudu; only the metaphorical meaning (intercourse) is intended.
