The Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: A Case Study from Aman
An usul principle tested against a fiqh application on aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined to a fiqh ruling on aman, and explain how shubhah and caution in matters of blood allow extension without violating the principle.

Core Idea
When preserving blood, a word's apparent form creates a shubhah that extends the ruling without intending metaphorical meaning.

This passage confronts a potential contradiction between a core usul principle and a fiqh ruling. If one cannot intend both literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously, how can a request for aman for 'sons' include grandsons? The Hanafi answer reveals the subtle role of shubhah (semblance) and the gravity of blood protection. We will walk through the reasoning and then examine an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether similar logic applies to grandparents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
The text begins by reaffirming the rule that one cannot combine haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) in a single usage. This is the principle under attack.
This principle is axiomatic in Hanafi usul.
2
Recognize the fiqh case
A non-Muslim (harbi) asks the Imam for aman for his 'sons' (abna) and 'clients' (mawali). The question: do grandsons and second-tier clients enter under these terms?
Sons' sons are a metaphorical extension of 'sons'.
3
Analyze the proposed resolution
The text answers that the entry is not by intending the metaphorical meaning, but because the apparent form of the word (zahir al-ism) creates a shubhah that necessitates caution in protecting blood. Thus they enter without being intended.
Shubhah is a semblance of reality, not reality itself, but it carries legal weight in blood matters.
4
Consider the objection
An objector asks: if shubhah works for sons and clients, why does it not also include grandparents under 'fathers and mothers'? This tests the consistency of the reasoning.
The objection targets the principle's uniform application.
5
Examine the response and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The response distinguishes between branches (furu') and roots (usul) in creation. Grandchildren are branches both in usage and creation, so they follow; grandparents are roots in creation, so they cannot follow. However, there is a dispute: two riwayat exist, one allowing grandparents to enter, preferred by the commentator based on a report from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifah.
The dispute shows the principle is not absolute; context and qarinah matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز ولما فرغ عن التفريعات شرع في رد اعتراضات ترد على هذه القاعدة، فقال: وفي الاستثمان على الأبناء والموالي تدخل الفروع جواب سؤال مقدر تقريره: أن يقال إذا استأمن الحربي من الإمام وقال آمنونا على أبنائنا وموالينا، يدخل في الأبناء أبناء الأبناء، وفي الموالي موالي الموالي مع أن أبناء الأبناء محاز في لفظ الابن وموالي الموالي محاز في الموالي فيلزم اجتماع الحقيقية والمجاز فأجاب بأنه إنما تدخل الفروع في هذا الاستثمان، لأن ظاهر الاسم صار شبهة في حقن الدم لا أنه يدخل في الإرادة، فالإرادة بالذات إنما هو للأبناء والموالي بلا واسطة، لكن لما كان لفظ الأبناء يتناول ظاهرًا لأبناء الأبناء في قوله تعالى: يَا بَنِي آدَمَ (الأعراف: ٢٦) وكذا لفظ الموالي يطلق عرفا على موالي الموالي، فلأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم يدخلون بلا إرادة. ويرد على هذا الجواب اعتراض وهو أنه ينبغي أن يعتبر مثل هذه الشبهة لأجل الاحتياط في حفظ الدم فيما إذا استأمن على الآباء والأمهات، فيدخل فيه الأجداد والجدات؛ لأن لفظ الآباء والأمهات أيضًا يتناول بظاهر الاسم للأجداد والجدات
على هذه القاعدة أي استحالة إرادة المعنى الحقيقي والمجازي معًا. (القمر) بأنه إلخ ويجاب بأنه من استأمن على أبنائه إنما يستأمن لإبقاء النسل، فهذه قرينة على أن المراد بالأبناء مطلق الفروع، فيتناول الأبناء أبناء الأبناء على سبيل عموم المجاز، وقس عليه الاستثمان على الموالي. (القمر) لأن ظاهر الاسم إلخ يعني أن ظاهر اسم الأبناء والموالي بسبب إطلاقه على أبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي صار شبهة أي أمرًا يشابه الحق فيثبت الأمان بحقن الدم، قيل: الشبهة ما يشبه الثابت وليس بثابت حقيقة) فإن الأصل في الدماء أن تكون محقونة أي محفوظة (القمر) لأن ظاهر الاسم إلخ حاصل الجواب: أنه لم يرد الحفدة بلفظ الابن لكن الاحتياط في حقن الدم أوجب الدخول في الأمان تبعًا لوجود شبهة الحقيقة بالاستعمال الشائع نحو: بنو آدم، وبنو هاشم، فعلم كذا، والأمان مما يثبت بالشبهة؛ لأن أمر الدم ليس سهلاً.(السنبلي) يطلق عرفًا إلخ: فإن معتق المعتق للرجل ينسب إليه مجازا؛ لأنه سبب لعتقه بإعتاقه الأول.(القمر) يدخلون إلخ : فإن الأمان يثبت بالشبهة أيضًا. (القمر)

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز فأجاب المصنف الله عنه بقوله: بخلاف الاستثمان على الآباء والأمهات حيث لا يدخل الأجداد والجدات؛ لأن ذا بطريق التبعية فيليق بالفروع دون الأصول يعني أن هذا أي الدخول التناول الظاهري إنما هو بطريق التبعية للمذكور فيليق هذا بأبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي؛ أي الشيء المذكور لأنهم فروع في الإطلاق والخلقة جميعًا دون الأجداد والجدات؛ لأنهم وإن كانوا فروعًا إطلاق الاسم للآباء والأمهات في إطلاق اللفظ، ولكنهم أصول في الخلقة، فكيف يتبعونهم في اللفظ، وإنما تسري الكتابة إلى أبيه فيما إذا اشترى المكاتب أباه لا لأنه دخول بالتبعية؛ لأنه ليس هنا لفظ يدخل فيه تبعا بل تحقيقا للصلة والإحسان، فإن الحر إذا اشترى أباه يكون حراً أي في الكتابة لا يدخل إلخ :قلت مختلف فيه، ففي رواية يدخل وهو ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية لا يدخل، وأخذ هذه الرواية مصنف "المنار"، والوجه الذي بينها خلاصته أن دخول الحفدة كان تبعًا، ودخول الأجداد والجدات إن كان فبالتبع أيضًا، وهم أصول خلقة، فلا يدخلون بالتبع قال بحر العلوم وهذا الوجه ليس بشيء؛ لأن الأصالة في الخلقة لا ينافي التبعية في أحكام أخر مع أنه قال في "الهداية": الأم لغة الأصل، فحينئذ الدخول بالذات لا بالتبع، فإذن الأشبه الرواية الأولى له دخول الأجداد والجدات في الآباء والأمهات وإن كانت الرواية الثانية ظاهر الرواية وههنا وجه آخر أسهل من الأول :وهو أن الظاهر أن الرجل لا يؤثر حياة نفسه وأبنائه دون أبناء أبنائه، فهم يدخلون بدلالة النص، لكن الظاهر أن الأجداد والجدات أيضًا يدخلون بالدلالة، اللهم إلا أن يكونوا مفسدين ذوي رأي، فيعلم أن الإمام لا يأمن مثلهم، فيخرجون عن الأمان، ولعل هذا مشترك بينهم وبين الحفدة (السنبلي) هذا : أي التناول الظاهري والتبعية (القمر) وإن كانوا فروعًا إلخ : فإن لفظ الأب يطلق أصالة على الأب، وإنما يطلق على الجد للملابسة، فصار هذا الإطلاق فرعًا، وكذا لفظ الأم يطلق على الأم أصالة، وإنما يطلق على أم الأب وأم الأم للملابسة، فصار فرعًا. (القمر) ولكنهم إلخ فيه أن الأصلية في الخلقة لا ينافي التبعية في الأمان، فالأظهر ما رواه الحسن عن الإمام أبي حنيفة بالله : أن الأجداد والجدات يدخلون في أمان الأب والأم كذا قال بحر العلوم أي مولانا عبد العلي له. (القمر) فكيف يتبعونهم أي الأجداد والجدات والآباء والأمهات (القمر) وإنما تسري إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر وهو: أن المكاتب إذا اشترى أباه صار الأب مكاتبًا عليه، فيتبع الأب مع كونه أصلاً للابن المكاتب. (القمر) بل تحقيقا للصلة: أي لصلة الرحم، فإن الإنسان مأمور بالإحسان بوالديه فهذه السراية -أي سراية الكتابة إلى الأب بالأمر الحكمي لا باعتبار لفظ يدل عليها، فلم يكن هذا من قبيل ما نحن فيه. (القمر)
Statement of the third division: Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning. When he finished with the subdivisions, he began refuting objections that come against this principle. He said: In seeking amān for sons and clients, the offshoots (i.e., grandchildren etc.) enter. This is an answer to an implied question: if a ḥarbī seeks amān from the Imam and says 'Grant us amān for our sons and our clients', then their sons' sons enter under 'sons', and their clients' clients enter under 'clients', even though sons' sons are metaphorical in the word 'son', and clients' clients are metaphorical in 'client', thus combining literal and metaphorical. He answered: The offshoots only enter in this amān because the apparent form of the name became a shubhah (doubt) for the sake of protecting blood, not because they are intended. The primary intention is only for sons and clients directly. But because the word 'sons' apparently encompasses sons' sons in His statement 'O Children of Adam' (al-A'raf 26), and likewise the word 'clients' is commonly used for clients of clients, therefore, due to caution in preserving blood, they enter without intention. An objection is made to this answer: that such a shubhah should be considered for caution in preserving blood when amān is sought for fathers and mothers, so that grandfathers and grandmothers would enter, because the word 'fathers' and 'mothers' also apparently encompasses grandfathers and grandmothers. Regarding this principle, i.e., the impossibility of intending literal and metaphorical meaning together. (al-Qamar) 'because the apparent form etc.' It is answered that one who seeks amān for his sons only seeks amān to preserve progeny, so this is a qarīnah (indication) that by 'sons' he means absolute descendants, so 'sons' includes sons' sons by way of general metaphor, and analogously for amān on clients. (al-Qamar) 'because the apparent form etc.' means that the apparent form of the word 'sons' and 'clients', due to being applied to sons' sons and clients of clients, became a shubhah, i.e., something resembling the truth, so amān is established for protecting blood. It is said: shubhah is what resembles what is established but is not actually established. The principle regarding blood is that it is protected (i.e., preserved). (al-Qamar) 'because the apparent form etc.' The summary of the answer: he did not intend descendants by the word 'son', but caution in protecting blood necessitated entry into amān as a follow-up due to the existence of a shubhah of literalness through common usage like 'Banī Ādam' and 'Banī Hāshim'. Thus it is known, and amān is established by shubhah because the matter of blood is not easy. (al-Sanbalī) 'commonly used etc.': for the freed slave of one's freed slave is metaphorically attributed to the man because he is the cause of his freedom through his initial freeing. (al-Qamar) 'they enter etc.': for amān is also established by shubhah. (al-Qamar)

Statement of the third division: Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning. Then the author (may Allah forgive him) answered by saying: Unlike seeking amān for fathers and mothers, where grandfathers and grandmothers do not enter, because this is by way of taba'iyyah (secondary status) and is suitable for descendants not ascendants. That is, this apparent encompassment is only by way of following the mentioned term, so it suits sons' sons and clients' clients because they are branches both in usage and in creation, unlike grandfathers and grandmothers because, although they are branches in the usage of the words 'fathers' and 'mothers', they are roots in creation, so how can they follow them in wording? And kitābah (contract of manumission) extends to his father when the mukātib buys his father, not because it is an entry by taba'iyyah, because here there is no word under which it would enter as a follow-up, but rather to realize connection and beneficence. For if a free person buys his father, he becomes free. (i.e., in kitābah) 'does not enter etc.' I say: it is disputed. In one riwāyah he enters, and that is the apparent riwāyah; in another riwāyah he does not enter, and the author of al-Manār adopted this riwāyah. The rationale he gave, in summary, is that the entry of descendants was as a follow-up, and the entry of grandfathers and grandmothers, if it were, would also be as a follow-up, and they are roots in creation, so they do not enter as a follow-up. Baḥr al-ʿUlūm said: This rationale is nothing, because being original in creation does not negate following in other rulings, especially since he said in al-Hidāyah: 'Mother is linguistically the root.' Thus the entry is by essence not by following. Therefore, the first riwāyah is more appropriate, that grandfathers and grandmothers enter under 'fathers' and 'mothers', even though the second riwāyah is the apparent riwāyah. And here is another easier explanation: it is apparent that a man does not prefer his own life and his sons' lives over his grandsons' lives, so they enter by dalālat al-naṣṣ (implication of the text). But it is also apparent that grandfathers and grandmothers also enter by dalālah, unless they are troublemakers with opinions, then it is known that the Imam would not feel safe with such people, so they are excluded from amān. Perhaps this is common between them and the grandchildren. (al-Sanbalī) 'this': i.e., the apparent encompassment and following. (al-Qamar) 'although they are branches etc.': for the word 'father' is primarily applied to the father, and is only applied to the grandfather due to similarity, so this application is secondary. Similarly, the word 'mother' is primarily applied to the mother, and is only applied to the grandmother due to similarity, so it is secondary. (al-Qamar) 'but they are etc.' It contains that being original in creation does not negate following in amān. So the most apparent is what al-Ḥasan reported from Imam Abū Ḥanīfah (may Allah be pleased with him): that grandfathers and grandmothers enter in the amān of the father and mother, as said by Baḥr al-ʿUlūm, i.e., our master ʿAbd al-ʿAlī. (al-Qamar) 'so how can they follow them' i.e., the grandfathers and grandmothers follow the fathers and mothers. (al-Qamar) 'and kitābah extends etc.': repelling an implied objection: that when the mukātib buys his father, the father becomes a mukātib under him, so the father follows despite being a root for the son-mukātib. (al-Qamar) 'but to realize connection': i.e., to maintain family ties, for a person is commanded to be good to his parents. Thus this extension (i.e., extension of kitābah to the father) is by a prescriptive command, not by consideration of a word indicating it, so it is not of the type we are discussing. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of a fiqh ruling regarding aman (safe conduct). The author is demonstrating the application of the principle that one cannot simultaneously intend both the literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings of a word. The specific case is when a non-Muslim (harbi) asks the Imam for aman on behalf of his 'sons' (abna) and 'clients' (mawali). The question is whether this includes grandchildren (abna al-abna) and clients of clients (mawali al-mawali), since these are metaphorical extensions of the terms. The text presents a resolution: the inclusion is not due to intending the metaphorical meaning, but because the apparent form of the word creates a shubhah (a semblance of reality) that requires caution in protecting blood. Thus, they enter without being directly intended. An objection is raised: if shubhah operates here, it should also include grandfathers and grandmothers under 'fathers and mothers'. The response differentiates: for sons, the context of preserving progeny suggests a general meaning (qarinah), making it a general metaphor. For fathers and mothers, since they are origins in creation, they cannot enter as a follow-up (taba'iyyah). The passage then discusses a dispute (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school: two riwayat, one that grandfathers enter and one that they don't. The commentator notes that the first riwayah (grandparents enter) is more appropriate, citing Baḥr al-ʿUlūm and a report from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifah. The discussion also distinguishes the case of kitabah (contract of manumission) where a mukatib who buys his father causes the father to become a mukatib, not due to linguistic inclusion but due to the command to honor parents.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal or original meaning of a word, used in its primary sense.
المجاز (al-majaz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, used in a secondary sense due to a relationship.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A semblance of truth or a doubt that resembles the established reality; it can be a basis for legal rulings especially in matters of blood (dam).
الاستئمان (al-isti'man): The act of seeking (or granting) aman (safe conduct) from the Imam by a non-Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis in this passage, why do grandchildren enter under the word 'sons' in a request for aman, without requiring the combination of literal and metaphorical intention?
A) Because the word 'sons' is inherently ambiguous and can mean both.
B) Because the apparent form of the word creates a shubhah (semblance) that necessitates caution in protecting blood.
C) Because the Imam intends both meanings equally.
D) Because the person seeking aman explicitly mentions grandchildren.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz is resolved in fiqh applications by invoking shubhah and caution in blood matters, allowing extension of rulings without violating the principle.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
The Principle: No Simultaneous Haqiqah and Majaz in Oaths
Responds to objections about combining literal and metaphorical meaning in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that a single utterance cannot simultaneously bear its literal and metaphorical meanings, using the Hanafi example of an oath about placing the foot in a house.

Core Idea
A term used in an oath applies to its literal and metaphorical senses individually, not together — the oath is broken by either.

The passage addresses a common objection: if a person swears not to place his foot in a house, does he break the oath if he enters barefoot (literal) or shod (metaphorical)? Some object that this requires combining haqiqah and majaz. The Hanafi response clarifies that the utterance applies to each sense separately, not simultaneously. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application to oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize that the questioner claims that if an oath covers both literal and metaphorical meanings, it would require combining haqiqah and majaz simultaneously, which is impossible.
The objection is based on the assumption that a single utterance can only have one meaning in a given context.
2
State the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi position is that a single utterance cannot be used in both its literal and metaphorical senses at once, but it can be applied to each sense in separate instances.
This is not a combination of meanings; each application is independent.
3
Analyze the oath scenario
The phrase 'placing the foot' (wad' al-qadam) has a literal meaning (barefoot) and a metaphorical meaning (shod). The oath is considered applied to both, but as separate occurrences, not as a single combined use.
The oath is broken if the person enters barefoot or shod, because each is a valid instance of 'placing the foot.'
4
Apply to the ownership context
Similarly, 'house of so-and-so' (dar fulan) literally means owned by him, and metaphorically means rented or borrowed. The oath applies to both meanings separately.
The oath is broken by entering either the owned house or the rented house.
5
Conclude the principle
The Hanafi ruling that the oath is broken in both cases does not violate the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz, because the utterance is not used to convey both meanings simultaneously but rather is interpreted in context.
This principle prevents confusion when a single term has multiple valid meanings.

بخلاف الاستثمان على الآباء والأمهات حيث لا يدخل الأجداد والجدات؛ لأن ذا بطريق التبعية فيليق بالفروع دون الأصول يعني أن هذا التناول الظاهري إنما هو بطريق التبعية للمذكور فيليق هذا بأبناء الأبناء وموالي الموالي؛ لأنهم فروع في الإطلاق والخلقة جميعًا دون الأجداد والجدات؛ لأنهم وإن كانوا فروعًا في إطلاق اللفظ، ولكنهم أصول في الخلقة، فكيف يتبعونهم في اللفظ، وإنما تسري الكتابة إلى أبيه فيما إذا اشترى المكاتب أباه لا لأنه دخول بالتبعية؛ لأنه ليس هنا لفظ يدخل فيه تبعا بل تحقيقا للصلة والإحسان، فإن الحر إذا اشترى أباه يكون حراً لا يدخل إلخ :قلت مختلف فيه، ففي رواية يدخل وهو ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية لا يدخل، وأخذ هذه الرواية مصنف "المنار"، والوجه الذي بينها خلاصته أن دخول الحفدة كان تبعًا، ودخول الأجداد والجدات إن كان فبالتبع أيضًا، وهم أصول خلقة، فلا يدخلون بالتبع قال بحر العلوم وهذا الوجه ليس بشيء؛ لأن الأصالة في الخلقة لا ينافي التبعية في أحكام أخر مع أنه قال في "الهداية": الأم لغة الأصل، فحينئذ الدخول بالذات لا بالتبع، فإذن الأشبه الرواية الأولى له دخول الأجداد والجدات في الآباء والأمهات وإن كانت الرواية الثانية ظاهر الرواية وههنا وجه آخر أسهل من الأول :وهو أن الظاهر أن الرجل لا يؤثر حياة نفسه وأبنائه دون أبناء أبنائه، فهم يدخلون بدلالة النص، لكن الظاهر أن الأجداد والجدات أيضًا يدخلون بالدلالة، اللهم إلا أن يكونوا مفسدين ذوي رأي، فيعلم أن الإمام لا يأمن مثلهم، فيخرجون عن الأمان، ولعل هذا مشترك بينهم وبين الحفدة (السنبلي) هذا : أي التناول الظاهري والتبعية (القمر) وإن كانوا فروعًا إلخ : فإن لفظ الأب يطلق أصالة على الأب، وإنما يطلق على الجد للملابسة، فصار هذا الإطلاق فرعًا، وكذا لفظ الأم يطلق على الأم أصالة، وإنما يطلق على أم الأب وأم الأم للملابسة، فصار فرعًا. (القمر) ولكنهم إلخ فيه أن الأصلية في الخلقة لا ينافي التبعية في الأمان، فالأظهر ما رواه الحسن عن الإمام أبي حنيفة بالله : أن الأجداد والجدات يدخلون في أمان الأب والأم كذا قال بحر العلوم أي مولانا عبد العلي له. (القمر) فكيف يتبعونهم أي الأجداد والجدات والآباء والأمهات (القمر) وإنما تسري إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر وهو: أن المكاتب إذا اشترى أباه صار الأب مكاتبًا عليه، فيتبع الأب مع كونه أصلاً للابن المكاتب. (القمر) بل تحقيقا للصلة: أي لصلة الرحم، فإن الإنسان مأمور بالإحسان بوالديه فهذه السراية -أي سراية الكتابة إلى الأب بالأمر الحكمي لا باعتبار لفظ يدل عليها، فلم يكن هذا من قبيل ما نحن فيه. (القمر) وأما حرمة نكاح الجدات في قوله تعالى: ﴿حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ﴾ فبالإجماع، أو دلالة النص، أو جعل الأمهات بمعنى الأصول ثمه للاحتياط. أي الآية وإنما يقع على الملك والإجارة والدخول حافيًا أو متنعلا فيما إذا حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان جواب سؤال آخر تقريره أنه إذا حلف شخص لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان فإن حقيقة وضع القدم في الدار أن يكون حافيًا، ومجازه: أن يكون متنعلاً، وقد قلتم إنه يحنث بكلا الأمرين فيلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وأيضا أن حقيقة دار فلان أن تكون بطريق الملك له ومجازه أن يكون بطريق الإجارة والعارية له. وقد قلتم : إنه يحنث بكلا الأمرين، فيلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز من وجه آخر. فأجاب بأنه إنما يقع هذا الحلف على الملك والإجارة جميعًا، وكذا على الدخول حافيًا أو متنعلاً في قوله: "لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان". وأما حرمة إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر هو : أن الجدات داخلة في الأمهات في قوله تعالى: حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَاتُكُمْ) حتى حرم نكاح الجدات من هذه الآية، فدخل الأصول تبعًا للفروع. (القمر) أو جعل إلخ أي على سبيل عموم المجاز. (القمر) أن يكون حافيًا لأن وضع الشيء في الشيء أن يجعل الثاني ظرفًا له بلا واسطة كوضع الدراهم في الكيس كذا في "التلويح".(القمر) ومجازه إلخ بدليل صحة النفي أي ما وضع قدمه فيما إذا كان متنعلاً. (القمر) بكلا الأمرين: أي الدخول حافيًا ومتنعلاً. (القمر) وأيضًا إلخ إيماء إلى أن ورود السؤال ههنا من وجهين.(القمر) ومجازه أن يكون إلخ بدليل صحة النفي أي ليس هذه دار فلان في غير الملك وعدم صحته في الملك.(القمر) بكلا الأمرين: أي كون دار فلان بطريق الملك، وكون داره بطريق الإجارة والعارية. (القمر) على الملك إلخ : أي على الدخول في الدار المملوكة، والدار المسكونة بالإجارة. (القمر)
Contrary to the inclusion of grandparents under the term 'parents' in the context of aman, where grandparents are not included because this inclusion is by way of following (taba'iyyah) and thus befits branches (furū') not roots (uṣūl). This means that this apparent inclusion is only by way of following the mentioned term, so it befits children's children and clients' clients, because they are branches in both naming and creation, unlike grandparents, because although they are branches in the naming, they are roots in creation, so how can they follow them in wording? The writing (kitābah) transfers to the father when a mukātab buys his father, not because of inclusion by following, because there is no wording here to include it by following, but rather to achieve connection and kindness. For a free person if he buys his father, the father becomes free. (The text says: does not enter etc.) I say: there is a difference of opinion. In one narration, he enters, and that is the apparent narration. In another narration, he does not enter, and the author of al-Manār adopted this narration. The gist of the reasoning is that the entry of grandchildren is by following, and the entry of grandparents, if at all, would also be by following, but they are roots in creation, so they do not enter by following. Bahr al-Ulūm said: this reasoning is nothing, because being a root in creation does not negate following in other rulings, especially since it is stated in al-Hidāyah that 'mother' linguistically means root. Therefore, the entry is by essence, not by following. So the more correct is the first narration that grandparents enter under the term 'parents', even if the second narration is the apparent narration. There is another easier aspect: it is apparent that a man does not prefer the life of himself and his sons over the lives of his sons' sons, so they enter by dalālat al-naṣṣ. But it is also apparent that grandparents also enter by dalālah, unless they are corrupt people of opinion, so it is known that the Imam does not feel secure with the likes of them, so they are excluded from the aman. Perhaps this is common between them and grandchildren. (al-Sanbālī). This means the apparent inclusion and following. (al-Qamar) 'Although they are branches etc.': the term 'father' is primarily applied to the biological father, and is only applied to the grandfather due to connection, so this application becomes secondary. Similarly, the term 'mother' is primarily applied to the biological mother, and is only applied to the paternal grandmother and maternal grandmother due to connection, so it becomes secondary. (al-Qamar) 'But they are roots etc.': there is an implication that being a root in creation does not negate following in aman. So the more apparent is what al-Ḥasan narrated from Imam Abū Ḥanīfa: that grandparents enter under the aman of the father and mother. Thus said Bahr al-Ulūm, i.e. Mawlānā ‘Abd al-‘Alī. (al-Qamar). 'So how can they follow them' i.e. grandparents follow the fathers and mothers? (al-Qamar) 'And it transfers etc.': repelling an anticipated objection: that when a mukātab buys his father, the father becomes a mukātab under him, so the father follows despite being a root for the son. (al-Qamar) 'Rather to achieve kindness': i.e. to maintain ties of kinship, for a person is commanded to be kind to his parents. So this transfer - that is, the transfer of kitābah to the father - is by a legal order, not by consideration of a wording that indicates it. So this is not of the type we are discussing. (al-Qamar) 'As for the prohibition of marrying grandmothers in His statement: 'Forbidden to you are your mothers' (Quran 4:23), it is by consensus, or by dalālat al-naṣṣ, or by making 'mothers' mean 'roots' for the purpose of caution.' The answer to another question: its summary is that if a person swears that he will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so, the literal meaning of placing the foot in the house is to be barefoot, and its metaphorical meaning is to be shod. You (the Hanafis) say that he breaks the oath in both cases, so combining literal and metaphorical meaning becomes necessary. Also, the literal meaning of 'house of so-and-so' is that it is owned by him, and its metaphorical meaning is that it is rented or loaned to him. You say that he breaks the oath in both cases, so combining literal and metaphorical meaning is necessary from another angle. So (the commentator) answered that this oath applies to both ownership and renting, and also to entering barefoot or shod, in the oath 'He will not place his foot in the house of so-and-so'. 'As for the prohibition etc.': repelling an anticipated objection: that grandmothers are included in 'mothers' in the verse 'Forbidden to you are your mothers' (4:23) so that the prohibition of marrying grandmothers is from this verse, thus the roots (uṣūl) enter by following the branches. (al-Qamar) 'Or making them mean' i.e. by way of general metaphor. (al-Qamar) 'To be barefoot' because placing something in something is to make the second a container for it without intermediary, like placing dirhams in a purse. Thus in al-Talwīḥ. (al-Qamar) 'Its metaphorical meaning etc.' as proven by the validity of negation: he did not place his foot if he was shod. (al-Qamar) 'In both cases': i.e. entering barefoot and shod. (al-Qamar) 'Also etc.' indicating that the question here comes from two aspects. (al-Qamar) 'Its metaphorical meaning etc.' as proven by the validity of negation: this is not the house of so-and-so if not owned, and its invalidity if owned. (al-Qamar) 'In both cases': i.e. the house being owned and the house being rented or loaned. (al-Qamar) 'On ownership etc.': i.e. on entering the owned house and the rented house. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a commentary responding to objections about combining literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in legal texts. The commentator (likely al-Qamar or al-Sanbālī) addresses two main points: (1) the inclusion of grandparents under 'parents' in the context of aman (security), and (2) the oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house'. The objection is that such oaths would require combining haqiqah and majaz simultaneously, which is impossible. The commentator explains that in the case of aman, grandparents are not included by following (taba'iyyah) because they are roots in creation, though some narrations indicate they are included. For the oath, the answer is that the oath applies to both literal (barefoot, owned) and metaphorical (shod, rented) meanings, but each is a separate application, not a simultaneous combination. The legal effect is that the oath is broken in both scenarios, but the haqiqah and majaz are not combined in a single usage; rather, the utterance is applied individually to each scenario. The passage also discusses the verse on marriage prohibition (4:23) where grandmothers are included under 'mothers' by consensus or by dalālat al-naṣṣ (indication of the text). This demonstrates the usul principle that a word can be used in either its literal or metaphorical sense, but not both in one instance. The Hanafi position relies on the possibility of using a word in multiple contexts (ishrāk lafẓī) without combining meanings in a single application.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal/original meaning of a word as established by linguistic convention.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical/figurative meaning of a word used due to some connection (mula'basah) with the original.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Inclusion by following, where a term includes other items not originally covered, but by virtue of being related to the specified term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed in this lesson, if a person swears not to place his foot in a house, and he enters shod (with shoes), does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because the metaphorical meaning (shod) is a valid separate application of the utterance.
B) No, because only the literal meaning (barefoot) counts.
C) Yes, but only if he intended both meanings simultaneously.
D) No, because entering shod is not 'placing the foot' at all.

Key Takeaway
A single utterance cannot be used in both its literal and metaphorical senses at once, but it can apply to each separately in different contexts — this is the key Hanafi principle for oaths and legal interpretation.

The Dispute: Inclusion of Grandparents under 'Parents' in Aman
Responds to objections about including grandparents in aman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi narrations on including grandparents in the term 'parents' for aman, and evaluate which is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Grandparents are included under 'parents' in aman according to the preferred view, because 'mother' means root — inclusion is by essence, not following.

The passage discusses a practical example: if a commander grants aman (security) to 'fathers and mothers,' are grandparents included? There are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. One excludes them because they are roots in creation, and inclusion would be by following (taba'iyyah), which befits branches. The other includes them, as 'mother' linguistically means 'root.' This lesson analyzes the dispute and its consequence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Aman (security) is granted to specified persons. The question is whether the term 'parents' (al-āba' wa al-ummahāt) includes grandparents in such a grant.
The issue arises from a real scenario in Islamic law of warfare and protection.
2
Identify the two narrations
Imam Abu Hanifa has two narrations: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah states grandparents are NOT included; (2) another narration (from al-Hasan) states they ARE included. The latter is preferred by later scholars.
The existence of two narrations within the Hanafi school shows flexibility.
3
Analyze the proof for inclusion
The preferred view argues that 'mother' in Arabic means root (aṣl), as stated in al-Hidāyah. Thus, grandparents are included by essence (bi al-dhāt), not by following (taba'iyyah).
This linguistic argument is the core of the inclusion position.
4
Analyze the proof for exclusion
Those who exclude argue that grandfather is called 'father' only by association (mulābasah), making the application secondary. As roots in creation, grandparents cannot be followers (furū') in wording.
This view relies on the concept of taba'iyyah, which applies only to branches.
5
Apply tarjih
Bahr al-Ulūm (Mawlānā 'Abd al-‘Alī) prefers inclusion, citing the linguistic root meaning and the narration from al-Hasan. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that grandparents enter.
Even though Zahir al-Riwayah supports exclusion, the later preference gives weight to inclusion.

Contrary to the inclusion of grandparents under the term 'parents' in the context of aman, where grandparents are not included because this inclusion is by way of following (taba'iyyah) and thus befits branches (furū') not roots (uṣūl). Commentary
This passage is a commentary responding to objections about combining literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in legal texts. The commentator (likely al-Qamar or al-Sanbālī) addresses two main points: (1) the inclusion of grandparents under 'parents' in the context of aman (security), and (2) the oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house'. The objection is that such oaths would require combining haqiqah and majaz simultaneously, which is impossible. The commentator explains that in the case of aman, grandparents are not included by following (taba'iyyah) because they are roots in creation, though some narrations indicate they are included. For the oath, the answer is that the oath applies to both literal (barefoot, owned) and metaphorical (shod, rented) meanings, but each is a separate application, not a simultaneous combination. The legal effect is that the oath is broken in both scenarios, but the haqiqah and majaz are not combined in a single usage; rather, the utterance is applied individually to each scenario. The passage also discusses the verse on marriage prohibition (4:23) where grandmothers are included under 'mothers' by consensus or by dalālat al-naṣṣ (indication of the text). This demonstrates the usul principle that a word can be used in either its literal or metaphorical sense, but not both in one instance. The Hanafi position relies on the possibility of using a word in multiple contexts (ishrāk lafẓī) without combining meanings in a single application.

Key Terms
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal/original meaning of a word as established by linguistic convention.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical/figurative meaning of a word used due to some connection (mula'basah) with the original.
تبعية (taba'iyyah): Inclusion by following, where a term includes other items not originally covered, but by virtue of being related to the specified term.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: The Oath of 'Placing Foot'
Explaining the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meanings using an oath example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a literal meaning abandoned in custom (haqiqa mahjura) does not affect the interpretation of an oath, and distinguish between absolute metaphorical generality and technical umum al-majaz.

Core Idea
When a literal meaning is abandoned by custom, the oath is interpreted by the metaphorical meaning alone—combining both is impossible.

This lesson addresses a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul: a single utterance cannot carry both its literal and metaphorical meanings simultaneously. Using the classic oath example of 'placing foot' meaning 'entering', we explore how custom (urf) abandons one meaning and prioritizes another. You will learn the legal effect of a haqiqa mahjura and the precise meaning of 'umum al-majaz'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
The phrase 'placing foot' has a literal meaning (physically placing the foot) and a metaphorical meaning (entering). Your first step is to clearly state both possibilities in the legal context.
The metaphorical meaning is broader, covering all modes of entry.
2
Recognize the conflict
Hanafi usul holds that an utterance cannot simultaneously convey its literal and metaphorical meanings at the time of utterance. This is the principle of 'impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz'.
This is a rule to avoid ambiguity in legal interpretation.
3
Apply the rule to the oath
In the oath 'I will not place my foot in this house', the speaker's purpose is to prevent entering. Since the literal meaning (placing foot without entering) is abandoned by custom (haqiqa mahjura), only the metaphorical meaning (entering) is operative.
The custom of the language determines which meaning is active.
4
Consider the role of intention
If the speaker has a specific intention (e.g., only barefoot entry), the Hanafi scholars differ: some honor the intention as the literal truth, others prioritize the customary metaphor. Understand that the haqiqa mahjura is not operative unless the speaker intends a used meaning.
Intention can be effective only if the intended meaning is still in use.
5
Differentiate 'umum al-majaz'
The passage clarifies that 'umum al-majaz' here means 'absolute metaphorical generality' (unrestricted entering), not the technical term which would require the literal meaning to be an instance of the metaphorical. This distinction prevents false conclusions.
In this oath, the metaphorical meaning is general, covering all types of entry, regardless of mode.

Source Text

(السنبلي) فعلى ما نوى قال ابن الملك: لأنه لو نوى أن لا يضع قدمه حافيًا فدخل متنعلاً، أو ماشيا فدخلها راكبًا، لم يحنث، ويصدق ديانة وقضاء؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وهي مستعملة ولو نوى منه وضع القدم من دخول لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه مهجور غير مستعمل. Statement of the third division: Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning considering the generality of the metaphorical meaning, which is entering. And the attribution of residence, so from his saying: 'He does not place his foot' is intended 'he does not enter', and this is a metaphorical meaning inclusive of entering barefoot or shod, so he breaks his oath by the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. This is if he has no intention; if he has intention, then according to what he intended, barefoot or shod, walking or riding. And if he placed his foot only without entering, he does not break the oath because it is a literal meaning that is abandoned, not operative. And from his saying: this oath is a prohibition of entering etc., because custom witnesses that the purpose is prevention from entering, not from mere placing of the foot. So he breaks the oath by the generality of the metaphor etc. Summary of the answer: that unrestricted entering is intended, so by its generality it includes some instances of literal and metaphorical. And the literal meaning being abandoned in custom in favor of unrestricted entering, and the abandoned literal meaning is left, and the metaphorical is given weight, so that he does not break the oath if he lies down outside and places his feet inside even though he is literally placing. Thus in Fatawa Qadi Khan. He said in al-Kashf, quoting al-Mabsut: If he intended entering barefoot, and he entered riding, he does not break the oath because he intended the literal meaning of his speech, and this is an abandoned literal meaning. And from al-Muhit: If he intended the literal meaning of placing the foot, he does not break the oath by entering riding, because he intended the literal meaning of his speech; he is believed judicially and religiously. On this basis this answer is not valid, but rather it is answered that the contextual evidence indicates that the abandonment is due to dislike of the house, and it prevents unrestricted entering, not placing the foot only. As for if he intends, then according to what he intended, because it is the literal meaning of his speech, so reflect. (al-Sunbuli) 'according to what he intended' Ibn al-Malik said: Because if he intended not to place his foot barefoot and he entered shod, or walking and he entered riding, he does not break the oath, and he is believed religiously and judicially because he intended the literal meaning of his speech and it is used. But if he intended from it placing the foot without entering, he is not believed judicially because it is abandoned, not used. (al-Qamar) 'without entering' such that he lies down with his feet in the house and the rest of his body outside the house. (al-Qamar) 'he does not break the oath' etc. according to what is in Fatawa Qadi Khan, and from here it appears that what the author meant by 'considering the generality of the metaphor' is absolute metaphor, i.e., the metaphorical meaning being absolute, not restricted by any condition. And it is not intended to be the technical generality of metaphor, because one of its conditions is that the literal meaning is an instance of the metaphorical meaning, so if that were meant, it would necessitate that he break the oath in this picture. (al-Qamar) 'abandoned' because from placing the foot customarily only entering is understood. (al-Qamar) 'and it is intended' etc. because the house does not show enmity nor is it abandoned for itself, but for dislike of its inhabitant. Thus in al-Talwih, and it contains: that the house may be messy so it is disliked for itself, and it can be said that the oath with the attribution of the house to Zayd is contextual evidence that the oath's intention is the abandonment of the house due to dislike of its inhabitant, so ponder.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meanings in a single utterance, using the example of an oath. The author begins by stating that the oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house' is understood metaphorically: 'placing foot' means 'entering'. This metaphorical meaning is general, covering all modes of entry (barefoot, shod, walking, riding). Therefore, the oath is broken by any entry under this general metaphor, not by combining the literal (placing foot) and metaphorical (entering) in the same utterance. If the person had a specific intention (e.g., only barefoot entry), then he is judged according to his intention, as long as it is a valid interpretation. However, if he merely places his foot without entering (e.g., lying down with feet inside), he does not break the oath because the literal meaning of 'placing foot' is abandoned in common usage; customarily, it is understood as entering. The passage then presents a reconciliation from Fatawa Qadi Khan: the intended meaning is unrestricted entry, so the general metaphorical expression covers both literal and metaphorical instances. The literal meaning is set aside in favor of the metaphorical due to custom. The discussion includes differences among Hanafi scholars: in al-Mabsut, if one intends barefoot entry and enters riding, the oath is not broken because he intended the literal truth of his words (even though the literal meaning is abandoned). But this is problematic; a better answer is that the contextual evidence (the house being disliked) indicates the prohibition is against entering entirely, not just placing foot. So the oath is broken regardless of mode. For intention, the person is believed both judicially and religiously if he intended a used meaning (like entering barefoot vs shod), but not if he intended an abandoned meaning (like placing foot without entering). The passage concludes by clarifying that 'عموم المجاز' here means 'absolute metaphor', not the technical 'general metaphor' which would require the literal to be an instance of the metaphorical. This distinction prevents false conclusions.

Key Terms
حقيقة مهجورة (haqiqa mahjura): A literal meaning that has fallen out of use in common custom; it is not operative in interpretation of oaths unless specifically intended.
مجاز (majaz): A meaning conveyed figuratively, here the general meaning of 'entering' from the expression 'placing foot'.
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): The generality of the metaphorical meaning, i.e., the metaphorical meaning being unrestricted and covering all modes of entry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'I will not place my foot in this house' intending the literal meaning (just placing foot without entering), and then he lies down with his feet inside the house, according to the Hanafi ruling for oaths:
A) He breaks the oath because he placed his foot literally.
B) He does not break the oath because the literal meaning is abandoned and only the metaphorical (entering) is considered.
C) He breaks the oath only if he also entered.
D) He breaks the oath because he intended the literal meaning, which is valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a literal meaning is abandoned by custom, only the metaphorical meaning is operative in oaths; combining both is impossible.

The Principle of Sukna and Tamakkun in Oaths
Addressing objections about combining literal and metaphorical in oaths involving house residence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an oath concerning a house is interpreted based on residence (sukna), explaining how actual and estimative residence (tamakkun) avoids combining literal and metaphorical.

Core Idea
An oath on 'his house' refers to residence, not ownership — combining literal and metaphorical is impossible.

The text addresses a key objection to the Hanafi position that an oath regarding 'his house' is understood by residence (sukna), which includes owned, rented, and borrowed homes as metaphorical. The objection arises: what if the person owns an empty house? Does he still break the oath? The answer introduces the concept of tamakkun (capability of residence) to resolve this without combining literal and metaphorical meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The text states that an oath on 'his house' is understood by the metaphorical meaning of residence (sukna), covering owned, rented, and borrowed homes. The oath is broken by the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical.
This is the foundational rule being defended.
2
Recognize the objection
The objection from al-Fatawa: if the house is owned but empty of habitation, the metaphor of residence does not apply, yet the oath is still broken. This seems to imply a forbidden combination.
The objector claims that in this case the literal meaning (ownership) is used alongside the metaphorical (residence) to break the oath.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The answer introduces the concept of tamakkun: residence is more general, including actual residence (living there) and estimative residence (capability of living there). The owner of an empty house has estimative residence.
Thus, the oath is broken by the metaphorical generality alone—no combination.
4
Apply tamakkun
Estimation (taqdir) means the person could live there if they wished. This includes owned houses not rented out, but excludes houses that are rented or borrowed but not lived in (only actual residence applies in those cases).
Full tamakkun is present for an owner; partial for a renter if not inhabited.
5
Conclude the usul rule
The principle is that in oaths, words are interpreted according to urf (custom), and urf considers 'house' as the place of residence. To avoid combining, the Hanafi school extends residence to estimative capability.
This expansion is justified by the general nature of 'sukna' and the need to preserve the oath's effect.

(السنبلي) أنه ذكر في "الفتاوى": أي فتاوى فخر الدين قاضي خان وفيه خلاف للسرخسي . له فإنه عنده يتبادر الاختصاص بالسكني، سواء كان ملكا أم لا، بقرينة الهجران فلا يحنث بالدخول في دار مملوكة غير مسكونة فتدبر . (السنبلي) عاطلة في "منتهى الأرب" يستعمل العطل في الخلو عن الشيء، وإن كان أصله في الخلو عن الحلي. (القمر) أو تقديرًا بأن يتمكن من السكنى تمكنا تاما بخلاف ما إذا استأجر الدار أو استعارها و لم يسكنها فلا يحنث الحالف بالدخول فيها؛ لأن التمكن ههنا ضروري بضرورة العقد وليس تامًا كذا قيل. (القمر)
Statement of the third division: Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning in the house of 'so-and-so' in the residence of so-and-so, and it is a metaphorical meaning inclusive of ownership, lease, and loan, so he breaks his oath by the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. But it is objected to by what is mentioned in al-Fatawa: that if that house is not a residence for so-and-so but rather an empty property without habitation, he also breaks the oath. Unless it is said: that residence is more general, either actual or estimative. And he only breaks the oath if he comes at night or day in the statement: 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives' as an answer to another question: if someone swears saying 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives', then 'day' is literal for daylight and metaphorical for night. And you combined them and said that if so-and-so arrives at night or day, the slave is freed. He answered that he only breaks the oath in this example by coming at night or day because the intended meaning of 'day' is 'time', which is general. That is, 'time' meaning of the property in the house so-and-so, etc. This is an answer to another objection: that if he swears 'I will not enter his house', he breaks the oath by entering a rented residence, even though the attribution is literal for ownership, so the rented house is his house metaphorically. And he also breaks the oath by an inhabited owned house, which is his house literally, thus necessitating combining literal and metaphorical, which you prohibited. The summary of the answer is that the attribution is for absolute specificity, either literally or metaphorically, by indication of contextual evidence that a man only abandons the house due to aversion to the owner, and specificity is general and includes residence. So then it includes the inhabited owned and others by way of literalness and generality of metaphor, so no combination. And if absolute specificity is intended, then he breaks the oath by his owned but uninhabited house—i.e., by entering it—because he also has specificity to it. And by this appears the answer to what the commentator mentioned after that concerning the statement of Qadi Khan regarding the answer to the objection. (al-Sunbuli) It is mentioned in al-Fatawa: i.e., Fatawa Fakhr al-Din Qadi Khan, and in it is a dispute with al-Sarakhsi: because according to him, specificity to residence is immediately understood, whether ownership or not, due to contextual evidence of abandonment, so he does not break the oath by entering an owned uninhabited house, so reflect. (al-Sunbuli) 'empty' in Muntaha al-Arab: 'utlah' is used for emptiness of something, though originally it was for emptiness of jewelry. (al-Qamar) 'he also breaks the oath' i.e., by entering it, and this according to Qadi Khan. But according to Shams al-A'imma, he does not break the oath due to the severance of the relation of residence. (al-Qamar) 'or estimative' such that he is fully capable of residence, unlike if he rents or borrows the house and does not live in it, then the swearer does not break the oath by entering it, because the capability here is necessary by the contract and not complete. Thus it is said. (al-Qamar) 'And he only breaks the oath' etc.: Know that the oath legally is a strong contract by which the swearer resolves to do or not do, so it includes conditionality, which is tying the occurrence of the content of one clause to the occurrence of the content of another clause, for it is an oath legally, and breaking it is the occurrence of what is conditioned.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion on the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meanings by addressing several objections. The first example: if one swears 'I will not enter his house' where 'his house' is understood by residence (sukna), metaphorical for rented or borrowed homes. The objection arises if the house is owned but empty: according to Fatawa Qadi Khan, the oath is still broken because residence can be actual or estimative (the owner could live there if he wanted). The second example: an oath using 'day' for the arrival of a person; 'day' is literal for daylight but metaphorical for night. The objection is how can the oath be broken both day and night without combining literal and metaphorical? The answer: 'day' here means 'time' (al-waqt), which is general and covers both day and night, so only the metaphorical generality is applied. The third example returns to the oath 'I will not enter his house' with respect to a rented residence. The objection is that 'his house' is literal for owned property, but the oath also covers rented property (metaphorical) and owned inhabited property (literal), seemingly combining. The answer is that the attribution (idafa) indicates absolute specificity (ikhtisas), not just ownership. The contextual evidence (the dislike of the owner) shows the intended meaning is the house of the disliked person, which covers both owned and rented by way of literalness (for owned) and generality of metaphor (for rented). Therefore no combination. The passage ends with a general note on the definition of an oath in Islamic law as a binding commitment that includes conditional statements.

Key Terms
سكنى (sukna): Residence or dwelling, used as the basis for the metaphorical meaning of 'house' in the oath, including owned, rented, or borrowed.
اختصاص (ikhtisas): Specificity or relation, here meaning the special connection a person has to a house, whether by ownership or residence.
تمكن من السكنى (tamakkun min al-sukna): Capability of residence, either actual (by living in the house) or estimative (by having the right to live there, such as an owner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Literal ownership of the house.
B) The metaphorical meaning of residence extended by estimative capability.
C) Combining literal and metaphorical meanings.
D) The intention of the swearer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school avoids combining literal and metaphorical in oaths by interpreting 'house' as residence, which includes both actual and estimative residence.

Attribution (Idafa) for Absolute Specificity (Ikhtisas) in Oaths
Addressing objections about combining literal and metaphorical in oaths involving house and day, using idafa lil-ikhtisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that attribution (idafa) indicates absolute specificity (ikhtisas), allowing an oath on 'his house' to cover both owned and rented houses without combining literal and metaphorical.

Core Idea
Attribution in oaths indicates absolute specificity to the person, not just ownership—resolving the literal-metaphorical tension.

The text presents a further objection: in the oath 'I will not enter his house', the word 'his' is literal for ownership, yet the Hanafi school also applies it to rented houses (metaphorical), seemingly combining. The answer introduces the concept of idafa lil-ikhtisas (attribution for absolute specificity), supported by contextual evidence (hajr). This allows covering both owned inhabited houses (literal) and rented ones (metaphorical) without combination. The lesson also addresses a parallel example involving the word 'day' and includes an intra-madhhab dispute between Qadi Khan and al-Sarakhsi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second objection
The objection from the previous lesson is extended: in the oath 'I will not enter his house', 'his' is literally for ownership, but the Hanafi school also applies it to rented houses (metaphorical). This seems to combine literal (owned inhabited) and metaphorical (rented) in one usage.
The objector claims that for an owned inhabited house, the literal meaning applies; for a rented house, the metaphorical meaning applies, so using both in one oath is a forbidden combination.
2
Understand the Hanafi response: idafa lil-ikhtisas
The answer is that idafa (attribution) is not solely for ownership; it indicates absolute specificity (ikhtisas mutlaq). This includes both ownership and residence. Contextual evidence (the swearer's hatred, leading to abandoning the house) shows the intended meaning is the house the person has a connection to, not just owned.
Thus, when the oath covers an owned inhabited house, it is by literal ikhtisas; when it covers a rented house, it is by metaphorical ikhtisas. But since both are within the same meaning of specificity, there is no combination.
3
Apply to the parallel example of 'day'
The text also addresses an oath using 'day' for a person's arrival. 'Day' is literal for daylight and metaphorical for night. The objection is how the oath can be broken both day and night without combining. The answer: 'day' here means 'time' (al-waqt), which is general. So only the metaphorical generality applies.
This reinforces the principle: where a word has a general meaning covering multiple senses, the general meaning is used, not a combination.
4
Note the intra-madhhab dispute
The passage mentions that according to Qadi Khan, entering an owned empty house breaks the oath due to absolute specificity. But according to Shams al-A'imma (al-Sarakhsi), the oath is not broken because the residence relation is severed (hajr). This highlights a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
The lesson of this dispute: the mufta bihi position may vary; the student should be aware of both views and their bases.
5
Synthesize the usul rule
The usul principle derived is: idafa fi al-ayman tuhtamilu lil-ikhtisas al-mutlaq, wa al-tarjih bi-al-qarina. When contextual evidence (like hatred) is present, attribution shifts from literal ownership to absolute specificity, covering both owned and non-owned connections.
This avoids combining by making the meaning general rather than having two separate senses.

بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث بيان استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز في دار "فلان" في سكنى فلان، وهو معنى مجازي شامل للملك والإجارة والعارية فيحنث بعموم المجاز لا بالجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز لكن يرد عليه أنه ذكر في "الفتاوى": أنه إن لم تكن تلك الدار سكنى لفلان بل كانت ملكًا عاطلةً عن السكونة يحنث أيضًا إلا أن يقال: إن السكنى أعم من أن يكون تحقيقا أو تقديرًا. وإنما يحنث إذا قدم ليلاً أو نهارًا في قوله : عبده حر يوم يقدم فلان جواب سؤال آخر تقريره: أنه إذا حلف أحد فقال: عبدي حر يوم يقدم فلان، فاليوم حقيقة في النهار، ومجاز في الليل. وأنتم جمعتم بينهما وقلتم بأنه إن قدم فلان ليلاً أو نهارًا يعتق العبد، فأجاب بأنه إنما يحنث في هذا المثال بالقدوم ليلاً أو نهاراً؛ لأن المراد باليوم الوقت وهو عام أي الوقت معنى الملك في دار فلان إلخ هذا جواب نقض آخر تقريره أنه لو حلف "لا يدخل داره" يحنث بدخول دار سكناه إجارة مع أن الإضافة حقيقة في الملك، فدار السكنى داره مجازًا، ويحنث أيضًا بدار مسكونة مملوكة له، وهو داره حقيقة فيلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، وأنكم منعتموه وحاصل الجواب أن الإضافة للاختصاص المطلق إما حقيقة أو مجازا بدلالة القرينة هي أن الرجل لا يهجر الدار إلا للنفرة عن المالك، والاختصاص يعم والسكنى، فحينئذٍ تتناول المسكونة المملوكة وغيرها بطريق الحقيقة وعموم المجاز فلا جمع، وإذا أريد مطلق الاختصاص فيحنث بمملوكه غير مسكونة أي بدخوله فيها؛ لأن له أيضًا اختصاصا به وبهذا يظهر جواب ما أورد الشارح بعد ذلك بقول قاضي خان على جواب النقض. (السنبلي) أنه ذكر في "الفتاوى": أي فتاوى فخر الدين قاضي خان وفيه خلاف للسرخسي . له فإنه عنده يتبادر الاختصاص بالسكني، سواء كان ملكا أم لا، بقرينة الهجران فلا يحنث بالدخول في دار مملوكة غير مسكونة فتدبر . (السنبلي) عاطلة في "منتهى الأرب" يستعمل العطل في الخلو عن الشيء، وإن كان أصله في الخلو عن الحلي. (القمر) يحنث أيضا أي بالدخول فيها، وهذا عند قاضي خان، وأما عند شمس الأئمة، فلا يحنث لانقطاع نسبة السكني. (القمر) أو تقديرًا بأن يتمكن من السكنى تمكنا تاما بخلاف ما إذا استأجر الدار أو استعارها و لم يسكنها فلا يحنث الحالف بالدخول فيها؛ لأن التمكن ههنا ضروري بضرورة العقد وليس تامًا كذا قيل. (القمر) وإنما يحنث إلخ: اعلم أن اليمين شرعًا عبارة عن عقد قوي به عزم الحالف على الفعل أو الترك، فدخل فيه التعليق، وهو ربط حصول مضمون جملة بحصول مضمون جملة أخرى، فإنه يمين شرعًا، والحنث فيه هو وقوع ما علق. (القمر)
Statement of the third division: Statement of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meaning in the house of 'so-and-so' in the residence of so-and-so, and it is a metaphorical meaning inclusive of ownership, lease, and loan, so he breaks his oath by the generality of the metaphor, not by combining literal and metaphorical. But it is objected to by what is mentioned in al-Fatawa: that if that house is not a residence for so-and-so but rather an empty property without habitation, he also breaks the oath. Unless it is said: that residence is more general, either actual or estimative. And he only breaks the oath if he comes at night or day in the statement: 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives' as an answer to another question: if someone swears saying 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives', then 'day' is literal for daylight and metaphorical for night. And you combined them and said that if so-and-so arrives at night or day, the slave is freed. He answered that he only breaks the oath in this example by coming at night or day because the intended meaning of 'day' is 'time', which is general. That is, 'time' meaning of the property in the house so-and-so, etc. This is an answer to another objection: that if he swears 'I will not enter his house', he breaks the oath by entering a rented residence, even though the attribution is literal for ownership, so the rented house is his house metaphorically. And he also breaks the oath by an inhabited owned house, which is his house literally, thus necessitating combining literal and metaphorical, which you prohibited. The summary of the answer is that the attribution is for absolute specificity, either literally or metaphorically, by indication of contextual evidence that a man only abandons the house due to aversion to the owner, and specificity is general and includes residence. So then it includes the inhabited owned and others by way of literalness and generality of metaphor, so no combination. And if absolute specificity is intended, then he breaks the oath by his owned but uninhabited house—i.e., by entering it—because he also has specificity to it. And by this appears the answer to what the commentator mentioned after that concerning the statement of Qadi Khan regarding the answer to the objection. (al-Sunbuli) It is mentioned in al-Fatawa: i.e., Fatawa Fakhr al-Din Qadi Khan, and in it is a dispute with al-Sarakhsi: because according to him, specificity to residence is immediately understood, whether ownership or not, due to contextual evidence of abandonment, so he does not break the oath by entering an owned uninhabited house, so reflect. (al-Sunbuli) 'empty' in Muntaha al-Arab: 'utlah' is used for emptiness of something, though originally it was for emptiness of jewelry. (al-Qamar) 'he also breaks the oath' i.e., by entering it, and this according to Qadi Khan. But according to Shams al-A'imma, he does not break the oath due to the severance of the relation of residence. (al-Qamar) 'or estimative' such that he is fully capable of residence, unlike if he rents or borrows the house and does not live in it, then the swearer does not break the oath by entering it, because the capability here is necessary by the contract and not complete. Thus it is said. (al-Qamar) 'And he only breaks the oath' etc.: Know that the oath legally is a strong contract by which the swearer resolves to do or not do, so it includes conditionality, which is tying the occurrence of the content of one clause to the occurrence of the content of another clause, for it is an oath legally, and breaking it is the occurrence of what is conditioned.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion on the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical meanings by addressing several objections. The first example: if one swears 'I will not enter his house' where 'his house' is understood by residence (sukna), metaphorical for rented or borrowed homes. The objection arises if the house is owned but empty: according to Fatawa Qadi Khan, the oath is still broken because residence can be actual or estimative (the owner could live there if he wanted). The second example: an oath using 'day' for the arrival of a person; 'day' is literal for daylight but metaphorical for night. The objection is how can the oath be broken both day and night without combining literal and metaphorical? The answer: 'day' here means 'time' (al-waqt), which is general and covers both day and night, so only the metaphorical generality is applied. The third example returns to the oath 'I will not enter his house' with respect to a rented residence. The objection is that 'his house' is literal for owned property, but the oath also covers rented property (metaphorical) and owned inhabited property (literal), seemingly combining. The answer is that the attribution (idafa) indicates absolute specificity (ikhtisas), not just ownership. The contextual evidence (the dislike of the owner) shows the intended meaning is the house of the disliked person, which covers both owned and rented by way of literalness (for owned) and generality of metaphor (for rented). Therefore no combination. The passage ends with a general note on the definition of an oath in Islamic law as a binding commitment that includes conditional statements.

Key Terms
اختصاص (ikhtisas): Specificity or relation, here meaning the special connection a person has to a house, whether by ownership or residence.
قرينة الهجران (qarīnat al-hajrān): Contextual evidence of abandonment, indicating that the swearer's anger leads to avoiding the person's residence, thus the oath focuses on the place of dwelling.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath in Islamic law: a strong contract (عقد قوي) that binds the swearer to act or refrain, including conditional statements (ta'liq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'I will not enter his house', what does the Hanafi school say about the attribution (idafa) to avoid combining literal and metaphorical meanings?
A) The attribution is strictly literal for ownership.
B) The attribution is for absolute specificity, supported by contextual evidence.
C) The attribution is metaphorical in all cases.
D) The attribution is ignored in oaths.

Key Takeaway
By using 'idafa lil-ikhtisas', the Hanafi school covers both owned and rented houses under one general meaning, eliminating the need to combine literal and metaphorical.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz in Oaths
Sharh explaining the rule against combining haqiqah and majaz in oaths, with objections and resolutions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined in a single context, and determine the meaning of 'day' in oaths based on whether the action is extended or non-extended.

Core Idea
In oaths, a word can only be literal or figurative, not both; context and action type decide.

Scholars of usul al-fiqh agree that a single word cannot simultaneously carry its literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings in one context. This principle has direct implications for oath formulations. The passage before us examines two objections to this rule and their resolutions, culminating in a practical rule for interpreting the word 'day' (yawm) in suspended oaths. We will unpack the reasoning used by Hanafi jurists to maintain consistency in oath enforcement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
State the rule: a word cannot simultaneously be intended in both its literal and figurative meanings in a single utterance. Recognise that this prevents arbitrary or combined interpretations in oaths.
This is foundational to Hanafi linguistic theory.
2
Analyse the first objection (idafa and dwelling)
Consider the objection: If someone swears not to enter 'his house' (daruhu), the idafa literally means ownership, but figuratively covers rented dwellings. The objection claims that holding the oath violated by entering a rented dwelling would combine literal and figurative. Follow the resolution: idafa denotes absolute ikhtisas (specific relationship), which can be literal (ownership) or figurative (dwelling) depending on context. The context of 'leaving' the house indicates dwelling, so the figurative meaning applies generally without combining.
Key term: ikhtisas – specific relationship qualifying both ownership and occupancy.
3
Analyse the second objection (day and arrival)
Consider the oath 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives'. 'Day' literally means daytime, figuratively absolute time. The objection claims that ruling the oath violated by a night arrival would combine literal and figurative. Follow the resolution: for non-extended actions like arrival, 'day' cannot literally mean daytime because arrival does not occupy the whole day. Therefore, the figurative meaning (absolute time) is intended. Hence, violation occurs at night or day via the generality of the figurative meaning.
No combination; only one meaning is operative.
4
Apply the rule for extended vs. non-extended actions
Extract the general rule: if the action is extended (e.g., riding), 'day' retains its literal meaning (daytime) because it can encompass the action. If the action is non-extended (e.g., arrival), 'day' takes the figurative meaning of absolute time. Practice applying this rule to new examples.
The operative factor is whether the action can be measured by the daytime.
5
Note the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement between Qadi Khan and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi: Qadi Khan holds that ikhtisas covers any property the swearer has a relationship to, so entering an unoccupied owned house violates. Al-Sarakhsi restricts ikhtisas to actual dwelling due to the context of leaving, so entering an unoccupied owned house does not violate. Recognise the reliance on context (qarīna) to prioritise a specific meaning.
This shows how context can narrow the range of ikhtisas.
